# 山海经

**The Classic of Mountains and Seas**

**Title:** *The Classic of Mountains and Seas* (*Shanhai Jing*). Eighteen scrolls. Base text sourced from the *Zhengtong Daoist Canon*, *Dongshen* section, *Benwen* category. Collated with: Hao Yixing’s *Annotations and Commentaries on the Classic of Mountains and Seas*, Dong Ke’s *Collated and Translated Classic of Mountains and Seas*, and Tan Chenggeng’s *Punctuated and Collated Classic of Mountains and Seas*.

**Preface to the Classic of Mountains and Seas**

Those who peruse the *Classic of Mountains and Seas* invariably regard its vast and fantastical claims—its abundance of strange and wondrous, extraordinary and marvelous accounts—with skepticism. I have ventured to discuss this, saying: As Zhuangzi once observed, "What humans know is nothing compared to what they do not know." This truth is manifest in the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*.

Consider the boundless expanse of the cosmos, the teeming multitude of living beings, the interplay of yin and yang, the myriad distinctions among the ten thousand things—where qi intermingles in chaos, pressing and blending into one another, where wandering spirits and numinous anomalies take form upon encountering images, shaping themselves into mountains and rivers, adorning themselves upon trees and stones—how could such wonders ever be fully recounted?

Thus, all that seems discordant converges into a single resonance; all that appears chaotic coalesces into a single image. The world deems certain things "strange," yet knows not why they are strange; it deems others "not strange," yet knows not why they are not. Why is this so? Things do not inherently possess strangeness; they become strange only in relation to us. The strangeness, then, lies within us, not within the things themselves.

Thus, the Hu people, upon seeing cloth, mistake it for felt; the Yue people, upon seeing fine wool, are startled by its softness. Familiarity breeds complacency toward the commonplace, while rarity evokes wonder at the unfamiliar—this is the perennial blindness of human nature.

Let me offer a few examples to illustrate: fire emerges from ice and water, while yin-aligned mice are born in scorching mountains. Those who discuss such matters find nothing strange in them, yet when they encounter the accounts in the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*, they deem them bizarre. This is to ignore what should be deemed strange and to marvel at what should not. To ignore what should be strange is to verge on seeing nothing as strange at all; to marvel at what should not be strange is to have never truly encountered the marvelous.

If one can discern what should not be so and what cannot be otherwise, then principle itself admits of no exceptions.

Examining the *Bamboo Annals of Ji Commandery* and the *Biography of King Mu*: King Mu, on his western expedition, met the Queen Mother of the West, who presented him with jade and silk as tokens of goodwill, along with brocades and woven ornaments. King Mu feasted with the Queen Mother upon the Jade Pond, exchanging poems of refined elegance. He then ascended Kunlun’s peaks, toured the Palace of the Yellow Emperor, gazed upon the ridges of Mount Zhong, and beheld the treasures of the divine rulers. He carved an inscription upon the Queen Mother’s mountain and left his mark upon the gardens of the Dark Grove. He gathered rare woods, resplendent herbs, exotic birds, and strange beasts, along with jade, precious stones, vessels of luminous beauty, golden unguents, and silver that shone like torches, bringing them back to cultivate and rear within the Central Kingdom.

King Mu rode a chariot drawn by eight steeds, with Daoli on the right and Lu’er on the left. Zaofu served as his charioteer, and Benrong as his attendant on the right. They traversed tens of thousands of miles, journeying through the renowned mountains and great rivers of the four directions, scaling and crossing them all. To the east, he ascended the halls of the Giants; to the west, he feasted in the abode of the Queen Mother; to the south, he crossed the bridge of giant turtles and alligators; to the north, he trod the thoroughfare of accumulated feathers. Only after exhausting all pleasures and delights did he return.

According to the *Records of the Grand Historian*, King Mu obtained the steeds Daoli, Lu’er, and Hualiu, and had Zaofu drive them on his western tour, where he met the Queen Mother of the West and reveled so joyously that he forgot to return. This account aligns with the *Bamboo Annals*.

The *Zuozhuan* states: "King Mu wished to indulge his desires, seeking to leave his chariot tracks and horse hoofprints across the world." The *Bamboo Annals* records precisely this.

Yet scholars like Qiao Zhou, though erudite and refined, dismissed these verified historical accounts as fabrications. Sima Qian, in his "Account of Dayuan," also remarked: "Since Zhang Qian’s mission to Daxia and his exploration of the Yellow River’s source, who has truly beheld the fabled Kunlun?" As for the strange creatures recorded in the *Basic Annals* and the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*, I dare not speak of them—is this not lamentable?

Had the *Bamboo Annals* not resurfaced after a thousand years to serve as evidence today, the words of the *Classic of Mountains and Seas* would have nearly been discarded. Consider further: Dongfang Shuo knew the name of the *Bifang* bird; Liu Xin distinguished the corpse of the *Daoxie*; Wang Qi sought the two-faced visitor; and the people of the distant seas obtained the garment of the long-armed being. These are precise verifications, rare confirmations spanning ages.

Alas! Might those who remain deluded awaken, even slightly?

Thus, the sagely emperors traced transformations to their utmost variations, embodying things to respond to anomalies, perceiving without obstruction, and exhaustively exploring the hidden and subtle. How profound! How mysterious!

This book spans seven dynasties and three thousand years. Though it briefly flourished during the Han, it soon fell into obscurity. The names and locations of its mountains and rivers often contain errors, differing from present-day records. With no transmitted teachings from masters, it nearly vanished. That which the Dao preserves, the world abandons—how tragic!

I fear this loss, and so I have undertaken to transmit it anew, clearing its obstructions, dispelling its overgrowth, elucidating its profound subtleties, and marking its depths and shallows. May this ensure that its neglected texts do not perish from the world, its wondrous words do not fade from the present, the traces of the Xia sovereigns are not erased in times to come, and the affairs of the eight wilds remain known to future generations. Would this not be worthwhile?

The flight of a mayfly cannot compare to the soaring of a celestial roc; the swimming of minnows in a puddle cannot fathom the ascent of a crimson dragon. The courts of heaven’s music are not trodden by mere musicians; the fordless abyss is not crossed by the rhinoceros or the buffalo. Without the utmost penetration of the world, one cannot discourse on the meaning of the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*.

Alas! May those of broad vision and vast learning take heed.

The main texts of *Within the Seas* and *The Great Wilderness* were originally appended externally.

The Attending Secretary, Chariot and Cavalry Commandant, and Palace Attendant, the Illustrious Grand Master, your servant Xiu, oversaw the collation of the secret archives and states: The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* collated by Minister Wang of the Secretariat and Chamberlain for Ceremonials originally comprised thirty-two scrolls, now consolidated into eighteen.

The established *Classic of Mountains and Seas* emerged during the era of Yao and Shun. In former times, the vast floods overflowed, inundating the Central Kingdom. The people lost their footholds, clambering upon hills and nesting in trees. When Gun’s efforts proved futile, Emperor Yao commanded Yu to succeed him. Yu rode the four conveyances, following the mountains to fell trees, determining the heights of mountains and the courses of great rivers. Together with Boyi, he drove forth the birds and beasts, named the mountains and rivers, classified the grasses and trees, and distinguished the waters and soils. The Four Peaks assisted him, surveying the four directions, reaching where human traces seldom ventured and carriages and boats rarely arrived. Within, he demarcated the mountains of the five regions; without, he divided the seas of the eight directions. He recorded their treasures and marvels, the waters, soils, grasses, trees, birds, beasts, and insects native to distant lands, the abodes of qilin and phoenix, the concealments of auspicious omens, and the foreign states beyond the four seas, the strange peoples of remote domains. Yu divided the Nine Provinces, apportioned tribute according to the land, and Yi and others categorized the good and ill of creatures, compiling the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*. These are all the legacy of the sagely and wise, the luminous records of antiquity. Their accounts are plain, clear, and trustworthy.

During the reign of Emperor Wu of Han, an exotic bird was once presented. When it was fed, no other creature would eat the same food. Dongfang Shuo saw it and named the bird, further stating what it should eat—just as he had said. When asked how he knew, he replied that it was recorded in the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*. During the reign of Emperor Xuan of Han, a stone was struck in Shang Commandery, collapsing to reveal a stone chamber. Within was a man bound in reverse with shackles. At the time, your servant Xiu’s father, Xiang, served as Remonstrance Grandee. He identified the man as the minister of Erfu. When asked how he knew, he too cited the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*, which states: “Erfu killed Yayu. The Emperor then bound him to a mountain of distant kinship, fettering his right foot and binding his hands behind his back.” The Emperor was greatly astonished. From then on, many at court marveled at the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*. Scholars of literature and great Confucians all studied it, deeming it extraordinary. It can be used to examine auspicious omens and strange transformations, to witness the customs and songs of distant lands and foreign peoples. Thus, the *Book of Changes* says: “To speak of the most recondite matters in the world without confusion—such is the breadth of the gentleman of wide learning.” Your servant Xiu, risking death, respectfully presents this.

**Scroll One of the Classic of Mountains and Seas**

**Account of Guo Pu**

**The Southern Mountains Classic**

The first of the *Southern Mountains Classic* is called Shei Mountain.

Its foremost peak is known as Mount Zhaoyao, which overlooks the Western Sea. It lies in Fushan of Shu, where the western end of the southern mountains borders the Western Sea. The mountain abounds with cassia trees, their leaves resembling those of the loquat, over two *chi* in length and several *cun* in width, with a pungent taste. Their white blossoms cluster upon the mountain peaks, remaining verdant through winter and summer, with no other trees interspersed. As the *Master Lü’s Spring and Autumn Annals* states: “The cassia of Mount Zhaoyao.” The mountain also yields much gold and jade.

There is a kind of herb here, resembling leeks—*Pu notes: “Leeks,” pronounced *jiu*. The *Approaching Elegance* also records their abundance in the mountains of Shu.*—yet bearing blue-green flowers. It is called *zhuyu* (or sometimes *guicha*). Those who consume it will know no hunger.

There is also a tree whose form resembles the paper mulberry (*Pu notes: The paper mulberry is also called *gou*; it is named *gu* because its fruit resembles grain.*) but with black grain. Its blossoms radiate light in all four directions—*Pu notes: This refers to luminous brilliance. The *Songs of Chu* speaks of the “crimson blossoms of the Ruo Tree, whose light illumines the earth,” which is of the same kind.*—and it is named *migu*. Those who carry it will never lose their way.

There is an animal here resembling a *yu*—*Pu notes: The *yu* resembles a macaque but is larger, with red eyes and a long tail, and is commonly found in the mountains south of the Yangtze. Some accounts fail to clarify this creature. It is called *yu*, written with the character for “ox,” and depicted in illustrations as ox-like (or sometimes as a monkey), though both are incorrect.*—but with white ears. It moves swiftly on all fours and is called the *xingxing*. Those who eat it will gain fleetness of foot. The *shengsheng* (*Pu notes: A kind of *yu* beast resembling an ape, moving on all fours with its limbs intertwined, belongs to the same category.*) is recorded in Jing Fang’s *Book of Changes*.

The Lili River—*the character is pronounced *ji*—flows from this mountain, coursing westward into the sea. Its waters abound with *yupei* (details unknown). Those who carry it will be free from *hen* diseases—*Pu notes: *Hen* refers to parasitic ailments.*

Three hundred *li* farther east lies Mount Tang (or sometimes written as *Chang*).

Mount Ting is rich in *yan* trees—*yan* is also called *lian*; its fruit resembles the *su* but is red and edible. (*Pronounced* yǎn.) The mountain abounds with white apes. Modern apes resemble macaques but have longer arms and legs, are agile and swift, and come in black and yellow hues. Their cries are mournful. It also yields *water jade*, which is today’s crystal. As the *Shanglin Fu* states: “Water jade gleams in heaped abundance.” This was what Master Red Pine consumed, as recorded in the *Lives of the Immortals*. The mountain is also rich in gold.

Three hundred and eighty *li* farther east lies Mount Yuanyi. It teems with strange beasts and its waters with strange fish. The term “strange” refers to creatures of grotesque and unusual form. As *Master Shi* notes: “King Xu of Yan loved the strange. He plunged into deep waters to obtain strange fish and ventured into deep mountains to capture strange beasts, many of which he displayed in his court.” The mountain also yields white jade and harbors *belly worms*—*fu* worms with patterns like embroidered ribbons, a needle-like proboscis, and specimens weighing over a hundred *jin*; another name is *fanbi*. (*Fu* is the ancient form of *hui*.) It abounds with strange serpents and strange trees, making ascent perilous.

Two hundred and seventy *li* farther east lies Mount Niuyang. (*Pronounced* niǔ.) Its southern slopes yield abundant red gold—*copper*—while its northern slopes yield white gold—*silver*, as recorded in the *Erya*. (The southern side of a mountain is called *yang*; the northern side, *yin*.) There is a beast here whose form resembles a horse but with a white head, tiger-like markings, and a red tail. Its cry is like a chant, akin to human song. It is called the *lushu*, and wearing its pelt ensures descendants. (*Wearing* refers to bearing its skin or tail.) A strange river flows from here, coursing eastward into the Xianyi River. Its waters teem with dark turtles whose form resembles a tortoise but with a crow’s head and viper’s tail—the tail tapers sharply. It is called the *xuan* turtle, and its cry resembles the sound of splitting wood. Wearing it prevents deafness and can be used for *treatment*. (*Treatment* here means healing.)

The *Outer Transmission* states: "Illness cannot be treated"—*or* "healed"—*means the disease has abated.*

Three hundred *li* to the east lies Mount Di. Its waters are abundant, but no grasses or trees grow there. A fish dwells here, its form resembling an ox, dwelling on the hills. It has a serpent’s tail and wings, with feathers beneath its flanks. Its cry is like that of a plowing ox. *Master Zhuang* says: "The dog that herds the plow"—referring to this ox. The *Mu Tianzi Zhuan* records: "The Son of Heaven’s hounds seize tigers and leopards." It is called the *lu*, pronounced *liu*. It dies in winter and revives in summer, belonging to the hibernating kind. When it is said to "die," this means it lies dormant, senseless as the dead. Eating it prevents swelling ailments.

Four hundred *li* farther east stands Mount Danyuan, pronounced *chan*. Its waters are plentiful, but no grasses or trees grow, nor can one ascend it—so sheer and towering is it. A beast dwells here, its form like a wildcat but with a mane. It is called the *lei*, also written as *peimao* or *fa*. It is self-sufficient as both male and female; those who consume it are free from jealousy. *Master Zhuang* likewise says: "The *lei* transforms as both female and male." The *huan* pig of today does the same.

Three hundred *li* to the east lies Mount Ji. Its southern slopes yield much jade; its northern slopes, strange trees. A beast dwells here, its form like a sheep, with nine tails and four ears, its eyes set in its back. It is called the *boshe*, pronounced *bo* and *shi* (or *yi*). Wearing it dispels fear—all dread and terror.

There is a bird here, its form like a chicken but with three heads, six eyes, six feet, and three wings. Its name is pronounced *ji* and *xing*, also read as *chang* and *hu* in two other tones. Consuming it prevents sleep.

Three *li* further east lies Mount Qingqiu. There is also a Qingqiu Kingdom beyond the seas. The *Water Classic* states: this is the same as the "Qingqiu" mentioned in the *Rhapsody on the Imperial Park*, where "autumn hunts are held at Qingqiu." Its southern slopes yield much jade; its northern slopes, abundant dark *qing*. *Qing* belongs to the *you* category, pronounced *hu*. A beast dwells here, its form like a fox but with nine tails—this is the nine-tailed fox. Its cry resembles an infant’s and it devours humans. Eating it wards off *gu*; consuming its flesh protects one from encountering malevolent and demonic *qi*. Some say: *gu* refers to *gu* poison.

There is a bird here, its form like a turtledove, its cry like a scolding voice, akin to humans berating one another. It is called the *guan'guan*, also written as *guan*. Wearing it prevents confusion.

The Ying River flows forth from here, coursing southward into the Marsh of Jiyi. Within it abound red *ru*, pronounced *ru*. Their form is like fish but with a human face, and their cry resembles that of mandarin ducks. Eating them prevents scabies. Some texts write this as "illness."

Further east three hundred and fifty *li* stands Mount Jiwei. Its tail stretches into the Eastern Sea, abundant with sand and stone. *Cun* is an ancient form of *dūn*, meaning "to overlook the sea." Pronounced *cún*. The Fang River flows forth from here, pronounced *fāng*, coursing southward into the Xiao, pronounced *yù*. Within it abounds white jade.

From the first of the *Quxian* Mountains—beginning at Mount Zhaoyao and extending to Mount Jiwei—there are in all ten mountains spanning two thousand nine hundred and fifty *li*. Their spirits all bear avian bodies and draconic heads. The rites for their worship prescribe: *mao* refers to selecting a living sacrifice by its fur color. As the *Rites of Zhou* states: "For yang sacrifices, use the fur of intoxicated animals." One *zhang*-jade is buried, a half-*gui* serving as the *zhang*. *Yì* means "to bury." The sacrificial grain is *xǔ*-rice, the refined rice for offering to spirits. Pronounced *xiān lǚ* in ancient times; in the Jiangdong region, it is read as *suǒ*, also pronounced *xǔ*. *Xú* is glutinous rice, pronounced *tā dǔ*. *Xǔ* is sometimes written as *shū*, which is incorrect. One *bì*-jade, glutinous rice, and a mat of white *jiān* are used. *Jiān* is a kind of grass akin to reeds, pronounced *jiān*.

The first mountain of the *Nan Ci Er Jing* is called Mount Ju, pronounced *jǔ*. To its west lies Liuhuang; to its north, Zhupi; to its east, Changyou—all mountain names. The Ying River flows forth from here, coursing southwest into the Red Water. Within it abounds white jade. As the *Shizi* states: "Where water bends sharply, there is jade; where it curves smoothly, there are pearls." It also holds much cinnabar grain, fine cinnabar like millet.

There is a beast here, its form like a suckling pig but with spurs. Its cry resembles a dog’s bark, and it is called the *lili*. Where it appears, the county will see much earthwork. There is a bird here, its form like an owl but with human hands, its feet likewise like human hands. *Chī* is pronounced *chǔ zhī*. Its cry resembles *bì*, and its name is uncertain, called *yā*, pronounced *zhū*. It calls its own name, and where it appears, the county will see many exiled scholars. *Fàng* means "banishment," though some write it as *xiào*.

Four hundred and fifty *li* to the southeast stands Mount Changyou. Devoid of vegetation, it abounds in water. There is a beast here, its form like a *yu* but with four ears, named the *changyou*, after the mountain from which it emerges. Its cry resembles a moan, like a human’s groaning. Where it appears, the commandery and county will suffer great floods.

Further east three hundred and forty *li* stands Mount Yaoguang. Its southern slopes abound in jade; its northern slopes, in metal. There is a beast here, its form like a human but with a boar’s mane, dwelling in caves and hibernating in winter. It is called the *huáguǒ*, pronounced *huá huái*. Its cry resembles the chopping of wood, like the sound of a man hewing timber. Where it appears, the county will see great labor service—or some say, great disorder.

Further east three hundred and fifty *li* stands Mount Yu. In present-day Zhuqi County of Donghai, to the southwest, lies Mount Yu, the very place where Gun met his fate. Calculating the distances, however, they do not align, suggesting this may not be the case. Below it abounds water; above it, rain. Devoid of grass and trees, it teems with vipers.

Further east three hundred and seventy *li* stands Mount Qúfù. There is no grass or trees, but much metal and jade.

Further east four hundred *li* stands Mount Gōuxiàn. There is no grass or trees, but much metal and jade. Today, it lies south of Yuyao County and north of Gouzhang County in Kuaiji, whence these two counties derive their names, as recorded in Zhang’s *Treatise on Geography*.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Fúyù. To its north lies Jùqū—present-day Taihu Lake southwest of Wuxian, called Zhènzé in the *Book of Documents*. To its east lie the Zhūpí waters. There is a beast here, its form like a tiger but with an ox’s tail, its cry like a barking dog. It is called the *fù*, and it devours humans. The Tiáo River issues from its northern slopes, flowing northward into Jùqū. Its waters abound in *cǐ* fish, long and slender with elongated heads, measuring over a *chi* in the largest specimens. Today, they are plentiful in Taihu Lake. Also called the *dāo* fish, pronounced *chánqǐ*.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Chéng. Square in shape with three tiers, its form resembles stacked altars built by human hands. *Chéng* also means "double-eared." Above, it abounds in metal and jade; below, in dark *qīng* stone. The *Zhuō* River issues from it, flowing southward into Hūsháo. *Hū* is pronounced like *chē*; *sháo* is sometimes written with the water radical. Its waters abound in yellow gold. Today, in Yongchang Prefecture, gold like chaff is found in the sands. As the *Shīzi* states: "Clear waters yield yellow gold, Wang Ying."

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Kuaiji, square in shape. Today, it lies south of Shanyin County in Kuaiji Prefecture, atop which stands Yu’s tomb and well. Above, it abounds in metal and jade; below, in *fū* stone. *Fū* is pronounced *fù*; it resembles jade, now unearthed in Linxiang of Changsha. Its surface bears red earth and white veins, its hues blending like green onions, indistinct. The Sháo River issues from it, flowing southward into the Jú.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Yí. Devoid of grass and trees, it abounds in sand and stone. The Jú River—also written as *sǔn*—issues from it, flowing southward into Lietú.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Púgōu—also written as *Xī*. Above, it abounds in metal and jade; below, in grasses and trees. There are no birds or beasts, nor any water.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Xiányīn. Devoid of grass, trees, and water.

Further east four hundred *li* stands Mount Xún—also written as *Xún*. Its southern slopes abound in metal; its northern slopes, in jade. There is a beast here, its form like a sheep but without a mouth, impervious to harm. It sustains itself on qi alone. It is called the *□*.

The sound *huán* (or *huàn*). The Xún River issues from it, its sound *xún*, flowing southward into the Marsh of Guān, its sound *è*. Within it grows much *bìléi*.

Further east four hundred *li* stands Mount Mósháo. Above, it abounds in catalpa and nanmu—*nan*, a divine tree of the mountains, its form grand, its leaves resembling the mulberry. Now written as 楠 (*nán*), the *Erya* identifies it as *nán*. Below, it abounds in *kèqǐ*—*qǐ*, the matrimony vine, its berries red. The Pāng River issues from it, its sound like the *pāng* of *pāngtuó*, flowing eastward into the sea.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Qūwú. Devoid of grass and trees, it abounds in sand and stone. The Lù River issues from it, flowing southward into the Pāng River.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Lùwú. Above, it is devoid of grass and trees, but abounds in metal and stone. The Zégēng River issues from it, flowing southward into the Pāng River. Within its waters dwells a beast named *gǔdiāo*—*gǔ* also written as *zuǎn*. Its form resembles an eagle but with horns, its cry like that of an infant; it devours humans.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Qīwú. Devoid of grass and trees, it abounds in *bó* stones but no jade. These stones may serve as the base for the game of *bó*. Situated east of the sea, when gazing toward Qiū Mountain, its radiance emerges and recedes—where the divine light lies concealed and shines. This is the station of the sun, where the sun’s shadow converges.

In all, from the beginning of the "Southern Second Classic" at Mount Jù to Mount Qīwú, there are seventeen mountains spanning seven thousand two hundred *li*. Their spirits all bear dragon bodies and avian heads. For their sacrifices: the offering consists of one jade *bì* buried in the earth, and refined rice as the essence.

The "Southern Third Classic" begins with Mount Tiānyú. Below, it abounds in water, rendering ascent impossible.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Dǎoguò. Above, it abounds in metal and jade; below, in rhinoceroses and *sì*—the rhinoceros resembles a water buffalo, with a pig’s head, a broad mouth, feet like an elephant’s, three hooves, a large belly, and black hide. It bears three horns: one on its head, one on its brow, and one on its snout. The horn on its snout is small and does not fall; it is the horn it feeds upon. It delights in gnawing, its mouth ever frothing with bloody spittle. The *sì* also resembles a water buffalo, its hide blue, its two horns weighing three thousand *jīn*. There are many elephants—the largest of beasts, with long trunks. The largest tusks reach a *zhàng* in length. Jealous by nature, they do not tolerate the young of other males.

There is a bird here, its form like a swan-goose, resembling a wild duck but smaller, its feet near its tail—its sound like the *qiāo* of *qiāojiàn*. It has a white head, three feet (or hands), and a human face. Its name is *qúrú*, its sound *qú*. Its cry is its own name.

The Yín River issues from it, its sound *yín*, flowing southward into the sea. Within it dwells the *hǔjiāo*—a *jiāo* resembling a serpent with four feet, akin to a dragon. Its form is that of a fish’s body with a serpent’s tail, its cry like that of a mandarin duck. Those who consume it do not swell, and it may cure hemorrhoids.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Dānxué. Above, it abounds in metal and jade. The Dān River issues from it, flowing southward into the Bó Sea—the Bó Sea being the curved headland of the sea’s shore. There is a bird here, its form like a chicken, adorned with five-colored patterns. Its name is *fènghuáng*. The markings on its head signify virtue; on its wings, righteousness; on its back, propriety; on its breast, benevolence; and on its belly, trust. This bird drinks and eats naturally, singing and dancing of its own accord. When it appears, the world knows peace. In the Han era, the *fèng* bird emerged several times, standing five or six *chǐ* tall, its five colors resplendent. Zhuāngzǐ’s account of the *fèng*’s markings differs from this. The *Guǎngyǎ* states: the *fèng* has a chicken’s head, a goose’s throat, a serpent’s neck, a tortoise’s back, and a fish’s tail. The female is called *huáng*, the male *fèng*.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Fāshuǎng. Devoid of grass and trees, it abounds in water and white apes.

The Fàn waters issue forth here, flowing southward into the Bohai Sea.

Further east four hundred *li* lies the tail of Mount Máo. To its south is a valley called Yùyí (or written as *Suì*). It abounds in monstrous birds. The *Guǎngyǎ* states: "... *péng* #14, *yuánjū*, and sparrows all belong to the category of monstrous birds." The *kǎifēng* wind originates here. *Kǎifēng* is the south wind.

Further east four hundred *li* stands the summit of Mount Fēi. Its heights yield much gold and jade, but no water; its lower slopes teem with venomous vipers.

Further east five hundred *li* rises Mount Yángjiā. Devoid of grass and trees, it abounds in water.

Further east five hundred *li* stands Mount Guànxiāng. Its heights are thick with trees but lack grass, and it abounds in monstrous birds, though no beasts dwell there. (An alternate name is *□Húshè* Mountain.)

Further east five hundred *li* lies Mount Jī. Its heights yield much gold, and its lower slopes abound in cinnabar and *dànhuò* (a red pigment). *Qú* refers to the red variety. Some say *qú* is fine cinnabar, as recorded in the *Shàngshū*. It is pronounced like *huò* in *chǐhuò*.

The Hēi River issues forth here, flowing southward into the sea. Within it dwells the *tuán* fish, pronounced like *tuán* in *tuánshàn* (round fan). Its form resembles the *fù* fish but bears fur, and its cry is like that of a piglet. When it appears, the world suffers great drought.

Further east four hundred *li* stands Mount Lìngqiū. Devoid of grass and trees, it abounds in fire. To its south lies a valley called Zhōnggǔ, from which the *tiáofēng* wind originates. The *tiáofēng* is the northeast wind. The *Records* state: "When the *tiáofēng* arrives, release those lightly bound and urge the settlement of debts."

There is a bird here whose form resembles an owl, with a human face, four eyes, and ears. Its name is *Xiǎn*, pronounced like *yú*. Its cry is its own name, and when it appears, the world suffers great drought.

Further east three hundred and seventy *li* lies Mount Lúnzhě, pronounced like *lùn* in *lùnshuō* (discourse), or alternatively like *lún*. Its heights yield much gold and jade, and its lower slopes abound in green *□*.

There is a tree here whose form resembles the *gǔ* tree but with red grain. Its sap is like lacquer, and its taste like malt sugar. Those who eat it never hunger, and it can relieve fatigue. Its name is *bái□* (or written as *gāosū*). *Gāosū* is also called *bái□*, as recorded in the *Guǎngyǎ*, pronounced like *gāo*. It can be used to "blood" jade—meaning it can dye jade to enhance its luster.

Further east five hundred and eighty *li* stands Mount Yúgǎo. It abounds in monstrous beasts and giant serpents.

Further east five hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Nányú. Its heights yield much gold and jade, and its lower slopes abound in water. There is a cave here: in spring, water enters it; in summer, it emerges; and in winter, it seals shut. The Zuǒ River issues forth here, flowing southeast into the sea. The *fènghuáng* and *yuānchú* (also of the *fèng* kind) dwell here.

In summary, the beginning of the "Southern Mountains: Third Classic" spans from Mount Tiānwú to Mount Nányú, comprising fourteen mountains over a distance of six thousand five hundred and thirty *li*. The spirits of these mountains all have dragon bodies and human faces. Their sacrifices involve one white dog, and the offering is called *qí* (prayer). The ritual grain used is *xú*.

To the right is the record of the southern mountains, totaling forty mountains large and small, covering a distance of sixteen thousand three hundred and eighty *li*.

**Eulogies of the *Nánshānjīng* Illustrations**

**Cinnamon**
Cinnamon grows in the southern lands,
Its branches and blossoms cling to towering peaks.
Its broad leaves and radiant petals
Defy frost with their pointed tips.
Its *qi* reigns over all herbs,
Towering like clouds in its majesty.

**Mí Gorge**
Here stands a wondrous tree,
Born in the swaying boughs.
Its blossoms cast flowing light,
Illuminating the vault of heaven.
Worn as a pendant, it dispels confusion,
For it bears a hidden numinous mark.

**Shēngshēng (Prior to "Life")**
The *shēngshēng* resembles a monkey,
Walking upright or crouching.
It gnaws on wood to strengthen its might,
Eating a little *xīn* to brighten its eyes.
Swift as *fēilián*,
How could it be mere flesh for food?

**Water Jade**
Bathed in water jade,
It gleams in hidden abysses.
Chisongzi wears it,
His spirit shedding its form to ride the mist.
Inhaling and exhaling the six *qi*,
He ascends and descends the nine heavens.

**White Ape**
The white ape displays its skill,
Like Youji drawing his bow.
Responding to a sigh, it howls—
Its spirit strikes first.
Its numbers follow in cycles,
Its wonders boundless.

**Lùshǔ**
The beast *lùshǔ*,
Horse in substance, tiger in pattern.
Tossing its head, it whinnies,
Its hooves leaping above the herd.
Wear its pelt and fur,
And your descendants will flourish like clouds.

**Lù**
O fish, the *lù*!
It dwells not in water.
Its form is like an ox’s,
With raven wings and a serpent’s tail.
It appears and vanishes with the times,
Relying on life and death.

**Lèi**
The beast *lèi*
Combines two forms in one.
Drawing near, it takes from the body,
No need for external tools.
Wear it as an ornament,
And it knows no jealousy.

**Bóshī**
The *bótuō* resembles a sheep,
Its eyes reversed upon its back.
Gaze upon it, and it seems strange,
Yet push it, and it is no marvel.
If you wish to feel no fear,
Wear its pelt.

**Zhùtú Grass, Xuán Tortoise, □□ Fish**
The fine grass *zhùtú*,
Eating it banishes hunger.
Tortoise with a crow’s head and viper’s tail,
Its name is *xuán*.
The *□□* has six legs,
Three wings, all splendid.

**Guànguàn Bird, Chìrú**
Its cry is like a shout,
Its form like a turtledove.
Wear it to discern confusion,
Born of the Blue Hills.
The creature *chìrú*,
Fish in body, human in head.

**Zhū Bird**
A comet streaks across the sky,
A whale dies in the waves.
The *zhū* bird cries in the city,
And worthy scholars are exiled.
Its principle is most subtle,
Words cannot describe it.

**Huáguǒ**
When the beast *huáguǒ* appears,
Corvée labor arises.
It emerges with governance,
And departs only when chaos reigns.
When the world is well-ordered,
Its hidden form vanishes.

**Chángyòu, Zhì**
The *chángyòu* has four ears,
Its form like a monkey’s.
It is truly an omen of water,
And when it appears, floods surge.
The *zhì* has a tiger’s body,
Its tail like an ox’s.

**Mount Kuàijī**
Yu’s ancestors gathered at Kuàijī,
Where he summoned his ministers.
Those who were disrespectful he punished,
Even executing the tall man.
Jade *gàn* marked the Xia,
Black stone inscribed the Qin.

**Huàn**
There is a beast without a mouth,
Its name is *Huàn*.
Harmful *qi* cannot enter,
Its body has no gaps.
The utmost of principle,
Arising from nature itself.

**Rhinoceros**
The rhinoceros’ head is like a wall,
Its form akin to an ox’s.
Its horns number three,
Emerging from its body in turns.
Snorting, it stirs the wind,
Its mighty *qi* brimming over.

**Sì**
The *sì* is a mighty beast,
Ox-like in form, dark and black.
Its strength topples all,
Even burning itself for its hide.
Its skin fills military stores,
Its horn aids civil virtue.

**Elephant**
The elephant is truly imposing,
Its body vast, its appearance strange.
Its flesh equals ten oxen,
Its eyes no larger than a boar’s.
Its head resembles its tail when seen from afar,
Its movement like a shifting hill.

**Zuan-Diao**
*Zuan-Diao* bears horns,
Its cry like a child’s wail.
With three hands, *qu*-like,
Its form resembles a *jiao*-heron,
Fish-bodied, serpent-tailed—
This is the tiger-dragon.

**Phoenix**
The phoenix, sacred bird,
Truly crowns the feathered kind.
Eight symbols compose its body,
Five virtues adorn its patterns.
Its wings and plumage arrive in harmony,
Responding to our sagely lord.

**Yu-Sui Valley**
The valley of *Yu-Sui*,
Therein dwells the balmy south wind.
When the green yang has faded,
Its *qi* answers *Zhu Rong*.
It fans the scorching haze,
Dispersing oppressive heat.

**Tuan Fish and Yong Bird**
The *yong* bird perches in the forest,
The *tuan* fish dwells in the deep.
Both foretell drought,
Disaster spreading across the heavens.
No image reveals their measure—
Their reckoning lies in profound mystery.

**White Su**
White *su* is lush and sweet,
Its sap like maltose.
Eating it dispels the need for grain,
Its flavor lingering, rich.
Roaming carefree, forgetting toil,
It sustains life to its fullest term.

*End of Scroll One of the Classic of Mountains and Seas*

#1 The original text was missing, reading *"Skin makes paper... [lacuna]... because of its..."*; according to Tan Chenggeng’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Skin makes paper. Can states: The valley is also called *gou*; it is named *gu* because its fruit resembles grain."*

#2 The original text was missing, reading *"Its light shines..."*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Its light shines upon the earth."*

#3 The original text was missing, reading *"The *yu* resembles... [lacuna]... large, red eyes, long..., [lacuna]... mountains of the south have many"*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"The *yu* resembles a macaque but is larger, with red eyes and a long tail. Today, the mountains of Jiangnan have many."*

#4 The original text was missing, reading *"Named *yu*,... [lacuna]... ox..., [lacuna]... also resembles an ox in form"*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Named *yu*, written with the character for ox, and the illustration also depicts an ox-like form."*

#5 The original text was missing, reading *"Produces..., [lacuna]... beast"*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Produces *shengsheng*, the *yu* beast."*

#6 The original text was missing, reading *"Moves with limbs intertwined..., [lacuna]..."*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Moves with limbs intertwined—this, too, is of the same kind."*

#7 The original text was missing, reading *"*Jia*... [lacuna]... disease"*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"The *jia* insect causes disease."*

#8 The original text was missing, reading *"The *hui*... [lacuna]... sharp"*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"The *hui* has a blunt tail."*

#9 The original text was missing, reading *"... [lacuna]... cannot... [lacuna]... as *di*"*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Swift and incurable—alternatively written as *di*."*

#10 The original text was missing, reading *"Three hundred *li* east is called... [lacuna]..."*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Three hundred *li* east is Mount Di."*

#11 The original text was missing, reading *"... [lacuna]... dog"*; according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"The plow-holding dog."*

#12 *"Pig-head storehouse..."*: according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Pig-head storehouse feet."*

#13 *"Likes to devour..."*: according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"Likes to devour thorns."*

#14 *"... [lacuna]... *peng*"*: according to Tan’s collated edition, it is emended to: *"The *duanli* and *zhepeng* birds."*

*End of Scroll Two of the Classic of Mountains and Seas*

**Guo Pu’s Commentary on the *Classic of Western Mountains***

The *Classic of Western Mountains* begins with Mount Qianlai. At its summit grow many pines; at its base lie many *xishi* stones. *Zaoxi* may be used to scrub the body, removing grime and filth. *Qiang* is pronounced *chu liang*. There is a beast here whose form resembles a sheep but with a horse’s tail, called the *xianyang*. In the land of the Greater Yuezhi, there are great sheep as large as donkeys with horse tails. The *Erya* states: “A sheep six *chi* tall is called *xian*,” referring to this sheep. *Xian* is pronounced like *zhen*. Its fat may be used to heal chapped skin. *La* is written with the character for “past.” Forty-five *li* to the west is Mount Songguo. The Huo River originates here, flowing northward into the Wei. Within it is much copper. There is a bird here called the *wuju*, *wu* pronounced like *tong* in *tonggong*. Its form resembles a pheasant, with a black body and red feet, and it may be used to heal □. This refers to skin that is broken and raised, pronounced *po bo*. Sixty *li* farther west is Mount Taihua, the Western Peak of Huayin. Today, it lies southwest of Huayin County in Hongnong. Its cliffs are sheer and four-sided, the mountain’s shape broader above and narrower below, towering and precipitous. Its height is five thousand *ren*, its breadth ten *li*; one *ren* equals eight *chi*. At its summit are the Bright Star Jade Maiden, who holds jade nectar. Those who consume it ascend as immortals. The Dao here is perilous and secluded, impassable. The *Poems Contain Divine Mist* states...

No beasts or birds dwell here. There is a serpent called the *feifeng*, with six feet and four wings; its appearance heralds great drought across the land. In the time of Tang, this serpent appeared beneath Yang Mountain. There is also the *feifei* serpent—likely another name for the same creature.

Eighty *li* farther west lies Mount Xiaohua, the Lesser Hua Mountain. Its trees abound with thorn and wolfberry, and its beasts with □-oxen. Today, in the Hua Mountains, there are many mountain oxen and goats, each weighing a thousand *jin*; these are the same oxen. (Pronounced *zuokou*.) Its northern slopes hold much sounding stone, which may be fashioned into musical chimes. Its southern slopes yield much □*fu* jade—*fu* being the name of the jade, though its nature remains unclear. (Pronounced *fu* or *fou*.) Among its birds are many red *jing*, a kind of pheasant: their breasts and bellies are hollow, their crests red, backs golden, heads yellow, tails green, with red at the center, their plumage vivid and bright. (Pronounced *bi*, or sometimes written as *bie*.) They may be used to guard against fire.

Its grasses include *bilie*, a fragrant herb. (Pronounced *bi* or *li*.) It resembles black moss but grows upon stone, or climbs trees; the black moss that grows on roofs is called *xixie*, while that on walls is called *yuanyi*. Eating it cures heart pain.

Eighty *li* farther west lies Mount Fuyu. Its southern slopes abound with copper, its northern slopes with iron. Upon it grows a tree called *wenjing*, whose fruit resembles dates and may cure deafness. Its grasses include much *tiao*, which resembles mallow but bears red flowers and yellow fruit, like an infant’s tongue; eating it prevents confusion.

The waters of Fuyu flow forth and course northward into the Wei River. Its beasts are many *conglong*, resembling sheep but with red manes. Its birds are many *min*—pronounced *min*—which resemble kingfishers but with crimson beaks and a dark blue hue like swallows. They may be kept to ward off fire and prevent conflagration.

Sixty *li* farther west lies Mount Shicui. Its trees abound with □*shan* trees. The □ tree stands some three *zhang* tall, without branches, its leaves large and round. Forked shoots emerge from its crown, and its fruit and bark grow in layers: each layer of bark counts as one segment, and may be twisted into rope. It is also called *pinlv*. (Pronounced like the *ma* in *ma□* and the *□* in *□*.)

Its grasses abound with *tiao*, which resemble leeks but bear white blossoms and black seeds; eating them cures scabies.

Its southern slopes yield many *fufu* jades, while its northern slopes abound with copper. The Guan River rises here and flows northward into the Yu River. Within it lies flowing ochre—ochre being red earth—used to daub oxen and horses to prevent illness. Today, people also paint ox horns with cinnabar, claiming it wards off evil. Some horses grow horns.

Seventy *li* farther west lies Mount Ying. Its heights are thick with *niu* and *jiang* trees. *Niu* resembles *di* but with finer leaves, also called *tujiang* (pronounced *niu*). *Jiang* is the wood used for chariot wheels (pronounced *jiang*). Its northern slopes abound with iron, its southern slopes with red gold. The Yu River rises here and flows northward into the Zhao River (pronounced *shao*). Within it dwell many *bang* fish (pronounced like the *bang* in clams). They resemble soft-shelled turtles and bleat like sheep. Its southern slopes yield many arrow *mei* bamboos—nowadays, Hanzhong Commandery produces *mei* bamboo, thick-walled with long internodes, deep roots, and winter shoots that grow underground, which people dig up to eat (pronounced *mei*).

Its beasts abound with *liu* oxen and *xian* sheep. There is a bird here resembling a quail, with a yellow body and red beak, called the *feiyi*. Eating it cures pestilence—*li* meaning epidemic disease. Some say it heals malignant sores. *Hanzi* states: "The *li*-stricken pity their lord." It can also kill parasites.

Fifty-two *li* farther west lies Mount Zhu. Its heights are thick with towering trees, their branches reaching upward (pronounced *qiao*).

Its northern slopes abound with iron. There grows a herb called *huangguan*, resembling the *chu* tree in form, its leaves like hemp, bearing white flowers and red fruit—its appearance like ochre, a crimson hue. Bathing in it cures scabies, and it can also heal *fu* (swelling). *Fu* refers to swollen sores. Pronounced like the *fu* in talisman.

The Bamboo River originates here, flowing northward into the Wei. Its southern slopes teem with arrow bamboo—*jian* and *xiao* varieties. There is much dark jade. The Cinnabar River also rises here. Nowadays, cinnabar deposits are found everywhere. It flows southeast into the Luo River. Within its waters lie many water-jades, and many *ren* fish, resembling the *bang* fish but with four legs.

There is a beast here, shaped like a piglet, yet covered in white fur, as large as a hairpin with black tips—*ji* refers to hairpins. It is called the *haozhi*, a type of wild boar. Its flanks bear coarse bristles several feet long, capable of shooting objects with the quills on its back. It is also self-sufficient as both male and female. *Huan* may also be written as *jia*; in Wu and Chu, it is called the *luanzhu*, belonging to the same species.

One hundred and twenty *li* farther west lies Mount Fu. It abounds with *pan* trees—*pan* pronounced like the *pan* in "beautiful eyes glancing." Their roots and leaves cause no harm, unlike the *zhi*, which bears thorns and can injure people, hence the name. Wood-boring insects dwell within them.

There grows a herb called *xun*—pronounced like the *xun* in "instruction." Its leaves resemble hemp, its stem square, bearing red flowers and black fruit. Its scent is like *miwu* (*miwu* being a fragrant herb). The *Yi* states: "Its fragrance is like orchid." *Mei* and *wu* are pronounced separately. Wearing it can cure pestilence.

Seventy *li* farther west lies Mount Yuci—*yu* pronounced like the *yu* in "moment." The Lacquer River originates here; nowadays, the Lacquer River rises in Mount Qi. It flows north into the Wei. Its heights abound with *yu* and *ju* trees—*yu* being the white *rui*. *Yu* pronounced like the *yu* in "region." Its lower slopes teem with arrow bamboo. Its northern slopes abound with red copper, while its southern slopes yield *yingyuan* jade. Some write it as *duan*, others as *gen* or *mai*; scribal errors have obscured the details.

There is a beast here, shaped like a *yu* but with long arms, skilled at throwing. It is called the *xiao*. It also appears in the *Wei Huan* paintings, resembling a macaque in its throwing ability.

There is a bird here, shaped like an owl, with a human face and a single foot. It is called the *tuoyu*—*tuo* pronounced like the *fei* in "fat." It appears in winter and hibernates in summer. Wearing its feathers makes one unafraid of thunder. Donning its plumage renders one immune to celestial thunder. Some write it as *zai*.

One hundred and fifty *li* farther west lies Mount Shi. Barren of grass and trees, the *Zhu* (or *Sui*) River originates here, flowing north into the Wei. Its waters abound with water-jades.

One hundred and seventy *li* farther west lies Mount Nan. Its heights abound with cinnabar grains. The Cinnabar River rises here, flowing north into the Wei. Its beasts abound with fierce leopards—fierce leopards resemble bears but are smaller, with pale, lustrous fur. They can devour snakes and consume copper and iron, found in Shu. Some write *leopard* as *tiger*. Its birds abound with *shijiu*—*shijiu* being a type of cuckoo. Some say it is the *penghe*. *Jiu* may also be written as *qiu*.

One hundred and eighty *li* farther west lies Mount Dashi. Its heights abound with *gu* and *zuo* trees—*zuo* being the oak. Its lower slopes teem with *niu* and *ju*. Its northern slopes abound with silver, while its southern slopes yield white jade. The *Cen* River—*cen* pronounced like the *qian* in "latent"—originates here, flowing north into the Wei. The Clear River also rises here, flowing south into the Han. Nowadays, in the north of Xiuwu County in Henan, Black Mountain also gives rise to the Clear River.

Three hundred and twenty *li* farther west lies Mount Panzhong—now located south of Daoxian in Wudu. *Bo* pronounced like the *bo* in "wave." The Han River originates here, flowing southeast into the Mian. Near Jiangxia’s Anlu County, the Jiang is the Mian River. The *Xiao* River also rises here, flowing north into the Tang River. Some write it as *Yang*.

Its heights abound with *taozhi* and *gouduan*—*gouduan* being a type of *taozhi*. Its beasts abound with rhinoceroses, water buffaloes, bears, and *xiong*—*xiong* resembling bears but with yellowish-white fur, fierce and strong enough to uproot trees. Its birds abound with white *han* and red *bie*—*han* being the white *zhan*, also called *zhan* pheasant or white pheasant.

There grows a herb here, its leaves like *hui*—*hui* being a fragrant herb of the orchid family. Some mistakenly take *hui* as *xun* leaves. Its root resembles *jiegeng*. It bears black flowers but no fruit, called *gurong*. The *Erya*’s *Explanation of Plants* states: "That which blooms but bears no fruit is called *gu*." Pronounced like the *gu* in "bone." Eating it renders one childless.

Three hundred and fifty *li* farther west lies Mount Tiandi. Its heights abound with *nan* trees, while its lower slopes teem with *jian* and *hui*—*jian* being a type of grass akin to cogon. There is a beast here, shaped like a dog, called the *xi* (or *gu*) *bian* (or *yi*). Sitting on its hide prevents *gu* poisoning.

There is a bird here, shaped like a quail, with black markings and red *fang*—*weng* refers to the feathers beneath the head. Pronounced like the *weng* in "water jar." It is called the *li*—pronounced like the *li* in "gravel." Eating it cures hemorrhoids.

There grows a herb here, shaped like the *kui*, its scent like *miwu*. It is called *duheng*, a fragrant herb. It can make horses run swiftly; wearing it benefits horses, or some say horses gain strength from it. Eating it cures goiter.

Three hundred and eighty *li* to the southwest lies Mount Gaotu.

The *qiang* (pronounced like *se* in "color," or sometimes written as *huang* or *kui*) grows here. A river flows forth, coursing westward into the Zhuzi waters. The Tu River also emerges here, flowing southward into the Jihao waters. On its southern slopes abound cinnabar grains; on its northern slopes, silver and gold. Its heights are thick with cassia trees. There is a white stone here, called *yu*, which can poison rats—nowadays, *yu* stone is used to kill rats (pronounced like *yu* in "prepared"). Silkworms that feed on it grow plump.

There is a herb here, shaped like *gaoba*, yellow when discarded, a fragrant plant. Its leaves resemble those of the *kui* but with red undersides; it is called *wutiao* and can poison rats.

There is a beast here, shaped like a deer with a white tail, horse’s hooves, and human-like front paws. It has four horns and is called *jueyu* (pronounced like *wo jue* in "ape").

There is a bird here, shaped like an owl but with human feet, called *shusi*. Eating it cures emaciation (or epilepsy).

One hundred and eighty *li* farther west lies Mount Huang. Today, in Huaili County of Shiping, there is a Mount Huang, atop which stands an old palace built by Emperor Hui of Han—this may not be the same mountain. It has no grass or trees but abounds in bamboo arrows. The Pan River (pronounced like *pan* in "beautiful eyes glancing") flows forth, coursing westward into the Red Water, and its depths hold much jade.

There is a beast here, shaped like an ox but dark gray with large eyes, called *min* (pronounced like *min* in "quick"). There is also a bird, shaped like an owl with azure feathers and a red beak, a human tongue, and the ability to speak. It is called the *yingwu* (parrot). The *yingwu* has a tongue like a child’s, with two toes each at the front and back. In the distant lands of Funan, beyond the borders, five-colored varieties appear, as well as pure red and white ones, as large as geese.

Two hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Cui. Its heights are thick with *nan* trees, and its lower slopes abound in bamboo arrows. On its southern slopes lie gold and jade; on its northern slopes, *mao* oxen, musk deer, and antelopes. The *ling* resembles a sheep but has large horns, fine fodder, and favors cliffs. The *she* resembles a river deer but is smaller and fragrant. Its birds are mostly *lei* (pronounced like *lei* in "rampart"), shaped like magpies but red and black, with two heads and four feet. They can ward off fire.

Two hundred and fifty *li* farther west lies Mount Gui (pronounced like *wei* in "lofty," or like *po* in "Chenxiao’s soul"). It stands beside the Western Sea. *Chun* here means "to stand beside" (pronounced like *zhang* and *run* in reverse). It has no grass or trees but abounds in jade. The Qi River (or *nei*) flows forth, coursing westward into the sea. Its depths hold many colored stones and gold—colored stones are those with hues, such as orpiment, azurite, and malachite. It also abounds in cinnabar grains.

In all, the western mountains recorded here begin at Mount Qianlai and end at Mount Gui, comprising nineteen mountains over a distance of two thousand nine hundred and fifty-seven *li*. Mount Hua is their ancestral tomb, for tombs are where spirits and ghosts reside. The rites for its sacrifice: a grand offering of a bull, a ram, and a boar. The god of Mount Yu is also sacrificed to with torches (or *yang*), fasting for a hundred days with a hundred sacrificial animals (pure-colored beasts are called *xi*). The seating uses a hundred *yu* (a fine jade, pronounced like *yu* in "carry"), and the wine is warmed in a hundred vessels. It is adorned with a hundred *gui* and a hundred *bi* (to *ying* means to arrange them in a ring for the sacrifice; some say *ying* is an ancient form of *ying*, meaning a jar). As Xu Zhou states: *"The* Mu Tianzi Zhuan *says: 'Objects such as golden* ying *belong to this.'"*

For the remaining seventeen mountains, all use unblemished animals (a *quan* is a beast with all its parts intact, as the *Zuozhuan* says: "a fat and plump sacrificial animal") and a single sheep for the sacrifice. The torches are made from the ashes of a hundred grasses, laid upon white mats with pure silk borders. The silk is woven in five colors, arranged in graded patterns. As the *Zhouli* states: "Reed mats with multicolored borders."

The second western section begins at Mount Qian (pronounced like *qian* in "shackles," or sometimes written as *leng* or *tu*). Its heights abound in copper, its lower slopes in jade, and its trees are mostly *niu* and *jiang*.

Two hundred *li* to the west lies Mount Tai (or Qin) Mao. Its southern slopes abound in gold, its northern slopes in iron. The Yu River flows forth, coursing eastward into the Yellow River. Its depths hold much *zao* jade (*zao* jade is jade with patterns, sometimes written as *jian*, pronounced like *lian* in "refined"). It also abounds in white snakes, water *chi*.

One hundred and seventy *li* farther west lies Mount Shuli. Its heights abound in gold, its lower slopes in silver, and its trees are mostly *niu* and *jiang*. Its birds are mostly *yingwu*. The Chu River flows forth, coursing southward into the Wei. Its depths hold many white pearls. Today, the marshes of Shu Commandery yield green pearls. As the *Shizi* states: "Where water bends and swirls, pearls are found."

One hundred and fifty *li* farther west lies Mount Gao. Its heights abound in silver, its lower slopes in azure jade (jade-like *bi* is also produced in the eastern mountains of Yuexi and Kuaiji counties) and realgar. In the third year of the Daixing era of the Jin, a landslide in Gaoping Commandery unearthed several thousand *jin* of realgar. Its trees are mostly *nan*, and its grasses are mostly bamboo. The Jing River (pronounced like *jing* in "classic") flows forth, coursing eastward into the Wei. Today, the Jing River rises in the western wellspring mountains of Kongdingchao County and enters the Wei at Gaoling County in Jingzhao. Its depths hold much *qing* stone (as the *Book of Documents* says: "floating *qing* from the Si River banks"). It also abounds in azure jade.

Three hundred *li* to the southwest lies Mount Nüchuang. Its southern slopes abound in red copper, its northern slopes in alum stone (also called *nie* stone; the people of Chu call it *nie* stone, while the Qin call it *yu nie*; the *Shennong Bencaojing* also calls it *shi nie*). Its beasts are mostly tigers, leopards, rhinoceroses, and water buffaloes. There is a bird here, shaped like a *di* but with five-colored patterns (the *di* resembles a pheasant but is larger, with a long tail; or sometimes written as *□□*, a type of eagle). It is called the *luan* bird, and its appearance heralds peace throughout the land. Old accounts say the *luan* resembles a chicken, a propitious bird. It was presented as tribute by the Western Rong during the reign of King Cheng of Zhou.

Two hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Longshou. Its southern slopes abound in yellow gold, its northern slopes in iron. The Tiao River rises here and flows southeast into the Jing River. Within it are many fine jades.

Two hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Lutai, now located in Shang Commandery. Its summit abounds in white jade, its base in silver. Its beasts are mostly *□* oxen, *xian* sheep, and white *hao*. (*Hao* refers to the *huan* pig.) There is a bird here, shaped like a rooster but with a human face, called the *fuxi*. Its name is its own cry, and its appearance heralds war.

Two hundred *li* to the southwest lies Mount Niaowei. Its southern slopes abound in sounding stones, its northern slopes in sandalwood and paper mulberry (*chu*, also called *gu* wood). Within it are many *nüchuang* (unverified). The Wuwei River rises here and flows west into the Chi River, containing much cinnabar grain.

Four hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Xiaoci. Its summit abounds in white jade, its base in red copper. There is a beast here, shaped like an ape but with a white head and red feet, called the *zhuyan*. Its appearance heralds great war. (One version says its appearance heralds the rise of six armies; another says it heralds warfare.)

Three hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Daci. Its southern slopes abound in chalk (*e*, white as earth; pronounced *wu*), its northern slopes in azure jade. Its beasts are mostly *□* oxen and antelopes.

Four hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Xunwu. It has no vegetation but abounds in gold and jade.

Four hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Diyang (pronounced *zhi*). Its trees are mostly *ji*, *gou*, and *yuzhang*. (*Ji* resembles pine, with thorns and fine grain; pronounced *ji*. *Yuzhang* is a large tree resembling the *jiu*, its leaves evergreen in winter and summer; its nature becomes known only after seven years of growth.) Its beasts are mostly rhinoceroses, water buffaloes, tigers, dogs, and *□* oxen. (*Zhuo* is pronounced *zhiyao*.)

Two hundred fifty *li* farther west lies Mount Zhongshou. Its summit abounds in *□fu* jade, its base in sandalwood and paper mulberry, and much yellow gold. Its beasts are mostly rhinoceroses and water buffaloes.

Five hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Huangren. Its summit abounds in gold and jade, its base in azure orpiment (also called *cihuang*; some say it includes *kongqing* and *zengqing*). The Huang River rises here and flows west into the Chi River, containing much cinnabar grain.

Three hundred *li* farther west lies Mount Zhonghuang. Its summit abounds in yellow gold, its base in *huitang* (a type of *tongtang*; *hui* may also be written as *gao*).

Three hundred fifty *li* farther west lies Mount Xihuang. Its southern slopes abound in gold, its northern slopes in iron. Its beasts are mostly elk and *□* oxen. (The *mi* is as large as a small ox, a type of deer.)

Three hundred fifty *li* farther west lies Mount Lai. Its trees are mostly sandalwood and paper mulberry, and its birds are mostly *luoluo*, which devour humans. (The *luoluo* bird remains unverified.)

In all, the beginning of the *Western Second Classic* spans seventeen mountains from Mount Qian to Mount Lai, covering four thousand one hundred forty *li*. Of its ten spirits, all have human faces and horse bodies. Of its seven spirits, all have human faces, ox bodies, four legs, and one arm, wielding staffs as they walk—these are the spirits of flying beasts. Their sacrifices use the *shaolao* (a sheep and pig as the *shaolao*). White *jian* grass is used for mats. (The *bei* in "ten *bei*" is pronounced *bei*.)

For the spirits, their sacrifices use one rooster, *qian* but no *xu*. (*Qian* is the name of a sacrificial vessel, unverified; or it may mean "to pray for instruction without *xu*." Sacrifices do not use rice. *Maocai* refers to using a multicolored rooster.)

The beginning of the *Western Third Classic* is Mount Chongwu. Located south of the River, to the north it overlooks Mount Zhongsui (a mountain name), to the south it overlooks Duoyao Marsh (pronounced *yao*), to the west it overlooks the Emperor’s *Boshou* Hill (*bo* may also be written as *bu*), and to the east it overlooks *□* (pronounced *yuran*) Abyss. There is a tree here with round leaves and white *fu* (nowadays, people south of the Yangtze call the seed pods of plants *fu*, pronounced *fu*; one version says *fu* is the calyx beneath the flower, pronounced like the character for "husband"; or it may be written as *fu*, pronounced *fu*). Its flowers are red with black veins, and its fruit resembles the *zhi*. Eating it benefits descendants.

There is a beast here, shaped like a *yu* but with patterned arms, resembling a leopard or tiger and skilled at throwing, called the *Jufu* (or *Kuafu*). There is a bird here, shaped like a wild duck but with one wing and one eye; they must pair to fly, called the *Manman* (the *biyi* bird). Its color is greenish-red, and it cannot fly alone. (*Erya* writes it as *□□* bird.) Its appearance heralds great floods throughout the land.

Three hundred *li* to the northwest lies Mount Changsha. The Ci River (pronounced *zi*) rises here and flows north into the You River (*jiao* is pronounced *jiao*; also pronounced *you*, meaning "black water"). It has no vegetation but abounds in azure orpiment.

Three hundred seventy *li* farther northwest lies Mount Buzhou. This mountain’s form has gaps, hence its name. The northwest *buzhou* wind originates from this mountain. To the north, it overlooks Mount Zhupi and faces Mount Yuechong; to the east, it overlooks You Marsh, where the River lies hidden. Its source surges turbulently. (The River emerges from Kunlun, flows underground, and resurfaces at Congling in the state of Yutian. It then splits, converges, and flows east into You Marsh, where it hides again before emerging south at Jishi Mountain as the Chinese River. You Marsh is also called Pu Marsh or Puchang Sea, spanning three to four hundred *li*. Its waters stagnate, neither increasing nor decreasing in winter or summer. It lies over three hundred *li* from Yumen Pass and is the River’s secondary source, referred to as the "hidden flow." *Hunhun paopao* describes the sound of surging water. Both *gun* and *pao* are pronounced accordingly.)

Here grows a fine fruit. Its fruit resembles the peach, its leaves the jujube. Its flowers are yellow with red *fu*. Eating it prevents fatigue.

Further northwest four hundred and twenty *li* lies Mount Mi. Its name is pronounced *mi*. Atop it grow many cinnabar trees—round-leaved with crimson stems, bearing yellow blossoms and red fruit. Their taste is like maltose; eating them banishes hunger. The Cinnabar River rises here, flowing west into Ji Marsh. The Divine Farmer Hou Ji drowned in it, hence the name. Within it lies abundant white jade, and there is a jade nectar. Its source seethes and surges, the very appearance of jade nectar welling forth. The *River Chart Jade Tablet* states: “On Shaoshi Mountain grows white jade nectar; one dose and one becomes immortal.” This is of the same kind. *Fei* is pronounced *fu*.

The Yellow Emperor consumed and offered it. Thus he ascended the dragon at Ding Lake and shed his mortal form. From it is born dark jade, meaning all black jade emerges from the jade nectar. Where the jade nectar flows, it irrigates the cinnabar trees. After five years, the cinnabar trees assume the five colors and become radiant—meaning their luster shines. Their five flavors become fragrant, meaning their aroma is rich. The Yellow Emperor then gathered the jade blossoms of Mount Mi—referring to the jade’s splendor. The *Li Sao* says: “I cherish the splendid blossoms of the pepper tree,” and again: “I ascend Kunlun to feast on jade blossoms.” This is the jade spoken of in the *Ji Tomb Writings* as “Cao Hua’s jade.” He cast it upon the southern slopes of Mount Zhong to serve as jade seed.

The finest jade is that of *jin* and *yu*, meaning it is most excellent. Some write it as “to consume.” Both *jin* and *yu* are pronounced accordingly. Its grain is firm, fine, and dense—this describes the jade’s texture. The *Book of Rites* says: “Its density resembles millet,” where *su* may also be written as *li*. Jade with millet-like veins is called *gu bi*. It is lustrous despite its turbid richness—*zhuo* here means thick and moist. The five colors emerge in interplay, meaning their hues reflect one another. Prince Lingfu’s *Divine Talisman* states: “Red as a rooster’s comb, yellow as steamed millet, white as sliced fat, black as pure lacquer—these are the jade’s divine hues.” It harmonizes softness and hardness, meaning jade embodies the nine virtues.

Heaven and Earth, ghosts and spirits, consume and offer it. Jade is used in prayers and sacrifices because it can move Heaven and Earth and stir ghosts and spirits. The noble person wears it to ward off misfortune. Nowadays, beyond the borders, adamantine stone is produced. It belongs to the stone family yet resembles metal, gleaming and capable of carving jade. Foreigners carry it, saying it repels malign qi—this is of the same kind.

From Mount Mi to Mount Zhong spans four hundred and sixty *li*, all marshland. There abound singing birds, strange beasts, and wondrous fish—all extraordinary creatures.

Further northwest four hundred and twenty *li* lies Mount Zhong. Its child is named Gu, also a divine name, simply called the child of Mount Zhong. Their forms are recorded in the *Returning to the Hidden: Divination of Qi*. They resemble humans with dragon bodies. The *Divination of Qi* says: “The child of Li Mountain has green feathers, eight faces, and a horse’s body.” This form is similar. They, along with Qin □ (pronounced *pi*), slew Bao Jiang on the southern slopes of Kunlun. *Bao* may also be written as *Zu*. The Emperor executed them east of Mount Zhong at □ (pronounced *yao* with a cliff radical). Qin □ transformed into a great osprey—*e*, of the eagle family, pronounced □. Its form resembles an eagle but with black patterns, a white head, red beak, and tiger claws. Its cry is like the morning swan—*chen hu*, of the osprey family, meaning “morning brightness.” The *Garden of Persuasion* says: “The county’s barking dogs compare to serving the morning swan.” When it appears, great war follows. Gu also transformed into a *jun* bird (pronounced *jun*). Its form resembles an owl, with red feet and a straight beak, yellow patterns and a white head. Its cry is like a swan’s. When it appears, its domain suffers severe drought. The *Tale of King Mu* says: “Mount Zhong heralds spring,” though the pronunciation is the same.

King Mu journeyed north beyond this mountain to survey the four wilds and declared: “Mount Zhong is the highest mountain under Heaven, where all beasts gather and flying birds roost.” There are red leopards, white tigers, white crows, and green eagles, seizing dogs and sheep, devouring boars and deer. King Mu observed Mount Zhong for five days, then inscribed his traces upon the hanging gardens to instruct later generations.

Further west one hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Taiqi. The Guan River rises here, flowing west into the Flowing Sands. There abound patterned *yao* fish (pronounced *yao*). They resemble carp but with fish bodies and bird wings, azure patterns and white heads, red beaks. They constantly travel the Western Sea, roaming the Eastern Sea, and fly by night. Their cry is like the *luan* chicken—*luan ji*, a bird name, not yet identified. Some write it as *le*. Their flavor is sour-sweet; eating them cures madness. When they appear, the world enjoys great abundance—*feng rang*, meaning bountiful harvests. The *Hanzi* says: “In the autumn of a rich year.”

Further west three hundred and twenty *li* lies Mount Huaijiang. The Qiushi River rises here, flowing north into the You River.

Within this mountain dwell many *yingmu*, which are spiral conch shells. Its heights abound in green *xionghuang* (realgar) and conceal *langgan*, gold, and jade. *Langgan* refers to stones resembling pearls. *Cang* means "hidden." Pronounced *lang* and *gan*. Its southern slopes yield many cinnabar grains, while its northern slopes yield fine gold and silver.

This is truly the Emperor’s level garden, that is, the Dark Garden. *The Chronicle of King Mu* states: "Thereupon he inscribed his traces upon the Dark Garden," meaning he carved stone to record his merits and virtues, as the First Emperor of Qin and Emperor Wu of Han did.

The spirit Yingzhao presides over it. *Si* means "to govern." Pronounced *shao*. His form is that of a horse’s body with a human face, tiger stripes, and bird wings, patrolling the four *mei*. *Xun* means "to circumnavigate." His cry is like the *liu* (sound *liu*, sometimes written as *zhou*), though its meaning remains unclear.

Gazing south toward Kunlun, its radiance blazes like a bear, its *qi* billowing and surging—such is the intensity of its fiery brilliance and vital force. Gazing west toward the great marsh, this is where Hou Ji was concealed. Hou Ji was born divine; even in his hidden transformations, he followed this marsh and became its spirit.

To the north, one sees Mount Zhupi, where the spirit Huaigui Lilun resides. Lilun is the name of this deity. It is the dwelling place of eagles and *zhan* (a kind of hawk, related to the owl). Zhuangzi said: "Owls delight in mice." *The Chronicle of King Mu* records: "On Mount Zhongshan there are white crows and azure eagles," all of this kind.

Looking east toward Mount Heng, which has four *cheng* (layers). *Cheng* also means "layers." *The Erya* states: "Two layers are called *ying*." The spirit Youqiong resides here, each group occupying one *bo*. *Bo* means "flank," indicating that the various spirits gather by kind and dwell on the four flanks of the mountain. *Youqiong* is their general name. *Bo* is sometimes written as *chuan*.

Here flows the Yin Water, its clarity rippling in gentle waves. *Luo-luo* describes the water’s descending flow; *yu* is pronounced like *yao*. Within resides a celestial spirit, its form like an ox, yet with eight legs, two heads, and a horse’s tail. Its voice resembles *Bo Huang* (the nature of *Bo Huang* remains unclear). Wherever it appears, war befalls the nearby settlements.

Four hundred *li* to the southwest lies the Kunlun Range, the true Lower Capital of the Thearch, the celestial abode of the Heavenly Emperor below. *The Chronicle of King Mu* states: "On the auspicious day *xin-you*, the Son of Heaven ascended Kunlun’s heights to behold the Yellow Emperor’s palace, and there sealed the burial of Feng Long, bequeathing it to later generations." This speaks of the augmented burial upon Kunlun’s summit. The spirit Lu Wu governs it—Lu Wu being the same as Jian Wu. Zhuangzi said: "Jian Wu received it to dwell upon the great mountain." This spirit has the body of a tiger, nine tails, the face of a human, and tiger claws. It oversees the nine divisions of heaven and the seasons of the Thearch’s gardens, presiding over the boundaries of the nine celestial domains and the seasonal adornments of the Heavenly Emperor’s parks.

There is a beast here, its form like a sheep but with four horns, called *Tu Lou*, which devours humans. There is a bird, its form like a wasp but as large as a mandarin duck, named *Qin Yuan* (*Qin* may also be written as *You* or *Zhi*). Where it stings beasts, they die; where it stings trees, they wither. There is another bird, named *Chun Niao*, which oversees the Thearch’s myriad implements—*fu* meaning "utensils" or, alternatively, "service." Elsewhere, there is a tree resembling the *tang* (pear), with yellow blossoms and red fruit. Its taste is like a plum but without a pit, called *Sha Tang*. It can ward off drowning, and those who eat it shall not sink. (The text here is fragmentary.) *Sha Tang* is a wood that cannot... (text incomplete). *The Annals of Lü Buwei* states: "Among the finest fruits is the fruit of the *Sha Tang*." An inscription reads: "...*Sha Tang*... carved into a boat, drifting upon the azure sea, to roam and wander."

There is a herb named *□-grass* (pronounced *pin*), its form like the mallow, its taste like the scallion. Eating it cures fatigue. *The Annals of Lü Buwei* states: "Among the finest vegetables is the *□* of Kunlun." The Ke Water issues forth from the mountain’s northeast corner, flowing southward and eastward into Wu Da (a mountain name). The Chi Water emerges from the southeast corner, flowing southeast into Fan Tian’s waters (Fan Tian being another mountain, where the Chi Water ends). *The Chronicle of King Mu* records: "Thereupon he lodged beside Kunlun, on the yang (north) side of the Chi Water." *Yang* refers to the north bank of a river; *fan* is pronounced with the *fu* and *jian* initials.

The Yang Water flows from the northwest corner (sometimes written as *Wei*). It courses southwest into Chou Tu’s waters (Chou Tu being another mountain, all located in the southern extremity). *The Chronicle of King Mu* states: "On *wu-chen*, he crossed the Yang Water," also called "the Son of Heaven’s Yang Water." The Hei Water also issues from the northwest corner, flowing west into Da Yu (a mountain name). *The Chronicle of King Mu* records: "Thereupon he sealed the burial of Chang Gong west of the Hei Water’s western river, declaring it the summit of Kunlun’s Hong Lü, to serve as the Zhou royal house’s presiding spirit." *Gan* is pronounced *yu*. The region abounds in monstrous birds and beasts—such as a single beast with nine heads or a bird with six heads.

Three hundred and seventy *li* farther west lies Mount Le You. The Tao River flows from it, coursing west into Ji Marsh, where white jade is plentiful. Within dwell many *□-fish* (pronounced *yu*), their form like a serpent but with four legs, which prey upon fish.

Traveling four hundred *li* west by waterway and two hundred *li* farther reaches Mount Ying Mu, governed by the spirit Chang Cheng, who embodies the nine virtues of heaven—the vital *qi* of the nine virtues. This spirit resembles a human but with a *you*-like tail (pronounced with the *zhuo* and *yao* initials). The mountain’s heights are rich in jade, its lower slopes in green stone, yet it lacks water.

Three hundred and fifty *li* farther west lies Jade Mountain, the dwelling of the Queen Mother of the West. This mountain abounds in jade and stones, hence its name. *The Chronicle of King Mu* calls it the Mountain of Myriad Jades, noting its unobstructed rivers and roads, all aligned as if by a carpenter’s line, the so-called "strategic treasury" of the former kings. Sparse in grass and trees, devoid of birds and beasts, King Mu there quarried its jade and stones, taking three chariot-loads of jade slabs, jade utensils, and ceremonial objects, carrying ten thousand *zhi* of jade back with him. (Two jades make a *ke*; half a *ke* makes a *zhi*.)

The Queen Mother of the West resembles a human, with a leopard’s tail, tiger’s fangs, and a penchant for howling. Her hair is disheveled, and she wears a *sheng* (a jade hairpin, pronounced *pang*). She governs heaven’s calamities and the Five Destructions, presiding over the *qi* of disasters, plagues, and the five punishments of execution. *The Chronicle of King Mu* states: "On the auspicious day *jia-zi*, the Son of Heaven was received as a guest by the Queen Mother of the West. He presented a *gui* tablet of jade and a white jade disc, offering a hundred rolls of dark silk and three hundred rolls of deep blue silk. The Queen Mother of the West bowed twice and accepted them. On *yi-chou*, the Son of Heaven toasted the Queen Mother of the West upon the Jasper Pool. The Queen Mother of the West sang for the Son of Heaven:

*White clouds drift in the sky,*
*The mountains and hills arise on their own.*
*The roads stretch far and wide,*
*Mountains and rivers lie between.*
*May you not die, my lord,*
*And come again.*

The Son of Heaven responded:
*I return to the eastern lands,*
*To harmonize and govern the central states.*
*When the myriad people are equitable and at peace,*
*I shall see you again.*
*Within three years,*
*I shall revisit your wilds.*

The Queen Mother of the West then chanted for the Son of Heaven:
*Dwelling in the western lands,*
*Abiding in your place,*
*Tigers and leopards gather in packs,*
*Crows and magpies keep you company.*
*Auspicious fate does not depart,*
*For I am the Thearch’s daughter.*
*What manner of mortal are you,*
*Who would now leave me?*
