*Blowing the sheng and striking the reed pipes,*
*My heart soars freely.*
*O child of this generation,*
*You are heaven’s hope.*

Thereupon the Son of Heaven was moved. He ascended Yan Mountain, inscribed his traces upon its stones, and planted a locust tree, declaring: 'This is the Mountain of the Queen Mother of the West.'"

Yan Mountain is Mount Yanzi. According to the *Bamboo Annals*, in the fifty-seventh year of King Mu’s reign, the Queen Mother of the West came to audience and was received as a guest in the Bright Palace. In the time of Shun, the Queen Mother of the West dispatched envoys to present jade rings, as recorded in the *Rites of the Three Courts*.

There is a beast whose form resembles a dog but bears leopard markings, with horns like those of an ox—or sometimes a ram. Some call it the *jiao*, and its cry is like a barking dog. Wherever it appears, the land enjoys great abundance.

In the tenth year of the Tai Kang reign of Jin, a beast was captured in a cage in Fuyi County of Shaoling. Its form bore leopard markings, with two horns but no forelegs. The people of the time took it for the *jiao*, though this was uncertain.

There is a bird whose form resembles a pheasant but is red, called the *shengyu*, of the *Ting* clan. It feeds on fish, and its cry is like *lu* (音绿), though the meaning is unclear. Wherever it appears, the land suffers great floods.

Four hundred and eighty *li* further west lies the Mound of Xuanyuan. No grasses or trees grow there. The Yellow Thearch dwelt upon this mound and took a daughter of the Western Mound clan as his wife, whence it is called the Mound of Xuanyuan. The Xun River (音询) issues forth from here, flowing southward into the Black River. Its waters abound with cinnabar grains and much green realgar.

Three hundred *li* further west stands Mount Jishi. At its base lies a stone gate through which the River waters surge westward—*mao* meaning "to cover." Mount Jishi is located among the Qiang tribes southwest of Jincheng and Hemen Pass, where the River waters course [through gaps] and enter the passes from the east. This mountain contains all things under heaven.

The *Classic of Waters* cites the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*, stating: "Mount Jishi lies east of Denglin Mountain, where the River enters."

Two hundred *li* further west lies Mount Changliu, where the White Thearch Shaohao resides. Shaohao, of the Metal Heaven clan, is the title of Thearch Zhi. The beasts here all bear patterned tails—or sometimes long ones. The birds all bear patterned heads—or sometimes long ones. The mountain abounds with patterned jade stones. It is in truth the palace of the Round Spirit Kui (音隗). This spirit governs the reflection of light. When the sun sets in the west, its light is reflected eastward, and the spirit oversees its observation.

Two hundred and eighty *li* further west lies Mount Zhangyi. No grasses or trees grow there, but it abounds with *yao* jade and *bi* jade (*bi* being another kind of jade). The phenomena there are most strange, and many extraordinary things are found.

Herein dwells a beast resembling a red leopard, with five tails and one horn, its cry like the striking of stone—its name is *Zheng*. As the *Jing Shi Yi Yi* states: "Its sound is like stones striking one another," meaning its voice is tranquil.

Herein dwells a bird resembling a crane, with one foot, red markings on a green body, and a white beak—its name is *Bifang*. It cries its own name, and where it appears, the town suffers strange fires. (*E* here signifies the character for "portent" or "misfortune.")

Three hundred *li* further west lies Mount Yin, whence the turbid *Zhuoyu* River flows southward into Fan Marsh. Within it abound patterned cowries, of the kind described in the *Erya*. Herein dwells a beast resembling a leopard—or sometimes a wildcat—with a white head, named *Tiangou*. Its cry is like *liuliu*—or sometimes *miaomiao*. It can ward off misfortune.

Two hundred *li* further west lies Mount Futì. Its heights abound with *□ge* trees, its depths with gold and jade. The spirit Jiang Yi resides here. This mountain is the source of strange rains, winds, and clouds.

Two hundred and twenty *li* further west lies Mount Sanwei, now located in Dunhuang Prefecture. As the *Book of Documents* states: "The Three Miao were exiled to Sanwei." Three azure birds dwell here. This mountain spans a hundred *li* in circumference. The three azure birds serve the Queen Mother of the West by fetching her food, though they rest separately upon this mountain. The *Bamboo Annals* record: "King Mu, on his western campaign, reached the place where the azure birds alighted."

Herein dwells a beast resembling an ox, with a white body, four horns, and fur like a rain-repelling straw cloak—its name is *Ao□* (*Ao* or *Yi* in pronunciation). It devours humans.

Herein dwells a bird with one head and three bodies, resembling a *□* (a bird akin to an eagle, with black markings and a red neck)—its name is *Chi*. (*Luo* in standing posture. A later line, possibly misplaced here, reads: "If one grasps its talons, death follows; if one grasps its roots, withering ensues." This should belong beneath the *Yinyuan* entry above.)

One hundred and ninety *li* further west lies Mount Gui. Its heights abound with jade but lack stone. The spirit Qi Tong resides here—Qi Tong being the Old Child, son of Zhuanxu. Its voice is ever like the ringing of bells and chimes. Its depths teem with coiled serpents.

Three hundred and fifty *li* further west lies Mount Tian. It abounds in gold and jade, and contains green realgar. The Ying River flows from here, coursing southwest into Tang Valley. Herein dwells a spirit resembling a yellow sack, red as cinnabar fire, its body yellow yet radiant with crimson light. It has six legs and four wings, is utterly formless, without face or eyes, yet knows song and dance—it is indeed the Emperor Jiang. For when form is incomplete, the spirit becomes naturally numinous; when essence is unseen, it merges with principle—does this not describe the Emperor Jiang? Zhuangzi’s tale of the Central Emperor Hundun, who died when Shu and Hu drilled his seven apertures, is but a parable drawn from this.

Two hundred and ninety *li* further west lies Mount You (*You*, pronounced like the "you" in "dark"). The spirit Mu Shou resides here.

Here also resides a metal spirit, human-faced with tiger claws, a white tail, and holding a *yue*-axe—so recorded in the *Outer Traditions*. Its summit abounds in *yingduan* jade, though the term remains undetermined. On its sunlit slopes lie many *jinyu* jades, while its shaded side holds abundant green realgar. This mountain stands where the sun sets in the west; its *qi* is round, the sun’s form is round, and thus its celestial aspect is likewise round. It is governed by the spirit of Crimson Radiance, though its appearance has not been described.

Proceeding west by water for a hundred *li* brings one to Mount Yiwang (or, as some write, Mount Tucui). No grasses or trees grow here, but gold and jade are plentiful. A beast dwells here, shaped like a wildcat, with one eye and three tails—its name is *Huan* (pronounced like "joy," sometimes written *Yuan*). Its cry resembles a hundred voices, for it can mimic the sounds of a hundred creatures. (Some say *Huan* is a creature’s name, though this remains unverified.) It wards off misfortune, and wearing its pelt cures *zhang*-illness (yellow *zhang*-disease, pronounced like "dawn"). A bird also lives here, shaped like an ordinary bird but with three heads, six tails, and a fondness for laughter—its name is *Qiyi* (awaiting the two missing pronunciations). Consuming it prevents *yan*-dreams (dream-oppression; in the *Book of Zhou*, *yan* means "unrestful," pronounced like *moli* in reverse; some say it refers to blurred vision). It, too, wards off misfortune.

From the first mountain of the Third Western Range, Mount Chongwu, to Mount Yiwang, there are twenty-three mountains spanning six thousand seven hundred and forty-four *li*. Their spirits all take the form of sheep-bodied, human-faced beings. The rites for their worship require burying a single auspicious jade (a jade adorned with colors; the *Master Shi* states: "Auspicious jade is a great tortoise"). The offering of essence uses glutinous millet.

The first mountain of the Fourth Western Range is called Mount Yin. Its heights are rich in grains but devoid of stone, and its grasses are mostly *mao* and *fan* (*mao* is *jikui*; *fan* is green *fan*, resembling sedge but larger; *mao* has two pronunciations). The Yin River flows from here, coursing west into the Luo. Fifty *li* to the north lies Mount Lao, where this grass grows in abundance. It is also called *eryi*, its center dyed purple. The Weak River flows from here, running west into the Luo.

Fifty *li* further west stands Mount Bafu. The Er River (pronounced like "ear") rises here, flowing west into the Luo, and its waters hold much purple jasper. One hundred seventy *li* to the north lies Mount Shen, whose heights abound in *guzuo* grain and whose lower slopes bear many *niu* and *ju* trees. Its sunlit side yields gold and jade. The Qu River flows from here, running east into the Yellow River.

Two hundred *li* to the north lies Mount Niao. Its heights are thick with mulberry trees, its lower slopes with paper mulberries, its shaded side with iron, and its sunlit side with jade. The Ru River flows from here, running east into the Yellow River.

One hundred twenty *li* further north stands Mount Shangshen. No grasses or trees grow on its heights, but many *luo* stones lie there (*luo*, pronounced like "stone," refers to large rocks). Its lower slopes bear many hazel and *hu* trees (hazelnuts resemble chestnuts but are smaller and sweet; *hu* wood is used for arrows; the *Book of Songs* says: "Hazel and *hu* grow thickly"; *hu* has two pronunciations). Its beasts are mostly white deer, and its birds mostly *danghu* (sometimes written *hu*). Shaped like pheasants, they fly by means of their throat whiskers (the whiskers beneath the throat). Eating them prevents dizziness.

The Tang River flows forth from here, running east into the Yellow River.

One hundred eighty *li* further north stands Mount Zhuci. The Zhuci River flows from this mountain, running east into the Yellow River. This mountain abounds in trees but has no grasses; neither birds nor beasts dwell here, though it teems with serpents.

One hundred eighty *li* further north stands Mount Hao. Its trees are mostly lacquer trees (the lacquer tree resembles the *chun* tree). Its grasses include many medicinal herbs: *yao*, *xi*, and *xiongqiong*. *Yao* is another name for *baizhi*. *Xi* is a fragrant grass. *Xiongqiong* is also called *jiangli* (pronounced *wu jiao* in reverse). It also abounds in *ling* stones (the character *ling* may also be pronounced *jin*, though this is uncertain). The Duan River flows from here, running east into the Yellow River.

Two hundred twenty *li* further north stands Mount Yu (pronounced *yu*). Its northern slopes bear much iron, and its southern slopes much copper. Its beasts are mostly white wolves and white tigers. The *Waizhuan* states: "When King Mu of Zhou campaigned against the Quanrong, he obtained four white wolves and white tigers." The tiger is named *biao* (the second character is unclear). Its birds are mostly white pheasants and white *di* (sometimes written as white *cui*). The Sheng River flows from here, running east into the Yellow River.

Two hundred fifty *li* to the west stands Mount Baiyu. Its heights bear many pines and cypresses, while its lower slopes abound in oak and sandalwood (oak here refers to *zuo*). Its beasts are mostly *xi* cattle and *qian* sheep. Its birds are mostly owls (the owl resembles the turtledove but is blue-green). The Luo River flows from its southern slopes, running east into the Wei River; the Jia River flows from its northern slopes, running east into the Sheng River.

Three hundred *li* to the northwest stands Mount Shenshou. No grasses or trees grow here, and snow covers it in both winter and summer. The Shen River flows from its heights, sinking beneath its lower slopes. This place abounds in white jade.

Fifty-five *li* further west stands Mount Jinggu (some omit the characters "Mount" and "gu"). The Jing River flows from here (some identify this as the present-day Jing River, though this is uncertain), running southeast into the Wei River. This place abounds in white gold and white jade.

One hundred twenty *li* further west stands Mount Gang. It abounds in firewood trees and *fufu* jade. The Gang River flows from here, running north into the Wei River. This place teems with divine *chi* (*chi* are a kind of *chimei*; pronounced *chi hui you*, or sometimes written with another character). They have human faces and beastly bodies, one foot and one hand, and their cries sound like *qin* (*qin* is a loan character for *yin*).

Two hundred *li* further west, at the tail of Mount Gang, the Luo River flows forth, running north into the Yellow River. Within it dwell many *manman*, creatures with the bodies of rats and the heads of turtles, whose cries sound like barking dogs.

Three hundred fifty *li* further west stands Mount Yingdi. Its heights abound in lacquer trees, and its lower slopes in gold and jade. Its birds and beasts are entirely white. The Wo River flows from here (the character *wo* may also be written with another character, pronounced like *yuan* in *yuanwang*), running north into the Lingyang Marsh. This place abounds in *ranyi* fish, creatures with the bodies of fish and the heads of serpents, six feet, and eyes like horse ears. Eating them prevents sleep and wards off misfortune.

Three hundred *li* further west stands Mount Zhongqu. Its southern slopes abound in jade, while its northern slopes bear much realgar, white jade, and gold. A beast dwells here, resembling a horse with a white body and black tail, one horn, tiger’s teeth and claws, and a cry like drumbeats. It is called the *bo* and devours tigers and leopards. The *Erya* describes the *bo* without mentioning its horn or tiger claws, though it appears in the *Changshou* illustrations. It can ward off weapons—raising it repels blades.

There grows a tree here, resembling the crabapple in form, with round leaves and red fruit. Its fruit is as large as a quince—*quince* like the character *gua* (瓜). It is called the *xiang* tree, pronounced like *huai*. Eating it grants great strength. The *Shizi* states: "Those who eat of trees often become benevolent, named as if by water. This is of that kind."

Two hundred and sixty *li* further west stands Mount Bang, pronounced like *gui*. At its summit dwells a beast resembling an ox, covered in hedgehog-like quills, called the *qiongqi*. It is like a mastiff and devours humans. Some say it resembles a tiger, with quills and wings. The *Ming* records: "The beast *qiongqi*, its form most hideous, chases away evil spirits, causing all to flee in terror. Thus it is also named the divine hound."

The Meng River, pronounced *meng*, rises here, flowing south into the Yang River. Within its waters abound yellow cowries—cowries being shelled creatures with bodies like tadpoles, possessing only heads and tails. There is also the *ying* fish, pronounced like *luo*, with a fish’s body and crow’s wings, its cry like that of mandarin ducks. When it appears, the region suffers great floods.

Two hundred and twenty *li* further west lies the mountain known as Bird-and-Mouse-Same-Burrow. Today it stands southwest of Shouyang County in Longxi. The mountain harbors birds and mice that share a burrow: the bird is called *□*, the mouse *□*. The mouse resembles a house mouse but with a short tail; the bird is like a swallow but yellow in color. They tunnel several *chi* into the earth, the mouse dwelling within, the bird without, yet living together. The *Kongshi Shangshu Commentary* states they mate as male and female. The *Zhangshi Dili Ji* claims they do not. Its summit abounds in white tigers and white jade.

The Wei River rises here, flowing east into the Yellow River. It emerges from the mountain’s east, reaching Hongnong’s Huayin County before entering the river. Within its waters dwell many *sao* fish, pronounced like *sao*, resembling the *zu* fish (a large fish with its mouth beneath its chin and a body covered in connected scales). Some write it as *nianli*. When they stir, the region faces great warfare. (Some texts omit the phrase "when they stir" and what follows.)

The Lan River, pronounced *kan*, rises to its west, flowing west into the Han River. It teems with *□□* fish, pronounced like *piliang*, resembling an overturned *diao* (a cooking vessel), with a crow’s head, fish’s wings, and fish’s tail, its cry like the sound of a chime stone. They produce pearls and jade—akin to pearl-oysters, yet capable of birthing them.

Three hundred and sixty *li* southwest stands Mount Yanzi, the mountain where the sun sets, as mentioned in the *Li Sao*. Pronounced *yanzī* (two readings). Its summit abounds in cinnabar trees, their leaves like millet, their fruit as large as melons, red in texture with black veins. Eating them cures malaria and can ward off fire. Its southern slopes teem with tortoises; its northern slopes, with jade. The *Tiao* River (or *Ruo* River, as some write it) rises here, flowing west into the sea. The *Yu Da Zhuan* states: "The Weipan River rises from Mount Yanzi." Within its waters abound whetstones—fine ones called *di*, coarse ones *li*.

A beast dwells here, resembling a horse in body but with crow’s wings, a human face, and a serpent’s tail. It delights in lifting people, often embracing and raising them. It is called *shuhu*. A bird also dwells here, resembling an owl but with a human face, a *wei*-monkey’s body (the *wei* being a kind of macaque, pronounced like *zengyi* or *zhu*), and a dog’s tail (or back, as some texts have it). Its name is its own cry. Some write it as *she*, meaning it also calls thus—this may be a textual error. When it appears, the region suffers severe drought.

In all, the fourth western section, from Mount Yin to Mount Yanzi, comprises nineteen mountains spanning three thousand six hundred and eighty *li*. For their sacrifices, one white chicken is used in prayer, with glutinous rice as the offering, and white *jian* grass as the mat.

In total, the Western Mountains record seventy-seven mountains, spanning seventeen thousand five hundred and ten *li.

*Western Mountains Classic: Illustrations and Eulogies*
*Xian Yang*

The Moon-Race Sheep
A breed most wild, the Moon-Race sheep stands—
six feet in height, its tail as red as a horse’s.
How know this true? The *Er Ya* records the course.

Mount Taihua
The Hua Peak, numinous and sheer,
carved square by Heaven’s own hand.
Here dwells a goddess, who ladles jade nectar.
Who shall ascend to her? One whose dragon-chariot wears cloud-robes.

The Fei-Yi Serpent
The Fei-Yi, a creature bound to calamity,
beats its wings on Yang Mountain, a sign of drought’s fury.
Yet once prayers rise from the mulberry groves,
it vanishes in an instant, gone without trace.

□-Qu, Red Turtle, Striped-Stem, *Wen*-Fruit, *Min* Bird
When the □-Qu brings woe, the red turtle shuns fire;
the striped-stem cures deafness—this is a fine fruit.
The *Min* bird wards off disaster, its form small and slight.

Flowing Ochre
Where sand lies hidden in streams, ochre flows unseen.
Dig for iron beneath, and there it shall be found.
To purge the *Juan*-ox’s plague, gather it for the altar rites.

The Bristled Boar
A clan of stiff-bristled beasts, called the bristled boar.
Their fur stands like clustered awls, sharp as arrows shot.
Their bodies bear both sexes, self-sufficient in form.

Yellow-Guan Grass, Fei-Yi Bird, Yao Beast
The bath-illness grass bears seeds of ochre-red.
The Fei-Yi, quail-like, its flesh brings plague.
The Yao beast, long-armed, delights in hurling stones.

The *Tuo*-□ Bird
A bird with a human face, standing on one leg alone.
Its nature runs counter to the seasons—
it emerges in winter, hibernates in summer.
Clad in its feathers, even swift thunder cannot strike.

Peach-Branch Bamboo
From Mount Pan’s fair groves, the peach-branch bamboo grows.
In dense thickets, deep and misty, it sways with graceful ease.
Woven into mats for peaceful rest,
carved into staffs to steady the frail.

*Du Heng*
The *Xing-Xing* flees from men, the *Du Heng* outruns horses.
Principle must follow its course, the body too may borrow aid.
A steed’s speed lies in its spirit—what need for a driver’s hand?

*Gu Rong* Tree, Riverside Beast, Oak Bird
A tree with blossoms but no fruit—the *Jing Rong*’s form.
The riverside beast resembles a dog, its hide repels dark sorcery.
Black-streaked with a red crown, the oak bird cures hidden hemorrhoids.

The Parrot, Wise Bird
The parrot, a wise bird, with azure plumes and crimson beak.

Praise-Stone
Though their natures differ, their principles intertwine in subtle ways.
The praise-stone slays mice, growing fat as silkworms feed.
Though their essences oppose, they converge in one return.

The *Jue-Ru* Beast
The *Jue-Ru* beast, deer-shaped with four horns,
hooves like a horse’s, hands like a man’s, its tail pure white.
Its form blends three shapes, scaling cliffs and climbing trees.

The Parrot
The parrot, a wise bird, perches in forests, pecking at mulberries.
Its four toes split at the center, walking on its beak.
It weaves its own cage, trapped in stillness, toes curled in.

The *Shu-Si* Bird, *□* Beast, *Lei* Bird
The *Shu-Si* has human feet, its form like an owl.
The *□* beast has large eyes; the *Lei* bird is its name.
Two heads, four feet, soaring as if paired in flight.

The Luan Bird
The Luan soars above the Woman’s Bed, the phoenix emerges from the Cinnabar Cave.
Flapping wings in harmony, they answer the sage’s call.
Striking stone, their songs fade—
the *Shao* music reaches its end.

The *Liang-Huan* Bird, Zhu-Yan Beast
The *Fu-Xi* and Zhu-Yan—
their sight foretells war.
Though their forms differ, their omens align,
a principle that does not act in vain.
Trace it to nature’s course—
its patterns are hard to fathom.

The *Man-Man* Bird
A paired-winged bird, like a teal, blue and red.
Though said to share one form, their qi divides their bodies.
Stretching necks apart like teals, they turn and fly, wings joined.

Cinnabar Tree, Jade Sap
The cinnabar tree blazes bright, its jade sap bubbling.
The Yellow Emperor wore it, then ascended on dragon’s might.
Soaring into the distant heights, the multitudes below wailed in vain.

Jade of Excellence
The treasure of Mount Zhong, its jade shines with radiant hues.
Its colors flow like light, its qi like rainbow clouds.
The noble wear it, its virtue warding off all ill.

The Sons of Mount Zhong: *Gu* and *Qin-□*
*Qin-□* and *Gu*—they slew Zu Jiang.
The Emperor executed them, east of Kunlun.
Both transformed, their wings arched alike.

The *Yao* Fish
Where it appears, the land prospers—its name is *Yao*.
It traverses the two seas, its wings gliding through the skies.
Its flavor is rare, praised in every cup.

Divine Ying Zhao
On Mount Huai Jiang, Ying Zhao presides.
Patrolling the four seas, it soars with cloud-dancing wings.
This is the Emperor’s park—some call it the Dark Garden.

The *Yao* Tree
The *Yao*, a sacred tree, grows beside the *Ruo* wood.
Layered roots lift its branches, its radiance shines afar.
Those who eat it transform in spirit,
their names recorded among the immortals.

Kunlun Mound
Kunlun, moon’s essence, the spirit-store of waters.
Here the Emperor descends, the Western Elder’s abode.
Towering at the center, it is called the Pillar of Heaven.

Divine Lu Wu
Lu Wu grasped the One, dwelling on Kunlun.
Facing the Luminous One, he guards the Emperor’s gate.
Breathing in the numinous qi, his spirit blazes like a bear’s.

The *Tu-Lou* Beast, *Qin-Yuan* Bird
The *Tu-Lou* devours men, four-horned like a sheep.
The *Qin-Yuan* resembles a bee, large as a mandarin duck.
Touching anything, it brings death—its sting is hard to withstand.

The *Sha-Tang* Tree

The *Sha-Tang* Tree
How might one obtain the *sha-tang* to refine the Dragon Elixir?
Sailing the vast sea, drifting in distant roaming,
carefree and unbound, riding the waves at will.

The Quail Bird and *Sha-Tang* Fruit, the □ Grass
The bird that serves the Emperor in all matters is called the quail.
The fruit of the *sha-tang* is prized above all others.
There grows a wondrous herb, named □.

Divine Changcheng
The qi of the Nine Virtues gives birth to Changcheng.
His form is human with a *you* beast’s tail, his spirit ever steadfast.
Mysterious beings conceal themselves—none in the world can name them.

Queen Mother of the West
Daughter of the Heavenly Emperor, hair unbound and visage like a tiger’s.
King Mu presented gifts, exchanging poems in joy.
Matters beyond the verse defy full telling.

Jishi Mountain
Within Jishi’s peaks, the twin rivers surge forth.
The Xia sovereign guided them, stone gates gushing waves.
Here rare treasures abound, rivaling the wonders of Kun’e.

White Emperor Shaohao
Shaohao the Emperor, styled the Golden Heaven.
The palace of the Kui clan also stands upon this mountain.
He governs the sun’s descent, its light ever round.

The *Zheng* Beast
On Zhang’e Mountain, strange creatures dwell.
A beast like a leopard, its color crimson.
Five tails and one horn, its cry like striking stone.

The *Bifang* Bird
The *Bifang* bears vermilion markings, radiant with the essence of fire.
In drought it soars high, flapping its wings in the sun’s glare.
Where it alights, disaster flows—its fire is not of the proper flame.

Patterned Cowrie
The ancients first wrought them, using tortoise and cowrie as currency.
Cowries valued for their patterns, priced by their size.
Simple to follow, transgressions do not exceed them.

Heavenly Hound
Though hemp does not grow tall, the Heavenly Hound is not large.
Though small in form, it wards off calamity and dispels harm.
The dominance of qi lies in consuming its belt.

Three Azure Birds
The mountain named Three Perils, where the azure birds alight.
They come and go from Kunlun, servants to the Queen Mother.
King Mu’s western campaign turned his chariot upon this land.

The *Jiang-Yi* Beast and □ Bird
Where the *Jiang-Yi* dwells, wind and clouds gather.
A beast named *Ao-□*, its fur like a tattered cloak.
A bird with one head, yet its body is doubled.

Divine Qitong
Son of Zhuanxu, he succeeded as Fire Regulator.
His voice rings like clashing bells and chimes.
Dwelling on Gui Mountain, his numinous power is supreme.

Emperor Jiang
In form, a chaos; in spirit, all-pervading.
Naturally radiant, hearing not through ears.
Forced to name it, it is called Emperor Jiang.

The *Huan* Beast and *Yiyu* Bird
The *Yiyu* has three heads, the *Huan* beast three tails.
Both repel misfortune, dispelling evil and averting blindness.
The noble who wears them meets no wrong or transgression.

The *Danghu* Bird
Birds fly with wings, but the *Danghu* requires them.
Though much is abandoned and little retained, it abounds in surplus.
The wheel turns at the hub—its utility lies in emptiness.

The White Wolf
Vigilant and proud, the white wolf roams where the Dao prevails.
Responding to omens, it transforms, bearing a sacred hook.
It follows virtue alone, appearing in Yin to be seen in Zhou.

The White Tiger
The tiger of *Gan-□*, fierce yet benevolent.
Its form is pure white, its patterns resplendent.
Responding to virtue, it is tamed, halting at our borders.

The *Bo* Horse
The *Bo* resembles a horse, truly the finest of beasts.
With flowing mane and raised head, its neigh shakes the heavens like thunder.
Its qi knows no restraint, devouring tigers and repelling weapons.

The *Man* Beast and *Ran-Yi* Fish
Its cry like a chant, one foot and a human face.
Body of a rat, head of a turtle, it is called the *Man*.
Eyes like a horse’s ears, its glut feeds on strange transformations.

The *Xiang* Tree
The *Xiang* tree’s form resembles the *lian*.
If one can consume it long, uprooting trees and moving mountains.
Strength it grants, and longevity as well.

Mount Bird-and-Mouse-Same-Den
□□ two creatures, different kinds yet sharing a home.
Gathering without regard to place, some run, some fly.
What should not be is—hard to reason through.

The *□-Pi* Fish

The *Fu-Diao*-Shaped Creature
Its form like an inverted cauldron, it cradles jade and holds pearls.
Though it possesses, it does not hoard—releasing all through its tail’s gate.
When it aligns with the Dao, it may be called a wondrous fish.

The *Dan* Tree
There is the *Dan* tree, growing on the rugged *Xiao-Pan*.
Its fruit like melons, its taste sweet and tart.
It dispels illness and repels fire, a marvel alongside cinnamon and orchid.

The *Qiong-Qi* Beast and *Luo* Fish; the *Shu-Hu* Beast
The *Qiong-Qi* resembles an ox, its bristles like a hedgehog’s.
The *Luo* of *Meng* waters—neither fish nor bird.
The beast of *Shu-Hu* embraces any human it encounters.

The *Sao* Fish
Things respond to resonance, and some move by fate’s decree.
A warrior brandishes his sword, his qi stirring a white rainbow.
The *Sao* fish lurks in the deep—when it emerges, the city trembles.

*Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Two* concludes

[Lacunae as marked in the original text]
*Mountains and Seas, Scroll Three*
*Guo Pu’s Commentary*

*The Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Three*
*Guo Pu’s Commentary*

*The Northern Mountains: First Section*
The first mountain of the *Northern Mountains* is called *Shan-Hu*. It abounds in *Ji* trees—*Tao* wood resembling elm, which may be burned to fertilize rice fields. It comes from *Shu*. Its name is pronounced *Ji*. Upon its heights grow many flowering plants. The *Feng* River rises here (pronounced *Feng*) and flows westward into the *You* River, where many *Bi* stones and patterned stones are found.

Two hundred and fifty *li* farther north lies *Qiu-Ru* Mountain. Its summit holds much copper; its base, much jade. No grasses or trees grow there. The *Hua* River (here called the *Xu* River) issues forth and flows westward into the *Zhu-Pi* River, which rises in *Zhu-Pi* Mountain. Within its waters dwell many *Hua* fish (here called *□* fish). Their form resembles the eel, with red backs. The *□* fish resembles the serpent (pronounced *Shan*). Their cries are like the sound of *wu* (as in the phrase *wu-zi wu*, the call of one man to another). Eating them cures warts (warts being growths). The river also teems with *water horses*: their shape is that of a horse, with striped forelegs and an ox’s tail (forelegs being the front limbs). As *The Rites of Zhou* states: “A horse with a black back and mottled forelegs is called *lü*.” In the fourth year of *Yuan-Shou* during the reign of Emperor Wu of Han, horses emerged from the *Wo-Jia* waters of *Dun-Huang* as a numinous portent—these were of the same kind. Their cries resemble human shouts.

To the north by three hundred li lies *Dai* Mountain. Its heights abound in jade, its depths in azure-green *bi* stone. There dwells a beast resembling a horse, bearing a single horn with serrated edges—its horn is armored with scales. Some write it as *li*. Its name is *□shu* (pronounced *huan*), and it can repel fire. There is also a bird resembling the crow, adorned with five colors and crimson markings, called the *qi□*. This bird has already been mentioned above; it may share the same name. It reproduces without male or female, and eating it prevents jaundice—jaundice being the disease of *yongju* (boils and abscesses).

The *Peng* River rises here, flowing westward into the waters of *Bi* Lake. Within it swim many *tiao* fish (pronounced *you*), shaped like chickens with crimson plumage, three tails, six feet, and four heads. Their cries resemble the magpie’s, and eating them dispels sorrow.

To the north by four hundred li stands *Qiao-Ming* Mountain. The *Qiao* River issues forth, flowing westward into the Yellow River. Within it teem many *He-Luo* fish, possessing one head and ten bodies. Their cries resemble a barking dog, and eating them cures boils. There is also a beast resembling the *huan* (badger) but with crimson bristles—the *huan* being the porcupine (pronounced *wan*). Its cries are like *liuliu*, and it is called the *Meng-Huai*. It can ward off misfortune—misfortune being malevolent *qi*—and is also depicted in talismanic paintings for protection.

This mountain bears no grass or trees, but abounds in green *xiong-huang* (realgar). Some texts say it abounds in azure-green *bi* stone.

To the north by three hundred and fifty li lies *Zhuo-Guang* Mountain. The *Xiao* River rises here, flowing westward into the Yellow River. Within it swim many *xi-xi* fish (pronounced like the second syllable of *kuazhe*), shaped like magpies but with ten wings, their scales all at the tips of their feathers. Their cries resemble the magpie’s, and they can repel the two fires. Eating them prevents *dan* (feverish illness).

Its heights are rich in pine and cypress, its depths in *□jiang* (a type of oak). Its beasts are mostly antelope, its birds mostly *fan* (unverified; some say it is the owl, pronounced *fan*).

To the north by three hundred and eighty li lies *Guo* Mountain. Its heights abound in lacquer trees, its depths in tung and *ju* trees—the *tongwu* being the tung tree, and *ju* being the *kui* wood, used for staffs with swollen nodes (pronounced *qu*). Its southern slopes are rich in jade, its northern slopes in iron.

The *Yi* River rises here, flowing westward into the Yellow River. Its beasts are mostly *tuo-tuo* (camels), which bear fleshy humps and traverse flowing sands with ease, covering three hundred li in a day. They can carry a thousand *jin* and know the locations of springs and water sources. Its birds are mostly *yu*, shaped like rats but with avian wings. Their cries resemble a goat’s, and they can repel weapons.

To the north by four hundred li lies the tail of *Guo* Mountain. Its heights abound in jade and bear no stone. The *Yu* River rises here, flowing westward into the Yellow River, and within it swim many patterned cowries.

To the north by two hundred li lies *Dan-Xun* Mountain. Its heights abound in *chu* (ailanthus) and cypress, its grasses in wild leeks and shallots—all mountain vegetables, as recorded in *The Erya*. It is rich in cinnabar and *huo* (vermilion pigment).

The *Xun* River rises here, flowing westward into the *Tang* River. There dwells a beast resembling a rat, with the head of a hare and the body of a *mi* (elk). Its cries resemble a *hao* dog’s (pronounced *hao*), and it flies by means of its tail—some texts say by its whiskers. It is called the *ear-mouse*, and eating it prevents *□* (distended abdomen, as seen in *Pi-Cang*, pronounced *cai*). It can also repel all poisons.

Further north by two hundred and eighty *li* lies *Shizhe* Mountain. Its heights bear no grasses or trees, but abound in fine jade. The *Ci* River rises here, flowing westward into the Yellow River. There dwells a beast resembling a leopard, its forehead patterned and body white—*ti* (forehead) refers to the brow. It is called the *mengji*, swift in running and skilled in crouching; its cry is its own name.

Further north by one hundred and ten *li* lies *Bianchun* Mountain—alternatively called *Chun* Mountain. It abounds in scallions, mallows, leeks, and mountain scallions (*ge*, broad-leaved); peaches and plums (the mountain peach, *chitao*, bears small fruit with inseparable pits). The *Gang* River rises here, flowing westward into *You* Marsh. There dwells a beast resembling a *yu* (monkey), its body patterned and fond of laughter; upon seeing humans, it feigns sleep—*yanmian* (feigning slumber). The waters here are called *Youyan*—alternatively *Yan*, pronounced *e*. Its cry is its own name.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies *Manlian* Mountain—*Manlian* pronounced as two syllables. Its heights bear no grasses or trees. There dwells a beast resembling a *yu*, with the mane of an ox and the tail of a bull, its arms patterned and hooves equine. Upon seeing humans, it calls out; it is named *Zuzhui*, and its cry is its own name. There also dwells a bird resembling a female pheasant but with a human face; upon seeing humans, it leaps—named *Songsi*, its cry is its own name. The *Jinhan* River rises here, flowing westward into *You* Marsh, and its waters abound in lodestone, which can attract iron. *Cishi* (lodestone)—as *Guanzi* states: "Where lodestone lies above, copper lies below; thus may it be retrieved."

Further north by one hundred and eighty *li* lies *Danzhang* Mountain. Its heights bear no grasses or trees. There dwells a beast resembling a leopard, with a long tail, human head, oxen ears, and a single eye—named *Zhujian*, pronounced like *jian* in *jianniu* (castrated ox). It roars fiercely, walking with its tail in its mouth and resting with its tail coiled. There also dwells a bird resembling a pheasant but with a patterned head, white wings, and yellow feet—named *Baiye*, pronounced *ye*. Eating it cures throat pain—*ai* (throat), as in the *Guliang Commentary*: "The throat admits no grain." The people of Wu call the throat *ai*, pronounced *ai*. It can also cure *chi* (epilepsy)—*chi* refers to convulsive illness. The *Li* River rises here, flowing southward into the *Gang* River.

Further north by three hundred and twenty *li* lies *Guanti* Mountain. Its heights abound in *chu* (ailanthus) and *zhe* (cudrania), its lower slopes in quicksand and whetstones. There dwells a beast resembling an ox but with a white tail; its cry resembles a human call—*tao* pronounced *jiao*. It is named *Nafu*. There also dwells a bird resembling a female pheasant but with a human face; upon seeing humans, it leaps—named *Songsi*, its cry is its own name. The *Jianghan* River rises here, flowing westward into *You* Marsh, and its waters abound in lodestone, which can attract iron. As *Guanzi* states: "Where a mountain bears lodestone above, copper must lie below." *Cishi* pronounced *ci*.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies *Panhou* Mountain. Its heights abound in pines and cypresses, its lower slopes in hazels and *hu* (lespedeza); its southern face is rich in jade, its northern in iron. There dwells a beast resembling an ox but with hair growing from its joints—named *maoniu* (yak). Today, the yak’s back, knees, and dewlap all bear long hair. The *Bian* River rises here, flowing southward into *Li* Marsh.

Further north by two hundred and thirty *li* lies *Xiaoxian* Mountain, barren of grasses or trees, where snow falls in both winter and summer. Further north by two hundred and eighty *li* lies *Daxian* Mountain, also barren of grasses or trees, though its lower slopes abound in jade. This mountain cannot be ascended from any side. There dwells a serpent named *changshe* (long serpent), its bristles resembling those of a boar. Some say it measures a hundred *xun* in length. The present-day *huangshe* (pit viper) is patterned like green silk cords, with bristles like a boar’s mane—this is its kind. *Changshan* also bears long serpents, though their forms differ. Its cry resembles the sound of a wooden clapper—like the *tuo* (night watchman’s rattle) sounded by travelers at night, pronounced *tuo*.

Further north by three hundred and twenty *li* lies *Dunhong* Mountain. Its heights abound in *□nan* (nanmu), its lower slopes in *zicao* (gromwell). The *Dunhong* River rises here, flowing westward into *You* Marsh, issuing from the northeastern corner of Kunlun—indeed the source of the Yellow River. This is where the Yellow River emerges from the Kunlun wastes. Its waters abound in *chigui* (red salmon)—today called *houyi*, with *gui* pronounced *gui*. Its beasts include *si* (rhinoceros) and *maoniu* (yak)—alternatively *puniu* (wild yak), as seen in *Li Sao*’s *Heavenly Questions*, though the term remains obscure. Its birds include many *shijiu* (turtledoves).

Further north by two hundred *li* lies *Shaoxian* Mountain, barren of grasses or trees but rich in malachite. There dwells a beast resembling an ox but with a red body, human face, and equine hooves—named *Yayu*. As *Erya* states: "*Yayu* resembles a *□*, with tiger claws," though this differs from the present account. Its cry resembles an infant’s; it devours humans. The *Dun* River rises here, flowing eastward into the *Yanmen* River, which issues from the *Yanmen* Mountains. Its waters abound in *□□* fish—*pei* unclear, possibly *pu* (sturgeon). Eating it kills humans.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies *Yufa* Mountain. The *Huaize* River rises here, pronounced *huai*, flowing northeastward into *Taize* Marsh. Its waters abound in *saoyu* (three-striped fish)—*yu* pronounced *zao*. It resembles a carp but with avian feet; eating it cures warts. There dwells a beast resembling a dog but with a human face, skilled at throwing; upon seeing humans, it laughs—named *Shanpei*, pronounced *hui*. It moves like the wind, swift as speech. Where it appears, great winds arise across the land.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies the Northern Marchmount. Its slopes abound in *gen* (knotweed), *ji* (thorns), and hardwoods—such as *tan* (sandalwood) and *zhe* (cudrania). There dwells a beast resembling an ox but with four horns, human eyes, and porcine ears—named *Zhuhuai*. Its cry resembles a honking goose; it devours humans. The *Zhuhuai* River rises here, flowing westward into the *Xiao* River.

Within its waters abound *yi* fish—*yi* pronounced *yi*. Their bodies resemble fish but with canine heads; their cries are like those of infants. In present seas there exist tiger-deer fish and sea boars, their bodies likewise piscine but heads akin to tigers, deer, or swine—these belong to the same kind. Eating them cures madness.

Further north by one hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Hunxi. It bears no grasses or trees, but abounds in copper and jade. The *Xiao* River rises here, flowing northwestward into the sea. There exists a serpent with one head, two bodies, and four feet—named *Fei*□. Where it appears, the land suffers great drought. *Guanzi* states: "The essence of dried waters is named □, possessing one head and two bodies. Its form resembles a serpent, eight *chi* in length. Calling it by its name allows one to command it to fetch fish and soft-shelled turtles. This, too, belongs to the same kind."

Further north by fifty *li* lies Mount Beidan. It bears no grasses or trees, but abounds in scallions and leeks.

Further north by one hundred *li* lies Mount Xiongchai. It bears no grasses or trees, but abounds in horses—wild horses, resembling steeds but smaller.

Further north by one hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Beixian. This mountain abounds in horses. The *Xian* River rises here, flowing northwestward into the *Tuwu* River. In the second year of *Yuanshou* during the Han, horses emerged from the *Xuwu* waters.

Further north by one hundred and seventy *li* lies Mount Di. Some texts write it as □—an archaic character. It abounds in horses.

Here follows the translation:

There is a beast here, its form like a leopard but with patterned markings on its head, called the *yao*. Its cry sounds like "me." The *Di* River rises here, flowing eastward into the *Tai* Marsh, wherein dwell many dragon-turtles.

In all, from the beginning of the *Classic of Northern Mountains*—from Mount Danhu to Mount Di—there are twenty-five mountains spanning five thousand four hundred and ninety *li*. Their spirits all have human faces and serpentine bodies. In their sacrifices, the ritual offering consists of a single male chicken, buried in disarray, and an auspicious jade tablet, buried without grain. This means that in the rites, no rice is used; all sacrificial animals and jade are interred.

North of these mountains, the people all eat raw, uncooked food. Some texts read: "all eat raw food without fire."

The beginning of the *Second Northern Classic* lies east of the Yellow River. Its head rests against the Fen, overlooking the Fen River. It is called Mount Guancen, now located north of the former Fenyang County in Taiyuan Commandery, on Xiurong Mountain. The character *cen* is pronounced like "cen." Its summit bears no trees but abounds in grasses; its base holds much jade. The Fen River rises here, flowing westward into the Yellow River, entering it west of Fenyang County to the north.

Further north by two hundred and fifty *li* lies Mount Shaoyang. Its summit abounds in jade, its base in red silver—the essence of silver. The *Suan* River rises here, flowing eastward into the Fen River, wherein lies much fine ochre. As the *Guanzi* states: "Where a mountain’s summit bears ochre, its base contains iron."

Further north by fifty *li* lies Mount Xianyong, now located west of Jinyang County, called Jiweng. The character *yong* is pronounced like "weng." Its summit abounds in jade, its base in copper. Its beasts are mostly *lümi*. The *lü* is the *yu*, resembling a donkey but with cloven hooves and horns like a gazelle; it is also called the mountain donkey. The *Book of Zhou* states: "The Northern Tang offers *lü*." It is also mentioned in the *Rites of Village Drinking*. Its birds are mostly white pheasants and white *yu*—that is, the white *yuan*. The character *yu* is pronounced "yuliureverse." The Jin River rises here, flowing southeast into the Fen River, passing south of Jinyang and then eastward into the Fen. Within it dwell many *ci* fish, their form like the *shu* but with red scales. Small fish are called *tiao*. Their cry is like a rebuke; eating them prevents arrogance. Some texts write "sao," meaning a foul odor.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies Mount Huqi. It bears no grasses or trees but abounds in green jasper. The *Sheng* River rises here, flowing northeast into the Fen River, wherein lies much dark jade.

Further north by three hundred and fifty *li* lies Mount Baisha. It spans three hundred *li* in breadth and circumference, entirely of sand. It bears no grasses, trees, birds, or beasts. The *Wei* River rises upon its summit, plunging beneath its base, emerging from the mountain’s peak and pooling at its foot. Here lies much white jade.

Further north by forty *li* lies Mount Ershi. It bears no grasses or trees, nor any water.

Further north by three hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Kuang, which bears no grasses or trees. This mountain is covered in snow throughout winter and summer. The *Kuang* River rises here, flowing westward into the *Fu* River, wherein lies much fine jade.

Further north by three hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Zhuyu. Its summit abounds in copper and jade, its base in pine and cypress. The *Zhuyu* River rises here, flowing eastward into the *Mao* River.

Further north by three hundred and fifty *li* lies Mount Duntou. Its summit abounds in gold and jade but bears no grasses or trees. The *Mao* River rises here, flowing eastward into the *Qiong* Marsh, wherein dwell many *bo* horses. The character *bo* is pronounced like "bo." They have ox tails and white bodies, a single horn, and their cry is like a shout.

Further north by three hundred and fifty *li* lies Mount Gouwu. Its summit abounds in jade, its base in copper. There is a beast here, its body like a sheep’s but with a human face. Its eyes lie beneath its armpits; it has tiger’s teeth and human claws. Its cry is like an infant’s; it is called the *paoxiao* and devours humans. As a creature, it is insatiably greedy, and if it does not finish eating a person, it turns to harm its own body. Its image appears on Xia dynasty tripods. This is the *taotie* mentioned in the *Zuo Tradition*. The character *pao* is pronounced like "pao."

Further north by three hundred *li* lies Mount Beixiao, which bears no stone. Its southern slopes abound in green jasper, its northern slopes in jade. There is a beast here, its form like a tiger but with a white body, a dog’s head, a horse’s tail, and a pig’s mane; it is called the *duyu*. The character *yu* is pronounced like "gu." There is also a bird here, its form like an owl but with a human face; it is called the *banmao*. The characters *banmao* are pronounced like "ban" and "mao," or sometimes written as *xia*. It flies by night and hides by day, belonging to the same kind as the *jiaoliao*. Eating it cures heatstroke. The character for heatstroke is pronounced like "ye." The *Cen* River rises here, flowing eastward into the *Kou* Marsh.

Further north by three hundred and fifty *li* lies Mount Liangqu. It bears no grasses or trees but abounds in gold and jade. The *Xiu* River rises here, flowing eastward into the *Choumen*, the name of a river. Its beasts are mostly *juji*, their form like the *hui* but with red fur. The *hui* resembles a rat, its red fur like bristles. The characters *wei* and *hui* are pronounced like "wei." Their cry is like a piglet’s. There is also a bird here, its form like *Kuafu*—some texts write *Jufu*—with four wings, one eye, and a dog’s tail. It is called the *xiao*, its cry like a magpie’s. Eating it cures abdominal pain and can halt diarrhea. The character for diarrhea is pronounced like "dong."

Further north by four hundred *li* lies Mount Guguan, which bears no grasses or trees. This mountain is covered in snow throughout winter and summer.

Further north by three hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Huguan. Its southern slopes abound in jade, its northern slopes in green jasper and many horses. The *Huguan* River rises here, flowing eastward into the sea. Within it dwell many *shan*—also the character for eel. There is a tree here, its leaves like a willow’s but with red veins.

Further north, traveling five hundred *li* by water and three hundred *li* through flowing sands, one reaches Mount Huan. Its summit abounds in gold and jade. Three mulberry trees grow here, their trunks branchless, towering a hundred *ren* in height, and a hundred fruit trees grow beneath them. Its base abounds in strange serpents.

Further north by three hundred *li* lies Mount Dunti, which bears no grasses or trees but abounds in gold and jade.

To the north of this lies the Northern Sea.

In total, the first section of the *Second Northern Classic* spans from Mount Guancen to Mount Dunti, comprising seventeen mountains across five thousand six hundred and ninety *li*. Their spirits all bear the form of serpents with human faces. For their sacrifices: the offering consists of one rooster and one pig, placed upon a mat and buried. One *bi* jade disk and one *gui* scepter are cast without offering of grain. Jade is cast into the mountains to honor the spirits, without burial.

The first mountain of the *Third Northern Classic* is called Mount Taihang, now located northwest of Yewang County in Henan. *Taihang* is pronounced as *hùgāng*. Its first peak is Mount Gui, where the summit abounds in gold and jade, and the base in green jasper. There is a beast here, its form resembling a gazelle but with four horns, a horse’s tail, and spurs. Its name is *□*, skilled in swift circling—*huanhuan* means whirling dance. *□* is pronounced *huī*. Its cry is self-named. There is also a bird here, its form like a magpie, with a white body, red tail, and six feet. Its name is *□*, pronounced *bēn*. It is easily startled, and its cry is self-named. The people of Wu call shouting *xiao*, pronounced *hūjiāo*.

Further northeast by two hundred *li* lies Mount Longhou. It bears no grasses or trees but abounds in gold and jade. The *Juejue* River rises here—*juejue* is pronounced *jué*—and flows eastward into the Yellow River. Within it dwell many *renyu*, their form like *□* fish but with four feet, their cries like those of infants. See the *Classic of Mountains* for details. Some say the *renyu* is the *ni*, resembling a catfish but with four legs, its voice like a child’s wail. It is now also called *nian*, pronounced *tí*. Eating it prevents dementia.

Further northeast by two hundred *li* lies Mount Macheng. Its summit abounds in patterned stone, its northern slopes in gold and jade. There is a beast here, its form like a white dog but with a black head; when it sees humans, it takes flight—its flesh wings allow it to soar freely. Its name is *tianma*, and its cry is self-named. There is also a bird here, its form like a crow, with a white head, green body, and yellow feet. Its name is *quju*, pronounced *qūjū* or *wū*. Its cry is self-named. Eating it prevents hunger and can cure *yu*—the meaning is unclear. Some say *yu* means error.

Further northeast by seventy *li* lies Mount Xian. Its summit holds jade, its base abounds in copper. It is rich in pines and cypresses, and its grasses are mostly purple madder. The Tiaojian River rises here—*jian* pronounced *jiān*—and flows southwest into the Long Marsh. Within it grows much *qisuān*, which matures once every three years; its nature remains unclear. Eating it cures *lì*—a kind of pestilence.

Further northeast by two hundred *li* lies Mount Tianchi. No grass or trees grow upon it, but it abounds in patterned stone. There is a beast here, its form like a rabbit but with a rat’s head. It flies using its back, its flight enabled by the fur on its back, and when it flies, it tilts upward. Its name is *feishu*, the flying mouse. The Mian River issues forth here, sinking beneath the mountain and pooling at its base. Within it lies much yellow ochre—ochre being a kind of earth.

Further east by three hundred *li* lies Mount Yang. Its summit abounds in jade, its base in gold and copper. There is a beast here, its form like an ox but with a red tail. Its neck bears a fleshy protuberance resembling a *gouqu*—*gouqu* pronounced *qú*—a hooked growth. Its name is *linghu*, and its cry is self-named. Eating it cures madness. There is also a bird here, its form like a female pheasant but adorned with five colors and patterns. It is both male and female unto itself, named *xiangshe*, and its cry is self-named. The Liu River rises here and flows south into the Yellow River. Within it dwells the *chanfu* fish—*chan* pronounced *chǎn*—its form like a crucian carp, with a fish’s head and a tangled body. Eating it cures vomiting.

Further east by three hundred and fifty *li* lies Mount Benwen. Its summit abounds in dark jade, its base in yellow ochre and black stone. Further north by one hundred *li* lies Mount Wangwu, now located north of Dongyuan County in Hedong. The *Book* states: "As far as Wangwu." It is rich in stone, and the *Nian* River—*nian* pronounced *niǎn*—rises here, flowing northwest into the Tai Marsh. The *Geographical Records* note: "Mount Wangwu is where the Shen River originates." *Nian* and *Yan* are phonetically close, likely the same water—*yan* meaning the Ji River.

Further northeast by three hundred *li* lies Mount Jiao. Its summit abounds in jade but has no stone. The Jiao River rises here and flows west into the Yellow River. This river runs dry in winter and flows in summer, truly a dry river. Today, northeast of Wenxi County in Hedong, there is a Dry River Mouth, named for the Dry River Village, where only the old channel remains, with no water left. Within it stand two mountains. These mountains span three hundred paces in breadth and circumference, named Mount Fawan. Their summits hold gold and jade.

Further south by three hundred *li* lies Mount Jing. [...] Gazing south, one sees the Salt Dealer’s Marsh, which is the Salt Lake. [...] Yi County, or [...] Gazing north, one sees the Lesser Marsh. Its summit abounds in grasses, including *shumo*—its root resembles sheep’s trotters and is edible. *Shu* pronounced *shǔ* or *yù*. Today, south of the Yangzi, it is simply called *shu*, pronounced *kǒu*, the difference being one of dialectal weight. Its grasses include *qinjiao*, a plant with seeds like pepper but with fine leaves. Its northern slopes abound in ochre, its southern slopes in jade. There is a bird here, its form like a serpent but with four wings, six eyes, and three feet. Its name is *suanyu*, and its cry is self-named. When seen, it portends terror for the town. Some say eating it prevents intoxication.

Further southeast by three hundred and twenty *li* lies Mount Mengmen. The *Master Shi* states: "Before the Dragon Gate was opened and Lüliang was quarried, the Yellow River emerged above Mengmen, flooding in great surges that reversed its flow, submerging all hills and mounds—this was called the Deluge." The *Tale of King Mu* states: "Northward he ascended Mengmen, the confluence of the Nine Rivers." Its summit abounds in dark jade and gold, its base in yellow ochre and black stone.

Further southeast by three hundred and twenty *li* lies Mount Ping. The Ping River rises upon it and sinks beneath it, and it abounds in fine jade. Further east by three hundred *li* lies Mount Jing. It holds fine jade, many lacquer trees, and abundant bamboo. Its southern slopes abound in red copper, its northern slopes in black whetstone—black whetstone being a kind of stone. The *Master Shi* states: "Add black and yellow whetstone." The colors of feathers are not uniform. *Xiao* pronounced like the *xiao* in "bamboo *xiao*."

The Gao River rises here and flows south into the Yellow River. Further east by two hundred *li* lies Mount Chongwei. Its summit abounds in gold and jade, its base in bamboo and green jasper. The Dan River rises here and flows south into the Yellow River. The Bo River also rises here—*The Master Huainan* states: "The Bo River rises on Mount Xianyu"—and flows southeast into the Yellow Marsh.

Further east by three hundred *li* lies Mount Pengpi. No grass or trees grow upon it, but it abounds in gold and jade, and its base holds much water. The Zaolin River—*zao* pronounced *zǎo*—rises here and flows southeast into the Yellow River. The Fei River also rises here and flows south into the Chuang River. Within it dwell many *feiyi* serpents.

Further east by one hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Xiaohou. The Mingzhang River rises here and flows south into the Yellow Marsh. There is a bird here, its form like a magpie but with white markings. Its name is *guque*—*gu* pronounced *gū*, *que* pronounced *xí*. Eating it prevents choking.

*The Erya* alternatively renders the pronunciation [as above].

Further east by three hundred and seventy *li* lies Mount Taihou. The Gong River—*gong* pronounced *gōng*—rises here and flows south into the Hu Pool—*hu* pronounced *hū*, *tuo* pronounced *tuó* (hereafter the same). Its heights abound in gold and jade, and its base holds much arrow bamboo.

Further northeast by two hundred *li* lies Mount Xuan Yuan. Its heights abound in copper, and its base holds much bamboo. There is a bird here, its form like an owl but with a white head. Its name is *huangniao*—its cry echoes its own name. Eating it prevents jealousy.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies Mount Yeli. It now lies in Nie County of Shangdang Commandery. Its heights abound in pines and cypress, and hold gold and jade. The Qin River rises here and flows south into the Yellow River, entering it northeast of Rongyang County. Some say it rises on Mount Yangtou in Gushu County. To its east lies a forest named Crimson Grove. The Crimson River rises here and flows south into the Yellow River. The Yinghou River also rises here and flows north into the Si River.

Further east by three hundred *li* lies Mount Juru. *The Odes* state: "There by the Fen’s marshy banks." No grass or trees grow upon it, but it abounds in gold and jade. The Qi River—*qi* pronounced *qí*—rises here and flows south into the Yellow River.

Now the Qi River rises in Mount Danhao of Longlü County in Jixia Commandery, flows east past Henei County, and southward becomes the White Ditch.

Further north by three hundred *li* lies Mount Shenqun—*qun* pronounced as in *cangqun* (granary). Atop it are patterned stones; below, white serpents and flying insects. The Yellow River rises here and flows east into the Huan. The Huan River rises northeast of Linlü County in Jixia Commandery and enters the Clear River at Changle in Wei Commandery—*huan* pronounced *wan*. The Fu River also rises here and flows east into the Ou River. The Fu River now rises in Mount Fukou west of Linshui County, passes northwest of Ye, and enters the Zhang at Lieren County; its waters are warm.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies Mount Fajiu, now located west of Changzi County in Shangdang Commandery. Its heights abound in *zhe* trees. There is a bird here, shaped like a crow, with a patterned head, white beak, and red feet, named Jingwei, whose cry is its own name. She was the youngest daughter of the Flame Thearch, named Nüwa—*wa* pronounced *ejia* (a misreading; sometimes written as *jie*). The Flame Thearch was Shennong. Nüwa drowned while playing in the Eastern Sea and did not return, thus becoming Jingwei. She constantly carries wood and stones from the western mountains to fill the Eastern Sea—*yan* (to block) pronounced *yin*. The Zhang River—*zhuo Zhang* pronounced *zhang*—rises here and flows east into the Yellow River. Some say it rises in Mount Lugu of Changzi County, flowing east to Ye and entering the Clear Zhang.

Further northeast by one hundred twenty *li* lies Mount Shaoshan, now in Zhan County of Leping Commandery (formerly part of Shangdang). Atop it are gold and jade; below, copper. The Clear Zhang River rises here and flows east into the Turbid Zhang. The Clear Zhang rises in the Great Rope Valley of Mount Shaoshan and enters the Turbid Zhang south of Wu’an County at Baogong Village. Some say it flows northeast to Yicheng and enters the Great River.

Further northeast by two hundred *li* lies Mount Xishan. Atop it is much jade; below, whetstone. The Niushou River rises here and flows east into the Fu River.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies Mount Jingshan. It holds fine jade. The Jing River rises here and flows southeast into Haize Marsh.

Further north by one hundred *li* lies Mount Tishou. It holds jade, much stone, and no water.

Further north by one hundred *li* lies Mount Xiushan. Atop it are jade and azure-green stone; its trees are mostly *xun*—the *mei* among trees, pronounced *xun*. Its grasses are mostly peony and *xiongqiong*. Peony is also called *xinyi* and belongs to the fragrant herbs. The Wei River rises here and flows east into the Yellow River. Within it dwells the *hu*, a fish resembling the *dian* but larger, and white in color.

The preceding mountain’s description is lost. There is a creature resembling a toad, small and green. Some say it is merely a variant of the *huòshuǐ*.

Further north by one hundred and twenty *li* lies Mount Song. The Yang River rises here and flows northeast into the Yellow River.

Further north by one hundred and twenty *li* lies Mount Dunyu. Atop it grow no grasses or trees; it holds gold and jade. The Jie River rises on its southern slope (pronounced *xīgè*) and flows east into the Tailu River. The Tailu River is today’s vast marsh north of Julu, known as Guangping Marsh. The Zhi River rises on its northern slope (pronounced *dǐsì*) and flows east into the Peng River. Today, the Pai River emerges from Qiongquan Valley west of Zhongqiu County and flows east into the Zhang River at Tangyang County. The Huai River rises here and flows east into Zhi Marsh.

Further north by one hundred and seventy *li* lies Mount Zhe. On its southern slope are gold and jade; on its northern slope, iron. The Liju River rises here and flows north into the Wei River.

Further north by three hundred *li* lies Mount Weilong. Atop it is azure-green jade; on its southern slope, gold; on its northern slope, iron. The Fei River rises here and flows east into Gao Marsh. Within it lies *gao* stone, the nature of which is unclear (pronounced *léi*, sometimes written as *lěi*; *kuǐlěi* describes the appearance of large stones, though some say it is a stone’s name). The Changtie River rises here and flows north into the Great Marsh.

Further north by one hundred and eighty *li* lies Mount Baima. On its southern slope are much stone and jade; on its northern slope, iron and abundant red copper. The Muma River rises here and flows northeast into the Hu Pool (pronounced *hūchí* or *hūtú*).

Further north by two hundred *li* lies Mount Kongsang. It bears no grasses or trees. A mountain of the same name appears above; this may be a duplicate. Snow covers it in winter and summer. The Kongsang River rises here and flows east into Hu Pool.

Further north by three hundred *li* lies Mount Taixi. It bears no grasses or trees but holds much gold and jade. There is a beast here whose form resembles a sheep, with one horn and one eye, the eye behind its ear. Its name is lost (pronounced like *dòng* in *wūdòng*). It cries out its own name. The Mo Pool River rises here (today, the Hu Pool River emerges from Mount Wufu south of Lucheng County in Yanmen) and flows east into the Lou River (pronounced *lóu*). The Ye Girl River rises on its southern slope and flows south into the Qin River (pronounced *yì*, as in *yìzé*).

Further north by three hundred *li* lies Mount Shi. It conceals much gold and jade. The Huohuo River rises here (pronounced like *huò* in *chǐhuò*) and flows east into Hu Pool. The Xianyu River rises here and flows south into Hu Pool.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies Mount Tongrong. The Gaoxu River rises here and flows east into the Louye River.

Further north by three hundred *li* lies Mount Gaoshi. Today, it lies in Lingqiu County of Beidi. The Zi River rises here (pronounced *cí*) and flows south into Hu Pool. Its trees are mostly lost (text corrupt); its grasses are mostly *tiao*. The Kou River rises here (pronounced *kòu*) and flows east into the Yellow River, passing south of Boling County before turning northeast into the Yi River.

Further north by three hundred *li* lies Mount Lu. It holds fine jade. The Zheng River rises here (sometimes written as the Tan River) and flows east into the Yellow River.

Further north by two hundred *li* lies Mount Yi (pronounced *qí*). The Pan River rises here (pronounced *pán*) and flows east into the Yellow River.

One hundred and twenty *li* to the north lies Mount Yan. It abounds in *ying* stone—stones resembling jade, adorned with colored bands like swaddling cloth, known as Yan stones. The Yan River rises here and flows east into the Yellow River.

Traveling five hundred *li* north by land and five hundred *li* by water, one reaches Mount Rao. It bears no grasses or trees but holds much *yao* jade and azure-green stone. Its beasts are mostly camels; its birds, mostly *liu* (nature unclear; some say *liu* refers to the *xiūliú*).

The Li-Guo River rises here and flows east into the Yellow River. Within its waters dwells the *shi* fish, which kills those who eat it. (Unclear; some say it is the *ni*.)

Four hundred *li* to the north lies Mount Qian. It bears no grasses or trees. On its southern slopes lie gold and jade; on its northern slopes, iron but no water. There is a beast here, shaped like an ox but with three feet, called the *huan* (pronounced *yuan*). Its cry is its own name.

Five hundred *li* farther north lies Mount Lun. The Lun River rises here and flows east into the Yellow River. There is a beast here, shaped like a *mi* deer, with a river flowing from its tail—the river emerges from an orifice. It is called the *Xiongjiu*.

Five hundred *li* to the north lies Mount Jieshi. The *Shui Jing* states: "This is Lin Cang Mountain, now submerged in the waters south of Jieshi County in Liaoxi." Others say it lies by the sea in Licang County of Youbeiping. The Sheng River rises here and flows east into the Yellow River. Its waters abound in *puyi* fish (unclear). Above the mountain lies jade; below, much azure-green stone.

Traveling five hundred *li* north by water, one reaches Mount Yanmen, which bears no grasses or trees. Mount Yanmen is the northern mound from which wild geese emerge westward, hence its name. It lies north of Gaoliu.

Four hundred *li* north by water lies Tai Marsh, within which stands a mountain called Mount Didu. It spans a hundred *li* in circumference, bears no grasses or trees, but holds jade and gold.

Five hundred *li* farther north lies Mount Chun-Yu Mu-Feng. To its north lies Mount Jihao, whose winds are like □ (a term describing violent gusts; pronounced *li*, or said to be a whirlwind). To its west lies Mount Youdu, from which the Yu River rises. The Yu is the Black River. Here dwells a great serpent with a red head and white body, whose cry is like an ox’s. Where it appears, the region suffers severe drought.

In all, the first section of the *Northern Third Classic* records forty-six mountains from Mount Taihang to Mount Gan-Wufeng, spanning twelve thousand three hundred and fifty *li*. Twenty of its spirits appear as horse-bodied with human faces. In their sacrifices, all are buried with a bundle of *zao* (clustered algae) and *ju* (a fragrant herb, akin to orchid). *Zao* is pronounced *qing chang dai*.

Fourteen spirits appear as pig-bodied, bearing jade. In their sacrifices, jade is used but not buried. Ten spirits appear as pig-bodied with eight feet and serpent tails. In their sacrifices, all are buried with a jade *bi* disk.

In total, forty-four spirits are sacrificed to with *xu* rice, and none receive cooked food.

This section of the mountain records encompasses eighty-seven mountains, spanning twenty-three thousand two hundred and thirty *li*.

**Northern Mountains Classic: Illustrations and Eulogies**

**Water Horse**
A dragon’s essence, a steed of water’s kind—
From Yu’s deep pools, a wondrous sign.
The swift of Wuwa’s well,
A spirit rare, a sacred spell.

**Tiao Fish**
No grief more bitter than a heart unwhole,
Yet *Xuan* grass soothes the troubled soul.
By mountain streams the *tiao* glides,
A fleeting joy where sorrow hides.

**□-Shu Beast, Qi-□ Bird, Heluo Fish**
A beast that loathes the flame, its name □-shu,
A bird that shifts its form—*qi-□* its hue.
The *heluo* fish: one head, tenfold frame,
A marvel strange, yet all the same.

**Meng-Huai**
Like a badger, red its bristles gleam,
A beast of dread in hunters’ dream.
Its presence wards the ill at bay,
Yet leaves no trace of its sway.

**Xi-Xi Fish**
With one flap of its wings, ten soar in flight,
Its cry like magpies in the night.
Scales edge its feathers, strange and bright—
A fish most odd, yet guards from blight.

**Camel**
A beast of wonder, burdened, wise,
With flesh for saddle, swift it flies.
Across the shifting sands it treads,
To hidden springs its memory leads.

**Ear Mouse**
With feet it digs, with wings it glides,
Its tail aloft in soaring rides.
Its pelt so fine, a shield so true,
A hundred poisons it subdues.

**You-Lei**
Like a monkey, yet not the same,
It feigns wit, plays the fool’s game.
Touch it and it laughs with glee,
See a man—it feigns sleep slyly.

**Yu Bird, Meng-Ji, Zu-Zi Beast**
A rat with wings, its call a bleat,
The *meng-ji* like a leopard’s seat.
It howls at men, its name it cries—
*Zu-zi*, a beast of ill surprise.

**Jiao Bird**
Its plumage like a pheasant’s hen,
In flocks they fly, a darkening ken.
When they take flight, the sun is veiled,
When they alight, the fields are pale.
Its flesh tests needles, spares the cure,
No need for stitches to endure.

**Zhu-Jian Beast, White Heron, Song-Si Bird**
The *zhu-jian* roars, in line they tread,
Each holds the tail of one ahead.
The white heron, the *song-si* bright,
Like pheasants dressed in embroidered light.
At sight of men, they leap and play,
Their crests like brocade in array.

**Lodestone**
The lodestone draws the iron near,
The hawksbill snatches mustard here.
A hidden force, a meeting strange,
Where things converge beyond all range.

**Yak**
A bull of war, with bannered might,
The yak leads armies to the fight.
Not flesh alone brings woe’s decree—
Its very hide commands the sea.

**Long Serpent**
A serpent vast, a hundred spans,
Its mane a tangle, fierce its plans.
It devours all in flight or run,
The worst of beasts, of venom done.

**Shan-Pi**
The *shan-pi* beast delights in play,
At sight of men, it darts away.
Swift as an arrow’s flight it goes,
A gust of wind where’er it flows.

**Ya-Yu, Zhu-Huai Beast, □ Fish, Fei-Yi Serpent**
The *ya-yu* and *zhu-huai* bring harm,
Like butterflies with sheep’s dark form.
The *fei-yi* snake: one head, two frames,
A monstrous sight with deadly claims.

**Chi Fish**
The sun’s reflection stirs the day,
The earthen snake brings night’s delay.
Yet small the *chi*, its bite so slight,
It humbles those who scorn its might.

**Pao-Xiao**
The *pao-xiao* craves the jade’s embrace,
Its eyes beneath its arms’ dark place.
It gnaws its flesh when prey’s undone,
A caution carved on sacred bronze.

**Yao-Lü, □ Horse, Du-□**
A beast like leopards, rich its hue,
The *lü* leaps cliffs with fearless view.
The *□* horse bears a single horn,
A tiger’s form, a steed’s tail worn—
Its name: *du-□*, a sight forlorn.

**□-□**
A bird that guards from summer’s heat,
Its name is □-□, a rare feat.

Dawn and dusk alternate,
By day concealed, by night revealed.
All things prize their proper use—
Why seek phoenix or simurgh’s news?

**Ju-Ji, Yao Beast, Three Mulberries**
The *ju-ji* grunts like swine,
Its hide a flush of crimson fine.
Four wings, one eye—its name is *yao*.
Three mulberries stand branchless, tall,
Their trunks alone rise straight and proud.

**□ Beast**
The *□* beast bears four horns,
A horse’s tail with spurs adorned.
It scales the cliffs, then homeward turns,
Leaping chasms, twisting, yearns.
Its form most strange, as if in dance,
It whirls and twists in trance.

**Celestial Steed**
Dragons ride the clouds, serpents glide through mist,
Yet neither matches the Celestial Steed,
Who soars by nature’s grace, untwisted.
Its veins suspend the turning wheel of fate,
The hidden workings of the heavens’ gate.

**Qu-Ju and Front Wader**
The *qu-ju* resembles birds,
Its body blue, its feet of gold.
Who eats it never hungers more,
And may forsake the grain’s dull store.
Within it holds a treasure rare,
Paired with the cinnabar tree’s care.

**Flying Squirrel**
Some soar by tail, some by their whiskers rise,
The flying squirrel beats its wings and flies,
Swiftly ascending to the skies.
Its use is not in mouth or limb,
But in the spirit’s power grim.

**□ Bird, Elephant-Snake, □-Father Fish**
A bird prone to alarm,
Its name is *□-□*.
The elephant-snake resembles pheasant’s guise,
Bearing its young in strange surprise.
The *□-father* fish has a swine’s head,
Its body like a pig’s, it treads.

**Suan-Yu**
On Jing Mountain dwells a bird,
Its form unlike all others heard.
Like a serpent shaped, with two feet, four wings,
Its sight brings fear to towns and kings.
Yet those who eat it never grow drunk.

**Gu-□ and Yellow Bird**
The *gu-□* bird, when eaten, brings no harm,
While the yellow bird sings its own charm.
Women wear it to feign grace,
Their natures shifting with its trace.

**Jingwei**
The Flame Emperor’s daughter, transformed to Jingwei,
Drowned in the Eastern Sea, her spirit flew free.
Westward she soared, then took up wood and stone,
To fill the vast waves with her ceaseless moan.

**□-□, Bear-Nine, Great Serpent**
The *□-□* resembles sheep,
Its eyes behind its ears they peep.
A hole upon its tail does grow,
Its name: *bear-nine*, as all men know.
In the mountains of Youdu’s gloom,
A serpent vast as oxen looms.

*The Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Three ends here.*
*The Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Four*
*Guo Pu’s Commentary*

**The Eastern Mountains Classic**

*The Eastern Mountains Classic* begins with Mount Su□. Pronounced *su* and *zhu*. To its north lies Mount Qianmei, also a mountain’s name. Pronounced *mei*. The Shui River originates here, flowing east and north into the sea. Within its waters abound *yongyong* fish, pronounced *rong*. Their form resembles the plow-ox, striped like a tiger. Their cries are like the grunting of swine.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Lei, pronounced *lei*. Its heights bear jade; its depths hold gold. The Hu River rises here, flowing east into the Shui River. Within it dwell many *huoshi*—tadpoles. The *Erya* calls them *huodong*.

Three hundred *li* farther south lies Mount Xunzhuang. Its heights abound in gold and jade; its depths teem with azure-green stone. A beast dwells here, shaped like a dog with six feet, named *congcong*. Its cry is its own name. A bird also resides here, resembling a chicken but clad in rat’s fur, called *□shu*, pronounced *zi*. Its appearance heralds great drought in the land. The Gen River, pronounced *zhi*, originates here, flowing north into the Hu River. Within it swim many *zhen* fish, shaped like the *tiao* but with a beak like a needle. Found in the Eastern Sea, they also inhabit the waters east of the Jiang. Eating them averts plague.

Three hundred *li* farther south rises Mount Boqi. Barren of grass and trees, devoid of water.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Fantiao. Barren of grass and trees, its expanse is mostly sand. The Jian River, pronounced like the word for "diminish," flows north into the sea. Within it swim many *gan* fish, also called *huangjia*, pronounced *gan*.

Four hundred *li* farther south lies Mount Guer. Its heights abound in lacquer trees; its depths teem with mulberry and *zhe*. The Guer River originates here, flowing north into the sea. Within it dwell many *jian* fish.

Four hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Gaoshi. Its heights bear much jade; its depths hold *zhen* stone, which may be fashioned into whetstones for needles, curing abscesses and swellings. The Zhusheng River rises here, flowing east into the marsh. Within it lie abundant gold and jade.

Three hundred *li* farther south rises Mount Yue. Its heights abound in mulberry; its depths teem with *chu*. The Luan River, pronounced *li*, originates here, flowing east into the marsh. Within it lie abundant gold and jade.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Cai. Its heights are barren of grass and trees; its depths hold much water. Within it swim many *kan□wu* fish, meaning uncertain. Pronounced *xu*.

Here dwells a beast, its form like Kuafu yet bristling with swine’s fur, its cry like a shout—wherever it appears, the world suffers great floods.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Du. Its heights bear abundant gold and jade; its depths hold fine stone. The Motu River rises here, flowing southeast into the Mian. Within it swim many *□□* (pronounced *tiao* or *rong*), their form like a yellow serpent with fish-like wings, emitting light as they move—wherever they appear, the region suffers severe drought.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Tai, the Eastern Marchmount, the Sacred Peak. Today it lies northwest of *□*gao County, rising forty-eight *li* and three hundred paces from base to summit. Its heights bear much jade; its depths hold abundant gold. Here dwells a beast, its form like a piglet yet bearing a pearl, named *tongtong* (its cry like *yin* and *dong*), its name derived from its own call. The Huan River originates here, flowing east into the Jiang (or "sea" in one version), within which lie many *shuiyu* (water-jade).

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Zhu. It stands firm beside the Jiang (or "shore" in one version), barren of grass and trees yet rich in *yao* and *bi* jade. The Ji River rises here, flowing southeast into the Qutan River, within which swim many *cilei*.

From the first mountain of the Eastern Mountains, Mount Su□, to Mount Zhu, there are twelve mountains spanning three thousand six hundred *li*. Their spirits all take human form with dragon heads.
