For their sacrifices, one offers a dog with fur intact, and *□* (fish) is used. The blood is smeared in ritual as *shen*. The *Gongyang Commentary* states: "One taps their noses with *□du*" (pronounced like the *er* in "bait").

The first mountain of the *Eastern Second Classic* is Mount Kongsang, famed for yielding wood for zithers and lutes (as recorded in the *Rites of Zhou*). To the north it overlooks the Shi River; to the east, the Juwu; to the south, Shaling; to the west, Min Marsh (pronounced *min*). Here dwells a beast, its form like an ox yet striped like a tiger, its cry like *qin* (or *yin*), named *lingling* (pronounced *ling*), its call its own name—wherever it appears, the world suffers great floods.

Six hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Caoxi. Its depths hold many valleys yet no water, and teem with birds and beasts.

Four hundred *li* to the southwest stands Mount Yigao (pronounced *yi*). Its heights bear abundant gold and jade; its depths hold much chalk. The Fenggao River rises here, flowing east into the Jinu River, within which dwell many *shenyao* (*shen* meaning clam, *yao* meaning mother-of-pearl, also a clam variant; pronounced *shen* or *yao*).

Farther south, sailing five hundred *li* across water and three hundred *li* through flowing sands, one reaches the tail of Mount Ge. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in whetstones.

Three hundred eighty *li* farther south stands the head of Mount Ge, equally barren. The Li River (pronounced *li*) rises here, flowing east into Yu Marsh. Within it swim many *zhubie* fish (pronounced *bie*), their form like lungs yet bearing eyes, six feet, and pearls. Their flavor is sour-sweet, and eating them prevents pestilence (or seasonal qi diseases). The *Master Lü’s Spring and Autumn Annals* states: "The fish of the Li River, named *zhubie*, have six feet and pearls—among the finest of fish."

Three hundred eighty *li* farther south stands Mount Yue. Its heights bear much catalpa and nanmu; its depths hold much thorn and *qi*.

The Za Yu River rises here, flowing east into the Yellow River. There is a beast whose form resembles a rabbit but with a bird’s beak, owl’s eyes, and a serpent’s tail. When it sees humans, it feigns death. Its name is *qiúyú* (pronounced *qiú* and *yú*). Its cry is self-named, and its appearance heralds locust plagues that ravage crops. (The locusts belong to the *zhōnghuáng* kind, meaning they damage fields and seedlings. Pronounced *zhōng*.)

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Du Father. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in water.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Geng. Barren of grass and trees, it holds much *shuǐbì* (a type of water jade). Many great serpents dwell here. There is a beast whose form resembles a fox but with fish-like wings. Its name is *zhūnú* (pronounced *rú*). Its cry is self-named, and its appearance foretells terror in the land.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Lu Qi. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in sand and stone. The Sha River rises here, flowing south into the Cen River. Within it swim many *□hú* (pronounced *lí*), their form like mandarin ducks but with human feet. Their cry is self-named, and their appearance heralds much earthwork in the land. (Today’s *tíhú* birds have feet somewhat resembling human feet.)

Three hundred eighty *li* farther south stands Mount Gu She. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in water.

Three hundred *li* by water farther south, and one hundred *li* through flowing sands, stands North Mount Gu She. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in stone.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands South Mount Gu She. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in water.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Bi. Barren of grass and trees, it holds many great serpents and much *bìshuǐyù* (jade from the Bi waters).

Five hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Gou Shi. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in metal and jade. The Yuan River rises here, flowing east into Sha Marsh. (Another name: Mount Xia Shi.)

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Gu Feng. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in metal and jade. There is a beast whose form resembles a fox but with wings. Its cry is like that of swans and geese. Its name is *bìbì* (pronounced *bì*). Its appearance heralds great drought across the land.

Five hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Fu Li. Its heights abound in metal and jade; its depths hold much *zhēn* stone. There is a beast whose form resembles a fox but with nine tails, nine heads, and tiger’s claws. Its name is *lóngzhì* (pronounced *lóng* and *zhì*). Its cry is like that of an infant, and it devours humans.

Five hundred *li* farther south stands Mount □ (pronounced *zhēn*). To the south, it overlooks the □ River; to the east, it gazes upon a lake marsh. There is a beast whose form resembles a horse but with a sheep’s head, four horns, and an ox’s tail. Its cry is like that of a *háo* dog. Its name is *yōuyōu* (pronounced *yōu*). Its appearance heralds many cunning guests in the land. (Cunning means deceitful.) There is a bird whose form resembles an owl but with a rat’s tail. Skilled at climbing trees, its name is *xié gōu*. Its appearance heralds much pestilence in the land.

In all, from the beginning of the "Second Eastern Classic" at Mount Kong Sang to Mount □, there are seventeen mountains spanning six thousand six hundred forty *li*. The spirits here all have the form of beasts with human faces, bearing antlers. (The *mí* deer family has antlers called *gé*. Pronounced *gé*.) Their sacrifices use the hair of one chicken, and their prayers employ one jade *bì* buried in the earth.

The "Third Eastern Classic" begins at Mount Shi Hu, which gazes north upon Mount □ (pronounced *xiáng*). Its heights abound in metal and jade; its depths hold much thorn. There is a beast whose form resembles a *mí* deer but with fish eyes. Its name is *shuǎ hú* (pronounced *wǎn*). Its cry is self-named.

Eight hundred *li* by water farther south stands Mount Qi. Its trees abound in peach and plum; its beasts abound in tigers.

Five hundred *li* by water farther south stands Mount Zhu Gou. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in sand and stone. This mountain spans a hundred *li* in breadth and width, and holds many *mèi* fish (the *wèi* fish, pronounced *wèi*).

Seven hundred *li* by water farther south stands Mount Zhong Father. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in sand.

One thousand *li* by water farther east stands Mount Hu She. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in sand and stone.

Seven hundred *li* by water farther south stands Mount Meng Zi. Its trees abound in catalpa, paulownia, peach, and plum; its grasses abound in mushrooms and reeds. (Unverified. Pronounced *xuǎn*’s □.)

Its beasts abound in elk and deer. This mountain spans a hundred *li* in breadth and width. Upon it a stream arises, named the Jasper Yang. Within it abound *zhān* and *wěi* fish. The *wěi* is the sturgeon: resembling the *zhān* but with a long snout, its body bears no scales or armor. Another name is □*qiú*. It is also called the *huáng*.

Five hundred *li* by water farther south lies the White Flowing Sands; five hundred *li* farther stands a mountain named Mount Qi Zhong. *Qi* is pronounced *qǐ*. It spans two hundred *li* in breadth and width, barren of grass and trees, and holds great serpents. Upon it abounds jade. There is a body of water spanning forty *li* in breadth and width, all surging forth—today in Fenyin County of Hedong, there is a *fèn* stream whose source lies beneath the earth, gushing and boiling forth with unfathomable depth, of this same kind. It is named the Deep Marsh. Within it abound *xī* turtles. *Xī* refers to the great turtle: its shell bears patterned colors, resembling the hawksbill but thinner. Pronounced *yí zhī jí*. There is a fish whose form resembles the carp but with six feet and a bird’s tail, named the *gé gé* fish. Pronounced *gé*. It cries out its own name.

Nine hundred *li* by water farther south stands Mount Mu Yu. Pronounced like the character *mǐn*. Upon it grow grass and trees, and abound gold and jade, as well as ochre.

There is a beast whose form resembles an ox but with a horse’s tail, named *jīng jīng*. It cries out its own name.

Farther south by five hundred *li* of water travel, across three hundred *li* of flowing sands, lies Mount Wu Gao. Gazing southward, one sees the Young Sea—this is the Lesser Sea. As the *Huainanzi* states: "The great isle of the east is called the Lesser Sea." Gazing eastward, one sees the *fú* tree—*fú sāng* in two readings. There are no grasses or trees, and the winds blow fiercely. This mountain spans a hundred *li* in breadth and width.

The beginning of the "Third Eastern Classic" commences at Mount Shi Hu and extends to Mount Wu Gao, comprising nine mountains over six thousand nine hundred *li*. The spirits here all bear human bodies with ram’s horns. Their sacrifices require one uncastrated ram and glutinous millet. When these spirits appear, wind, rain, and floods bring ruin.

The beginning of the "Fourth Eastern Classic" is Mount Bei Hao, which overlooks the Northern Sea. There grows a tree whose form resembles the poplar, with red blossoms. Its fruit is like the jujube but without a pit, its flavor tart and sweet. Eating it prevents malaria. The River of Drinking Water issues forth here, flowing northeast into the sea.

There is a beast whose form resembles a wolf, with a red head, rat’s eyes, and a cry like a piglet’s, named *hè jū*—*gé zhì* in two readings. It devours humans.

There is a bird whose form resembles a chicken but with a white head, rat’s feet, and tiger’s claws, named *qí*. It too devours humans.

Three hundred *li* farther south stands Mount Mao, barren of grasses and trees. The River of Dark Bodies issues forth here, flowing west into the Zhan River. Within it abound *xiū* fish—now written as *xiā qiū* (loach) in some texts, pronounced *qiū*. Its form resembles the carp but with a large head; those who eat it do not develop warts.

Three hundred and twenty *li* farther south stands Mount Dong Shi. Upon it abound dark jades. There grows a tree whose form resembles the poplar but with red veins. Its sap resembles blood, though it bears no fruit, and is named *qǐ*. This sap may be applied to horses, and when rubbed upon them, their temper becomes gentle and well-trained. The Ci River issues forth here, flowing northeast into the sea. Within it abound fine cowries and *zǐ* fish, whose form resembles the crucian carp but with one head and ten bodies. Its odor is like that of *mí wú*; eating it prevents [loss of qi].

Three hundred *li* farther southeast stands Mount Nü Zheng, upon which there are no grasses, trees, or stones. The Gao River issues forth here, flowing west into the Ge River. Within it abound *bó* fish, whose form resembles the sturgeon but with one eye. Its cry is like retching, resembling the sound of human vomiting. When it appears, the world suffers great drought.

Two hundred *li* farther southeast stands Mount Qin. It abounds in gold and jade but has no stones. The Shi River issues forth here, flowing north into Gao Marsh. Within it abound *xiū* fish and patterned cowries. There is a beast whose form resembles a piglet but with tusks, named *dāng kāng*. It cries out its own name, and when it appears, the world enjoys great abundance.

Two hundred *li* farther southeast stands Mount Zi Tong. The River of Zi Tong issues forth here, flowing west into Yu Ru Marsh. Within it abound *□* fish, pronounced *huá*. Its form resembles a fish but with bird’s wings; it emits light when entering or exiting the water. Its cry is like that of the mandarin duck. When it appears, the world suffers great drought.

Two hundred *li* farther northeast stands Mount Shan. It abounds in gold and jade. There is a beast whose form resembles a pig but with a human face, a yellow body, and a red tail, named *hé yú*. Its cry is like that of an infant. This beast devours humans and also feeds on insects and serpents. When it appears, the world suffers great floods.

Two hundred *li* farther east stands Mount Tai. Upon it abound gold, jade, and *zhēn* trees—*nǚ zhēn* (glossy privet), whose leaves do not wither in winter. There is a beast whose form resembles an ox but with a white head, one eye, and a serpent’s tail, named *fēi*. Its cry resembles the *fěi* of *fěi cuì* (kingfisher). Where it passes over water, the water dries up; where it treads upon grass, the grass dies. When it appears, the world suffers great pestilence. It is said that its body carries the qi of disaster.

Its *inscription* reads:
*"Fēi* is its name,
its form seems harmless.
Yet where it passes, all withers and dries,
more dire than the poison of the *zhèn* bird.
All things fear it,
and wish it far away."

The Gou River originates here, flowing northward into the Lao River. Within its waters abound *xiū* fish.

The first section of the *Eastern Fourth Classic* spans from Mount Beihao to Mount Tai, comprising eight mountains over a distance of one thousand seven hundred and twenty *li*.

To the right lies the record of the mountains of the *Eastern Classic*, encompassing forty-six mountains and a total distance of eighteen thousand eight hundred and sixty *li*.

**Eastern Mountains Classic: Illustrations and Eulogies**

**Yōngyōng Fish and Cóngcóng Beast, □ Rat**
The fish is called *yōngyōng*,
resembling an ox or a brindled tiger.
The *cóngcóng*’s form,
like a dog with six legs.
The □ rat resembles a chicken;
its appearance heralds drought and desiccation.

**Xiū□**
The *xiū□* serpent’s form,
wings quivering with radiant light.
Riding the waves, it soars and dives,
entering and emerging from the Jiang and Xiang.
Its sighting foretells drought in the year,
a portent of fire.

**Tóngtóng**
As the clam holds its pearl,
so too the beast harbors the inexplicable.
The *tóngtóng* resembles a pig,
clad in coarse cloth yet bearing calamity.
Disaster arises without cause,
summoned by none but ourselves.

**Kān□ Fish and Línglíng Beast**
*Kān□* and *línglíng*,
distinct in *qi* yet sharing omens.
Their appearance heralds floods,
and the world is plunged into darkness and deluge.
Is this mere falsehood descending,
or does it answer to written prophecy?

**Pearl-Backed Fish**
In the ritual waters’ freshness,
its form like floating lungs.
Its body unites the Three Powers,
yet it brings harm through trade and profit.
With its uses so manifold,
how can it protect itself?

**Qiúyú**
The *qiúyú* beast,
feigns sleep when it sees humans.
In harmony with disaster’s *qi*,
it emerges to reap the harvest of flesh and years.
Can this truly be its doing,
or must we ascribe it to Heaven?

**Lí Lì Beast and □ Hú Bird**
The *lí lì* and □ *hú*,
now flying, now crouching.
These are earthly omens,
appearing where earthworks rise.
At the labor of the Long Wall,
they gathered within Qin’s domain.

**Zhū Nòu**
The *zhū nòu* is unremarkable,
yet its appearance startles the townsfolk.
No sincerity moves it,
for it exists by fate’s decree.
It arises in response to events,
never without cause.

**Bìbì, Lóngchī Beast, and Xié Gōu Bird**
The *bìbì* resembles a fox,
winged yet unable to fly.
With nine tails and tiger’s claws,
it is called *lóng zhǐ*.
The *xié gōu* resembles a wild duck;
its sighting brings the people sorrow.

**Yōuyōu**
Order lies in obtaining the worthy;
ruin comes from the ruler’s folly.

The coming of *yōuyōu*
is ascribed to hidden resonance—
who has witnessed its passage?

**Xī Turtle**
Forty spans in girth,
it lurks where springs surge and seethe.
The numinous turtle dwells there,
flicking its tail to nurture *qi*.
Zhuangzi was moved by this,
casting his line in defiance of the noble.

**Yuàn Hú Beast, Jīngjīng Beast, and Gé Gé Fish**
The *yuàn hú* resembles a elk,
its eyes like a fish’s.
The *jīngjīng* is ox-like,
distinguishing itself by its tail.
Where the *gé gé* fish lurks,
its depths know no bounds.

**Hè Jū Beast and Qí Sparrow**
The *hè jū* is a cunning beast,
the *qí* a malevolent bird.
Some bear the form of wolves,
others the claws of tigers.
What need is there for armor and weapons?
Disorder is quelled by the Way.

**Qǐ Wood**
Horses, strong and swift,
are anointed with *qǐ* sap.
No need for Sun Yang’s skill—
they grow docile of their own accord.
Its art has no fixed method,
yet its principle holds hidden mastery.

**Zǐ Fish and Bó Fish**
A fish with ten bodies,
reeking of *mí wú* herbs.
Eating it harmonizes the body,
preventing *qi* from sinking downward.
When the *bó* leaps from the deep,
it heralds disaster.

**Hé Yǔ**
Boar-bodied, human-faced,
it is called *hé yǔ*.
Its nature is rapacious and cruel,
devouring all without restraint.
The essence of utmost yin,
its appearance brings rain and flood.

**Dāng Kāng Beast and □ Fish**
The *dāng kāng* resembles a piglet—
its sighting foretells a bountiful year.
The □ fish has avian wings,
its flight a trail of radiance.
Though they emerge together,
their omens diverge—some bring calamity, others fortune.

**Fēi**
The *fēi* is a beast of disaster,
its hooves and heels sinking deep.
Wherever it passes,
waters dry and forests wither.
Endowed with *qi* by nature,
its body embodies ruin and excess.

*Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Four (conclusion)*
*(Textual note: "Now located at the western pass of Mount Tai in Gaoxian County." Alternate edition: "Now located northwest of Mount Tai in Fenggao County.")*
*(Textual note: "Its form resembles lungs with eyes." Alternate edition: "Its form resembles lungs with four eyes.")*

*Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Five*
*Guo Pu’s Commentary*

*The Classic of Central Mountains*

*The Classic of Central Mountains* begins with Mount Ganzǎo. The Gòng River originates here—its name pronounced *gōng*—and flows westward into the Yellow River. Its heights abound in *niǔ* trees, while below grow grasses with mallow stems and apricot leaves, or in some accounts, *hù* leaves. Their yellow blossoms bear pods, called *□* (pronounced *tuōluò*), which can cure drowsiness.

There is a beast here, resembling a field mouse but with striped brows—*□* (a variant of *huǐ*, though the creature remains obscure). It is called *□* (pronounced *nà*, sometimes rendered as *bear*), and eating it cures goiter.

Twenty *li* east lies Mount Lì’ér. Its heights are dense with oak and *lì* trees (pronounced *lì*). This tree has square stems and round leaves, yellow and downy, with fruit like the *liàn* tree (a type of tree; its seeds resemble fingertips, white and sticky, used for washing clothes—pronounced *liàn*, sometimes written as *jiǎn*). Consuming it sharpens memory.

Fifteen *li* east stands Mount Qúzhū. Its heights are thick with bamboo. The Qúzhū River flows southward from here into the Yellow River. Its waters teem with *háoyú* (variant note: shaped like the *wěi* fish, which resembles the *zhān*), with red beaks and red-feathered tails, which can cure white scaling.

Thirty-five *li* east rises Mount Cōnglóng. Its valleys are vast, rich in white chalk, black-green, and yellow chalk (indicating multicolored chalk).

Fifteen *li* east lies Mount Wō (pronounced *wō*). Its heights yield red copper, its northern slopes iron.

Seventy *li* east stands Mount Tuōhù. Here grows a grass resembling mallow leaves with red blossoms, its pods like *□* pods (modern *□* pods resemble *zàojiá*). Called *zhíchǔ*, it can cure *shǔ* (a wasting disease; as the *Huainanzi* states, "fox heads cure *shǔ*"). Eating it prevents insomnia.

Twenty *li* east rises Mount Jīnxīng. It abounds in *tiānyīng*, shaped like dragon bones, which can cure *cuó* (boils and sores).

Seventy *li* east lies Mount Tàiwēi. Within it is a valley called Xiāo Gorge, rich in iron (some editions omit "valley").

Fifteen *li* east stands Mount Jiāng Gorge (or in some editions, *Tán Gorge*), where red copper is plentiful.

One hundred twenty *li* east rises Mount Wúlín, dense with *jiān* grass (also written *jiān*).

Thirty *li* north lies Mount Niúshǒu. (Modern Chang’an’s southwest has a Mount Niúshǒu, with a bend above and water below, though it is unclear if this is the same.) Here grows *guǐ* grass, its leaves like mallow with red stems, its tassels like grain. Consuming it dispels sorrow. The Láo River flows westward from here into the Qīng River (pronounced like *zhé*, meaning "to deceive").

The waters teem with flying fish, shaped like *fù* fish, which cure hemorrhoids.

Forty *li* north rises Mount Huò. (Modern Pingyang’s Yong’an County, Lujiang’s Jì County, Jin’an’s Luójiāng County, and Henan’s Gǒng County all have Mount Huò, for mountains named Huò are not unique. As the *Erya* states: "A great mountain encircling a lesser one is called Huò.") Its woods are rich in grain. There is a beast here, resembling a leopard but with a white tail and mane, called *nǎonǎo*. Raising it can dispel sorrow (pronounced *pǔzhū*, meaning "to keep and nurture").

Fifty-two *li* north lies Mount Hégǔ, dense with *yánjí* (obscure; pronounced *zhān*).

Thirty-five *li* north lies Mount Yīn, also called Mount Xiǎn. It abounds in whetstones and patterned stones. (Whetstones are those used for grinding.) A small stream flows from here, and it teems with *diāotáng* trees, whose leaves resemble elm leaves but are square, and whose fruit resembles red beans. (Beans are called *shū*.) Consuming it cures deafness.

Four hundred *li* northeast stands Mount Gǔdèng, rich in red copper. There is a grass here called *róng* grass, with leaves like willow and a root like a chicken’s egg. Eating it cures wind disorders.

From the beginning of the Bó Mountains, from Mount Gānzǎo to Mount Gǔdèng, there are fifteen mountains spanning six thousand six hundred and seventy *li*. (This is the domain of the *Lì’ér* clan.) Their sacrificial rites require: for the animal offering, the full *tàiláo* ritual implements; for the hanging, auspicious jade. (*Xiàn* is the name for mountain sacrifices, as recorded in the *Erya*.) For the remaining thirteen mountains, the animal offering is a single sheep, and the hanging uses a *sāngfēng*—a mulberry tablet, square at the base and tapered at the top, with a hole in the center adorned with metal. (This refers to crafting a spirit tablet for worship, decorated with gold or silver. As the *Gōngyáng Zhuàn* states: "The tablet for Yu the Great was made of mulberry." *Zhǔ* may also be written as *wáng*.)

The second section of the Central Mountains, the Jǐ Mountains, begins with Mount Huīzhū. Its heights are dense with mulberry trees, its beasts with *lǘ* deer, and its birds with *hé*.

The creature resembles a pheasant but bears patterns, its plumage blue-green and feathered. Fierce and valiant, it fights to the death. Its cry resembles *yì*. It emerges from Shangdang.

Two hundred *li* to the southwest lies Mount Fāshì. Its heights abound in gold and jade, its depths in whetstones. The waters of Jíyú Spring arise here, flowing into the Yi River.

Three hundred *li* to the west stands Mount Háo. Its heights teem with gold and jade, yet bear no grass or trees.

Three hundred *li* farther west rises Mount Xiān. Gold and jade fill its slopes, but no grass or trees grow there. The Xiān River originates here, flowing northward into the Yi. Within its waters dwell *míng* serpents, shaped like snakes but with four wings. Their cries ring like chime-stones; where they appear, great drought befalls the land.

Three hundred *li* to the west lies Mount Yáng. Its slopes are stony, devoid of grass or trees. The Yáng River emerges here, coursing north into the Yi. Within it lurk *huà* serpents, their forms like human faces on jackal bodies, with bird wings and serpentine movement. Their voices resound like shouts; where they appear, floods ravage the land.

Two hundred *li* to the west stands Mount Kūnwú. Its heights yield abundant red copper—this may be the famed copper, fiery-hued as flame. Forged into blades, it cuts jade as if slicing mud. The Western Rong presented it to King Mu of Zhou. As the *Shīzǐ* records, these were the swords of Kūnwú. The *Yuèjuéshū* states: "When Mount Chìjīn split, tin emerged; when the valley of Ruòyē dried, copper appeared. Ōuyězǐ used them to forge the *tōnggōu* swords." A bronze sword unearthed from a tomb in Jí Prefecture measured three *chǐ* and five *cùn*—now called the *Gānjiāng* sword. The Jí region also lacked iron, proving that ancient weapons were made by alloying copper with tin.

A beast dwells here, its form like a hedgehog’s but horned, its cry like a human’s wailing. Named *lóngchí*, it was mentioned earlier—likely the same creature. Consuming its flesh prevents nightmares.

One hundred and twenty *li* to the west lies Mount Jiān (sound uncertain). The Jiān River rushes forth, flowing north into the Yi. Its heights brim with gold and jade, its depths with green *xiónghuáng*. A tree grows here, its form like a crabapple but with red leaves, called *máng* grass (sound *wàng*). It can poison fish.

One hundred and fifty *li* farther west stands Mount Dúsū. Barren of grass and trees, it abounds in water.

Two hundred *li* to the west rises Mount Mànqú. Its heights yield *jīnyú*, its depths bamboo arrows. The Yi River originates here, flowing east into the Luo. (Now, the Yi rises northeast of Xióng’ěr Mountain in Lúshǐ County of Shàngluò, entering the Luo at Luòyáng in Hénán.)

A beast inhabits this place, named *mǎfù*, its form like a human face on a tiger’s body. Its cry resembles an infant’s wail, and it devours humans.

In all, the first section of the Jǐ Mountains—from Mount Huīzhū to Mount Mànqú—comprises nine mountains spanning one thousand six hundred and seventy *li*. Their spirits bear human faces and avian bodies. For sacrifices, animal offerings are used, selected by fur color. A single auspicious jade is cast, without *xǔ* grain.

The third section of the Central Mountains, the Pín Mountains, begins with Mount Áo’àn (or *Xiànbèi*, sound *bèi*). Its southern slopes yield *fúfū* jade, its northern slopes ochre and yellow gold. The spirit Xūnchí dwells here, often producing fine jade—sometimes from stone. Gazing northward toward the Hé forests, their forms resemble *qiàn* and *jǔ* trees. (Commentators say *qiàn* and *jǔ* are tree names, though details remain unclear. *Qiàn* is pronounced *qiàn*.)

A beast roams here, its form like a white deer but with four antlers, named *fūzhū*. Where it appears, floods engulf the land.

Ten *li* to the east stands Mount Qīngyào, the true secret capital of the Thearch. It is the hidden city of the Celestial Emperor.

Gazing northward toward the bend of the Hé, where the river winds a thousand *li*—one curve, one straight stretch. Here abound *jià* birds, though their nature remains unclear. Some say *jià* should be *jiā*, meaning "goose." *Jiā* is pronounced *jiā*.

Looking south toward Dànzhǔ, a small islet in the waters (Dàn is pronounced *chán*). This is where Yǔ’s father transformed—Gǔn was metamorphosed in the Yǔ Abyss into a yellow bear. Yet here it is said again, suggesting that one possessed of mutable and wondrous nature may transform anywhere without limit. Here also thrive *pú-lěi* and *pú-lú*. *Pú-lěi* are snails; the *Ěryǎ* states: *pú-lú* are *mínglíng*.

Wǔluó presides over this place—Wǔluó being the name of the spirit. The graph is the character for "spirit." His form is human-faced but marked with leopard stripes, slender-waisted with white teeth. Some texts write "head" instead of "teeth." His ears are pierced with *jù*, a name for vessels of gold and silver, though details remain unclear. *Jù* is pronounced *qú*. His cry rings like jade pendants chiming, as if a man’s voice sounding jade ornaments.

This mountain is fitting for women. The Zhěn River issues forth here (Zhěn is pronounced *zhěn*) and flows northward into the Hé. Within it dwells a bird named *yǎo* (pronounced like the *yǎo* in *yǎotiǎo*). Its form resembles a wild duck, with a green body, vermilion eyes, and a crimson tail. Eating it promotes fertility. (Vermilion here denotes a pale red.)

There is also a grass here, its form like *jiān* (a kind of rush, resembling a sprout), with square stems, yellow flowers, and red fruit. Its base resembles *gǎo* stalks, its roots like *gé* roots—also a fragrant herb. It is called *xún* grass, or sometimes *bāo* grass. Consuming it enhances a woman’s beauty, making her even more radiant.

Further east ten *li* stands Mount Guī (pronounced *wēi*). At its summit grow fine jujubes, and in its northern shade lies □-fù jade. The Zhènghuí River issues forth here, flowing northward into the Hé. Within it abound flying fish, their form like a suckling pig marked with red patterns. Consuming them dispels fear of thunder and may be used to repel weapons.

Further east forty *li* rises Mount Yísū. Its heights abound in gold and jade, its lower slopes in *mànjū* trees (meaning unclear). The Yōngyōng River (pronounced *róng*) emerges here, flowing northward into the Hé, where many yellow cowries are found.

Further east twenty *li* stands Mount Hé. Its soil bears no grass or trees but is rich in fine jade and azure jasper. In truth, it is the Nine Capitals of the Hé—where the nine rivers converge, hence the name Nine Capitals. This mountain winds in five bends, each bend coiling fivefold. The nine rivers issue forth here, merging as they flow northward into the Hé. Within them lie many dark jades.

The auspicious spirit Tàiféng presides over it (auspicious meaning propitious). His form resembles a man with a tiger’s tail (or, in some versions, a mouth-tail). He delights in dwelling on the southern slopes of Mount Fù, and his comings and goings are accompanied by light. The spirit Tàiféng stirs the qi of heaven and earth—his numinous power can summon clouds and rain.

In summer, at the mouth of... beneath Mount Fù, a great wind arose, plunging the sky into darkness. Kǒng Jiǎ lost his way... (see *Lüshi Chunqiu*).

From the head of Mount Fù—beginning at Mount Áo’àn and extending to Mount Hé—there are five mountains spanning four hundred forty *li*.

The sacrifices to the spirits Tàiféng, Xūnchí, and Wǔluó each require one male sheep, its bones crushed and scattered in offering (副副 refers to breaking the sheep’s bones and scattering them as ritual fragments; see *Rites of Zhou*, pronounced as the sincerity of kǔnbì). Fine jade is used for the main offering. For the secondary spirits, one rooster is buried, with xú-grain from Xú as the sacrificial grain.

From *The Fourth Classic of the Central Mountains* (音狸, pronounced lí):
The first mountain is called Lùtí. Its summit abounds in jade, its base in gold. The Gān River issues forth here, flowing northward into the Luò. Within it lie many *líng*-stones (未闻也; *líng* may also be written as *tú*).

Fifty *li* to the west stands Fúzhū Mountain, rich in *ruǎn*-stone (音耎; modern Yànmén Mountain also yields *ruǎn*-stone, white as ice, some with red veins). There dwells a beast resembling a raccoon dog but with human eyes (貉 or an ancient variant). Its name is *Yín* (音银, sometimes written as 麋). The Hào River flows from here, northward into the Luò, bearing many *ruǎn*-stones (said to emerge from water).

One hundred twenty *li* farther west lies Lí Mountain. Its southern slopes yield jade, its northern slopes *sōu* (音搜; *máo-sōu* is today’s madder grass). A beast dwells here, ox-like with a gray body, its cry like an infant’s—it devours humans and is called the Xīqú. The Yōngyōng River flows from here, southward into the Yī. Another beast, named *Xié* (音苍颉之颉), resembles a shaggy dog but with scales, its fur like a reversed mane.

Two hundred *li* west stands Jīwěi Mountain, with many valleys and *xú*-stones, its heights rich in *fúfú*-jade.

Two hundred fifty *li* farther west rises Qī Mountain, its summit laden with jade, its base with copper. The Tāodiāo River flows from here, northward into the Luò, bearing many *xián*-sheep. A tree grows here, resembling the *chū* in form, its leaves like the tung tree’s but bearing pods—it is called *bá* and can poison fish (*bá* may also be written as *ài*).

Two hundred *li* west lies Báibiān Mountain, its heights rich in gold and jade, its base in dark realgar.

Two hundred *li* farther west stands Xióng’ěr Mountain (now south of Shàngluò County). Its summit abounds in lacquer trees, its base in *qiū* (a type of oak). The Fúháo River flows from here, westward into the Luò, bearing many water-jades and *rén*-fish. A herb grows here, resembling the *sū* but with red blossoms, called *tíngníng* (亭宁, pronounced *tíng* or *níng*). It can poison fish.

Three hundred *li* west rises Zhuàng Mountain, its heights rich in patterned stone, its base in arrow bamboo and *zhú*-bamboo. Its beasts include *lí*-oxen and *xián*-sheep; its birds, many red *jīng* (音闭, the pheasant *biē*).

Three hundred fifty *li* farther west stands Huānjǔ Mountain. The Luò River flows from here, northeastward into the Xuánhù River, bearing many *mǎcháng*-creatures. These two mountains lie between the Luò (the Luò now rises in Zhǒnglǐng Mountain of Shàngluò County). As the *River Chart* states: “Xuánhù at the confluence of the Luò,” referring to this region.

In all, the mountains from Lùtí to Xuánhù number nine, spanning one thousand six hundred seventy *li*. Their spirits all have human faces and beast bodies. Their sacrifices require a white rooster, offered in prayer without *xú*-grain (meaning only supplication is made). The rooster is adorned with colored silks (彩衣 refers to decorating the bird with dyed fabrics).

From *The Fifth Classic of the Central Mountains*:
The first of the Bó Mountains is Gǒuchuáng (or Gǒulín Mountain). It bears no grass or trees, only strange stones (resembling jade; as the *Book* says: “Lead pines and strange stones”).

Three hundred *li* east stands Shǒu Mountain. Its northern slopes abound in oak and *gǔ* trees, its grasses in *jì*-thistles and *yuán*-blossoms (*jì* is the mountain thistle; *yuán*-blossoms are used in medicine). Its southern slopes yield *fúfú*-jade, its trees many locust trees. To the north lies a valley called Jī Valley, home to many *dì*-birds (音如钳釱之釱), resembling owls but with three eyes and ears, their cries like *lù*—eating them cures swelling (未闻).

Three hundred *li* east stands Xiànzhú Mountain (音如斤斸之冲). It bears no grass or trees, only patterned stone.

Three hundred *li* east lies Cōnglóng Mountain, barren of grass and trees but rich in *qiū*-stone (未详).

Five hundred *li* northeast stands Tiáogǔ Mountain. Its trees include locust and tung, its grasses peony and *kuàndōng* (the *Classic of Herbs* calls it *mǎndōng*; now written as 门, commonly as 耳).

Ten *li* north stands Chāo Mountain. Its northern slopes abound in dark jade; its southern side holds a well, full in winter but dry in summer.

Five hundred *li* east rises Chénghóu Mountain. Its heights bear many *chūn* trees (resembling *chū*, used for chariot shafts; in Wú dialect, *chūn* is pronounced like *chūn*-chariot or *yóu*-chariot), its grasses many *péng*.

Five hundred *li* east stands Cháogē Mountain, its valleys rich in fine chalk.

Five hundred *li* east lies Huái Mountain, its valleys abundant in gold and tin.

Ten *li* east stands Lì Mountain, its trees many locusts, its southern slopes rich in jade.

Ten *li* farther east lies [unnamed] Mountain, bearing dark jade and many *jīng*-deer (small, black deer).

The Shī River flows from here, southward into the Luò, bearing many fine jades.

Ten *li* east stands Liángyú Mountain. Its heights abound in oak and *gǔ* trees, but no stone. The Yú River flows from its northern slopes, northward into the Hé. The Rǔ River flows from its southern slopes, southeastward into the Luò.

Ten *li* southeast stands Gǔwěi Mountain, rich in whetstones and red copper. The Lóngyú River flows from here, southeastward into the Luò.

Twenty *li* northeast rises Shēng Mountain. Its trees include oak, *gǔ*, and thorns; its grasses, *shǔyǔ*, *huì*, and other fragrant herbs.

Ten *li* farther east stands a mountain abundant in *kòutuō* grass. This herb grows in the southern lands, reaching a height of over a zhang, its leaves resembling those of the lotus, its stem filled with pith of pure white. The fairest of women cultivate and irrigate it daily, treating it as a tree. The Yellow-Acid River flows from here, northward into the Hé. Within its waters lie many *xuán*-jade, stones second only to true jade. Master Xun said: *"Xuan-yao pearls remain unstrung."* (Pronounced *xuán*.)

Twelve *li* farther east rises Yángxū Mountain, rich in metal and overlooking the Xuánhù Waters. The *River Chart* states: *"When Cang Jie accompanied the Emperor on his southern inspection tour, he ascended Yángxū Mountain and gazed upon the confluence of the Xuánhù and Luò Rivers. A divine turtle emerged from these waters, bearing upon its shell red and green characters, which it bestowed upon him."*

From the first mountain of the Bó range, Gǒulín Mountain, to Yángxū Mountain, there are in total sixteen mountains spanning two thousand nine hundred and eighty-two *li*. Shēng Mountain is the burial mound of the range; its sacrificial rites require a grand offering of the finest jade. The first mountain of the range is its head; its sacrifices involve black millet, a black sacrificial victim, and a grand offering of *niè*-fermented ale (ale brewed with *niè*-malt). For the dance, a thousand mouths perform the myriad dances, drums set in place and struck to accompany the movements. The offering requires a single jade disc.

The Shī River unites Heaven and Earth, serving as the conduit for celestial spirits. A fattened sacrificial victim is offered: a black dog above, a hen below, a ewe slit open, and blood presented in libation. (To *cì* is to cut open.) As the *Rites of Zhou* states: *"The *cì*-officiant presents the canine sacrifice."* The offering requires fine jade, adorned with silk and further embellished with colored silks. The feast is held to encourage and strengthen the spirits.

The *Rites of the Special Sacrificial Victim and the Offering of Food* states: "To hold the libation vessel is the act of the invocator offering the feast."

The *Central Mountains: Sixth Section* records: The first mountain of the Gǎodī range is called Píngféng Mountain. To its south lie the Yī and Luò Rivers; to its east, the Gǔchéng Mountains, situated west of Jìběi’s Gǔchéng County. The Yellow Stone Elder’s stone rests beneath this mountain, where Zhang Liang retrieved it for burial. The mountain bears no grass or trees, no water, only abundant sand and stone. There dwells a spirit whose form resembles a human with two heads, named *Jiāochóng*. It is the stinging insect, chief of all stinging creatures, and the very hive of bees and honey. This refers to the gathering place of swarms, for *mì* (honey) is also the name of the bee. For its sacrifice, a single rooster is used, offered in exorcism but not killed. *Ráng* is also a name for sacrifice, meaning to ward off malign qi.

Ten *li* to the west lies Gǎodī Mountain. It has no grass or trees but abounds in metal and jade. Another ten *li* west stands Guī Mountain (pronounced like *xiāng* in *xiāngkǒu*). Its northern slopes yield *fú*-jade. To its west lies a valley called Guàn Gorge, where many willows and paper mulberries grow. Within it dwells a bird resembling a pheasant with a long tail, red as cinnabar flame and with a blue beak, named *líng* (pronounced *líng* or *yāo*). Its call is its own name, and wearing it prevents nightmares.

Jiāoshāng’s waters issue from its southern slopes, flowing south into the Luò River. Yúsuí’s waters emerge from its northern slopes, flowing north into the Gǔ River. Thirty *li* farther west stands Zhānzhū Mountain. Its southern slopes yield metal; its northern slopes, patterned stone. The Xiè River (pronounced *xiè*) rises here, flowing southeast into the Luò. The Shǎo River emerges from its northern slopes, flowing east into the Gǔ River.

Thirty *li* west lies Lóuzhuó Mountain. It has no grass or trees but abounds in metal and jade. The Zhān River rises on its southern slopes, flowing east into the Luò. The Bēi River emerges from its northern slopes, known in the world as the Bǎidá River, flowing north into the Gǔ. Within it lie many purple and patterned stones.

Forty *li* west stands Báishí Mountain. The Huì River rises on its southern slopes, flowing south into the Luò, and abounds in water-jade. The Jiàn River emerges from its northern slopes—*The Book [of Documents]* states: "The Yī, Luò, Chán, and Jiàn"—flowing northwest into the Gǔ. Within it lie many *mí*-stones and *lúdān* (furnace cinnabar), both of unknown nature.

Fifty *li* west lies Gǔ Mountain. Its heights abound in grain; its lower slopes, in mulberries. The Shuǎng River rises here, known in the world as Zhùmá Gorge, flowing northwest into the Gǔ. Within it lies much *bìlǜ* (green jasper).

Seventy-two *li* west stands Mì Mountain. (Today, Mì County in Xíngyáng also has a Mì Mountain, but this is likely not the same.) Its southern slopes yield jade; its northern slopes, iron. The Háo River rises here, flowing south into the Luò. Within it dwell many *xuán* turtles, whose forms have raven heads and turtle tails, and whose cries resemble the splitting of wood. It has no grass or trees.

A hundred *li* west stands Chángshí Mountain. It has no grass or trees but abounds in metal and jade. To its west lies a valley called Gòng Gorge, where many bamboos grow. The Gòng River rises here, flowing southwest into the Luò. Within it lie many *míng* stones. In the first year of the *Yǒngkāng* reign of Jìn, Xiāngyáng Commandery presented a *míng* stone, resembling jade in color, whose ringing sound carried seven or eight *li*. Today, in Quánlíng County of Línglíng in Língzhōu, there are two *míng* stones, one shaped like a drum, hence its popular name, "Stone Drum." These belong to the same type.

A hundred and forty *li* west stands Fù Mountain. It has no grass or trees but abounds in *yáobì* (precious jade). The Yànrǎn River rises on its southern slopes, flowing south into the Luò. Within it dwell many *rényú* (human-fish). To its west lies a forest called Pānzhǒng (pronounced *fān*). The Gǔ River rises here, flowing east into the Luò. (Today, the Gǔ River rises in Gǔyáng Valley, flowing northeast through Gǔchéng County into the Luò.) Within it lies much *yān* jade, though its nature remains unknown. *Yān* is pronounced *yīn*.

Further west fifty *li* stands Qì Mountain. Its trees abound in *chū* (ailanthus), and many □ trees. Today in Shǔ, there are □ trees: in the seventh and eighth months, they put forth ears, and when the ears form, they appear as if dusted with salt powder, suitable for vinegar broth. Pronounced *bèi*. Its southern slopes yield much gold and jade; its northern slopes, much iron and *xiāo* (mugwort). *Xiāo* is *hāo* (wormwood). See the *Ěryǎ*. The Tuó River rises here, flowing north into the Yellow River. Within it dwell many *xiūpì* fish, shaped like □□, a kind of frog. They have white beaks, their cries resemble an owl’s, and eating them cures white *xiǎn* (tinea).

Ninety *li* further west stands Chángzhēng Mountain. It has no grass or trees but abounds in *è* (chalk). The Qí River rises here, pronounced *qiáo*, flowing northeast into the Yellow River. Within it lies much *cāng* jade. The Zī River also rises here, flowing north into the Yellow River.

Ninety *li* further west stands Kuāfù Mountain. Its trees abound in □ and *nán* (nanmu); its bamboo, in *jiàn* (arrow bamboo). Its beasts include many □ cattle and *xián* sheep; its birds, many red *bì* (pheasants). Its southern slopes yield much jade; its northern slopes, much iron. To its north lies a forest called Táolín, spanning three hundred *li* in breadth. Within it dwell many horses. Today, Táolín is in the southern valley of Quēxiāng in Hóngnóng’s Hú County, rich in wild horses, mountain goats, and mountain cattle. The Hú River rises here, flowing north into the Yellow River, and within it lies much *yān* jade.

Ninety *li* further west stands Yánghuá Mountain. Its southern slopes yield much gold and jade; its northern slopes, much *qīng xiónghuáng* (green realgar). Its grasses include many *shǔyǔ* and *kǔshēn*, the latter resembling *sù* (willow) in form—this is the *jiū* character. Its fruit resembles a melon, its taste sour-sweet, and eating it cures malaria. The Yáng River rises here, flowing southwest into the Luò. Within it dwell many *rényú* (human-fish). The Mén River also rises here, flowing northeast into the Yellow River. Within it lies much *xuán*□, black whetstone born in the water. The Jiégū River rises on its northern slopes, pronounced *jiè*, flowing east into the Mén River. Its heights yield much copper. The Mén River, upon reaching the Yellow River, flows one hundred ninety *li* into the Luò.

In all, the peaks of Gǎodī Mountain range from Píngféng Mountain to Yánghuá Mountain, comprising fourteen mountains over seven hundred ninety *li*. The sacred peak lies among them; it is sacrificed to in the sixth month, the middle of the year. Following the sacrificial rites of the other sacred peaks, the realm will know peace.

The first peak of the *Zhōng Cì Qī Jīng* (Central Seventh Classic), Kǔ Mountain, is called Xiūyǔ Mountain. Variant: *Yú* (舆). Its summit bears a stone named the Emperor’s Terrace Chess, for Dìtái, a divine being. *Chess* refers to *bóqí* (a board game). It is five-colored and patterned, resembling a quail’s egg. The Emperor’s Terrace Stone is used to pray to the hundred spirits. In sacrificial rites, this stone is employed for the hundred spirits. Wearing it prevents *gǔ* (possession sickness).

There is a grass here resembling *shī* (yarrow), with red leaves and a clumping base, called *sùtiáo*. It can be made into *gǎn* (arrow shafts). Three hundred *li* east stands Gǔzhōng Mountain, where Dìtái feasts the hundred spirits. Celebratory banquets are held on this mountain, hence its name, Gǔzhōng (Drum and Bell) Mountain. There is a grass here with square stems and yellow leaves, round leaves arranged in three layers, called *niǎosuān*. It can be used to make poison or medicine. Its heights abound in *lì* (whetstone); its depths, in *dǐ* (fine whetstone).

Two hundred *li* further east stands Gūyáo Mountain.

The name is pronounced *Yáo*, and some omit the character *shān* (mountain). The Divine Maiden died here, and her name was Nǚshī (Woman-Corpse). She transformed into a grass called □, its leaves layered in pairs—meaning the leaves grow in overlapping pairs; the pronunciation is also *yáo*. Its flowers are yellow, and its fruit resembles *tùqiū* (*tùsī*, dodder, as noted in the *Erya*). Consuming it makes one beloved by others, for it renders a person endearing. As the tradition states: "One who takes it becomes so charming." It is also called *huāngfū cǎo* (Wild Man’s Grass).

Twenty *li* further east stands Kǔ Mountain. There is a beast here named Shāngāo, resembling a *zhú* (piglet, here meaning *tún*, pig). Its color is as red as cinnabar fire, and it is fond of cursing. Above it grows a tree called Huángjí (Yellow Thorn), with yellow flowers and round leaves. Its fruit resembles *lán* (eupatorium), and consuming it prevents childbirth—*zì* meaning "to give birth," as in the *Yijing*: "A woman’s steadfastness lies in not bearing children." There is also a grass here with round leaves and no stem, red flowers but no fruit, called Wútiáo (Stemless). Consuming it prevents goiter.

Twenty-seven *li* further east stands Dǔ Mountain, where the spirit Tiānyú resides. Strange winds and rains often occur here. Above it grows a tree called Tiānbiān (Heavenly Whip), pronounced *biān*. Its stem is square and resembles *kuí* (mallow). Those who consume it do not □—meaning they do not choke when eating.

Fifty-two *li* further east stands Fànggāo Mountain. *Fàng* may also be written as *xiào* (to imitate) or *mù* (to herd). The Bright Water originates here, flowing south into the Yi River. Within it abounds dark jade.

There grows a tree whose leaves resemble the *huái* (locust tree), with yellow flowers but no fruit, called Méng Wood. Consuming it prevents confusion.

There is a beast whose form resembles a bee, with a branching tail and a reversed tongue, skilled at calling—fond of crying out. Its name is Wénwén.

Fifty-seven *li* further east stands Dà□ Mountain. It abounds in □fú jade and *mí* jade. [Term unclear.]

There grows a grass whose leaves resemble the *yú* (elm), with a square stem and dark green wounds, called Niúshāng (Ox Thorn), akin to "ox thorn." Its roots bear dark veins. Consuming it prevents *jué*—a condition of rebellious *qi*. It can be used to temper weapons of *jīng* wood. The Yángkuáng Water originates here, flowing southwest into the Yi River. Within it abound three-legged turtles.

It is said that in Yángxiàn County of Wúxīng, there is a Jun Mountain, atop which lies a pool. In its waters dwell three-legged, large-eyed turtles. Tortoises with three legs are called treasures in the *Ěryā*. Those who consume them suffer no grave illnesses and may cure swelling.

Seventy *li* further east stands Bànshí Mountain. Atop it grows a grass that sprouts and flourishes, standing over a *zhàng* tall, with red leaves and red flowers, though the flowers bear no fruit. When it first sprouts, it forms a spike, then produces leaves, with flowers growing amid the spikes. It is called Jiāróng (Fine Glory). Consuming it prevents *tíng*—meaning one need not fear thunderclaps or lightning. Pronounced *tíng*, as in *tíngbó*.

The waters of Láixū emerge from its southern slopes and flow westward into the Yi River. Within them abound *lúnyú* fish—*lún* in pronunciation—marked with black patterns. Their form resembles the crucian carp, and those who consume them suffer no drowsiness.

The Hé River emerges from its northern slopes and flows northward into the Luo. It teems with *téngyú* fish—*téng* in pronunciation—whose shape resembles the mandarin fish, dwelling in waterways. Mandarin fish have canine-like eyes, large mouths, fine scales, and mottled colors. The term *kuí* refers to interconnected waterway tunnels—*guì* in pronunciation. These fish bear dark markings and red tails; those who eat them suffer no abscesses and may treat fistulas. Fistulas are a type of abscess. The waters also host many insects. The *Huainanzi* states: "Chicken’s head cures fistulas"—*lòu* in pronunciation.

Fifty *li* further east stands Shaoshi Mountain. Today, it lies west of Yangcheng in Henan, commonly called Qin’s Chamber. A hundred grasses and trees grow in dense clusters—though the meaning remains unclear. Atop it grows a tree called *Dìxiū* (Imperial Repose), its leaves resembling the poplar. Its branches fork into five paths, meaning its limbs intertwine and overlap in five directions, evoking the image of crossroads. The *Li Sao* states: "The floating duckweed spans nine thoroughfares." It bears yellow flowers and black fruit; those who consume it feel no anger. The summit abounds in jade, and near the peak lies a white jade nectar. Those who obtain and consume it attain the Dao of immortality, though the world’s people cannot ascend. The *Shi Han Shenwu* records this. Below the mountain lies abundant iron.

The Xiū River emerges here and flows northward into the Luo. Within it dwell many *zhōuyú* fish—*zhōu* in pronunciation—whose form resembles the *zhōu* pheasant (though the details remain unclear). They have long spurs, white feet that face each other—though the meaning is uncertain. Those who eat them suffer no *gǔ* illnesses and may repel weapons.

Thirty *li* further east stands Taishi Mountain, the central peak of Mount Song, now located west of Yangcheng County. Atop it grows a tree whose leaves resemble the pear but bear red veins, called *yǔmù* (Lush Wood)—*yù* in pronunciation. Those who consume it feel no jealousy. There also grows a grass resembling *gé* (though it appears similar to *jiào*). It bears white flowers and black fruit, its sheen like that of the grape—smooth and lustrous. Called *méi* grass, those who consume it remain unconfused.

The summit holds many fine stones, second only to jade. The stone into which Qimu transformed and gave birth to Qi stands on this mountain, as recorded in the *Huainanzi*.

Thirty *li* further north stands Jiang Mountain. Its summit abounds in jade, *zhè* (cudrania), and *bǎi* (cypress). There grows a tree called *Dìwū* (Imperial Chamber), its leaves resembling the pepper plant. Its fruit, though red, bears downward-curving thorns—meaning the spines hook downward. It may repel misfortune.

Thirty *li* further north stands Yingliang Mountain. Its summit holds much dark jade, embedded in black stone—meaning the dark jade grows alongside the black stone. Some say *chúnyú* is a musical instrument resembling a *wèi* (a mythical ape) head.

Thirty *li* further east stands Fuxi Mountain. There grows a tree whose leaves resemble the *chū* (ailanthus) but bear red fruit, called *kàngmù* (Lofty Wood). Those who consume it suffer no *gǔ* illnesses.

The Sì River emerges here and flows northward into the Yellow River. To its east lies a valley named Shegu (Snake Valley), so called because it produces snakes. The summit holds much *shǎoxīn* (slender *Asarum*).

Forty *li* further east stands Shaoxing Mountain. There grows a grass called *gāngcǎo* (Stalwart Herb)—*gāng* in pronunciation—its leaves resembling the mallow but with red stems, white flowers, and fruit like grapes. Those who consume it grow not foolish, for it sharpens the mind.

The Qínán River emerges here—some texts write it as *Xiāo*.

Flowing northward, it empties into the Yi River—some texts write it as *Qin*.

Ten *li* to the southeast stands Tai Mountain. There exists also a lesser Eastern Tai Mountain, now located in Zhuxu County, from which the Wen River emerges; this may not be the same. There grows a grass called *lí* (Pear Grass), its leaves resembling *dí* (reed)—also a type of mugwort—with a pronunciation like *dí*. Its red flowers can cure ulcers. The Tai River emerges from its southern slope, flowing southeast into the Mo River. The Cheng River emerges from its northern slope, flowing northeast into the Mo. The world calls it Jing Stream.

Twenty *li* further east stands Mo Mountain. Its summit holds much red gold. The Mo River emerges here, flowing north into the Mo—*Shui Jing* writes it as *Mei*.

Twenty-five *li* further east stands Yi Mountain. Its summit holds much white gold and iron. The Yi River emerges here, flowing north into the Yellow River.

Thirty-five *li* further east stands Min Mountain. Atop it grows a tree resembling the *kè* (carved wood), bearing white flowers and red fruit, called *jiào* (Cold-Resistant)—pronounced *jì*. The *bǎi* (cypress) tree: those who consume it suffer no cold, for it makes one resistant to chill. Its southern slopes hold much *fú* jade.

Thirty *li* further east stands Da Gui Mountain. Today, in Mi County of Rongyang, there is a Da Gui Mountain, from which the Gou River emerges—*gui* pronounced *guī*. Its northern slopes hold much iron, fine jade, and green chalk. There grows a grass resembling *shī* (yarrow) but with hair, bearing blue flowers and white fruit, called *láng*—pronounced *láng*. Some write it as *ǎo*. Those who consume it do not die young, for it grants full longevity. It can also cure abdominal ailments—*wèi* means "to treat"; some write it as *yǐ*.

The entirety of the *Ku* Mountains’ range, from Mount Xiuyu to Mount Da Gui, comprises nineteen mountains spanning one thousand one hundred and eighty-four *li*. Of its sixteen spirits, all possess the body of a boar and a human face. Their sacrifices require: a *maoquan* offering of one sheep as *xiū* (delicacy)—meaning the sheep is presented as an offering. The *ying* (ornamental jade) is buried with *zao* jade, which is jade of five colors. Some say it is used to contain the jade, while *zao* refers to the mat beneath. The mountains *Ku*, *Shaoshi*, and *Taishi* are all ancestral mounds. Their sacrifices demand the full array of a *tailao* (grand offering), with *ying* adorned in auspicious jade. Their spirits all appear as human-faced with three heads, while the rest of their kind bear boar bodies and human faces.

The *Zhong Ci Ba Jing* begins with the *Jing* Mountains. Mount Jing now lies within the borders of Nanjun. Its heights abound in gold and jade, and its woods are rich in *zhu* and sandalwood—*zhu* pronounced like the *zhu* in "pillar." The Ju River originates here—*Ju* pronounced like the *ju* in "carbuncle"—flowing southeast into the Jiang. Today, the Ju River rises in Weichang County of Xincheng, east of Mount Fa’e, then flows south to enter the Jiang at Zhijiang County in Nanjun. Its waters hold much cinnabar grain and many *wen* fish, which bear patterned markings. One hundred *li* northeast lies Mount Jing. It now stands south of Muxiang County in Xincheng. Its northern slopes hold much iron, its southern slopes much red gold, and its valleys many *mao* oxen—*mao* oxen are a kind of yak, black in color, found beyond the southwestern frontier—pronounced *li*, or alternatively *lai*. There are also many leopards and tigers. Its woods are rich in pine and cypress, its grasses in bamboo and many *ju* and *you*—*you* resembles the orange but is larger, with thick skin and a sour taste. The Zhang River originates here, flowing southeast into the Ju. It emerges from Mount Jing and enters the Ju at Dangyang County in Nanjun. Its waters hold much yellow gold and many *jiao* fish—*jiao* are akin to *fu* fish, their skin bearing pearl-like patterns and being tough, with tails three to four *chi* long, ending in a venomous sting that harms humans. Their hides may adorn sword hilts or be used to polish rhinoceros horn. They are also found in Linhai County—pronounced *jiao*. Its beasts include many *lü* deer.

The creature resembles a deer but is larger.

One hundred and fifty *li* northeast lies Mount Jiao. Its summit abounds in jade, its base in green *□*. Its woods teem with pine and cypress, and many *taozhi* and *gouduan* trees. The spirit *□wei* dwells there—*□* is pronounced like the *tuo* in *tuoyu* (alligator). Its form bears a human face, ram’s horns, and tiger’s claws, ever roaming the depths of the Ju and Zhang Rivers, the abyssal palace of those waters. Its comings and goings are accompanied by light.

One hundred and twenty *li* northeast lies Mount Nüji. Its summit abounds in jade, its base in yellow gold. Its beasts include many leopards and tigers, many *lü* deer and *jing* deer. The *ji* deer resembles a river deer but is larger, with coarse fur and leopard-like legs—pronounced *ji*. Its birds include many white *jiao*, which resemble pheasants but have long tails, running as they cry. There are many green *jiao*, many *di*, and many *□*. The *□* is as large as an eagle, with purple-green plumage, a long neck, and a red beak, feeding on the heads of vipers. The male is called *yunyue*, the female *yinxie*.

Two hundred *li* northeast lies Mount Yizhu. Its summit abounds in gold and jade, its base in green *□*. The Gui River originates here—pronounced *gui*—flowing south into the Zhang. Today, the Gui River rises in Donggui Mountain of Nanjun and enters the Yangtze at Huarong County. Its waters hold much white jade.

Three hundred and fifty *li* northeast lies Mount Lun—pronounced *lun*. Its woods teem with catalpa and nanmu, many *taozhi* trees, many *zuli* and chestnuts, and many orange and *you* trees. The *zu* resembles a pear but is sour and astringent. Its beasts include many *lüchen* and *ling□*. The *□* resembles a hare but has deer-like legs and is green—pronounced *chiluo*.

Two hundred *li* northeast lies Mount Lu□—pronounced like the *gui* in *guidui*. Its summit abounds in *fufu* jade, its base in chalk. Its woods teem with *niu* and *jiang* trees.

One hundred and thirty *li* east lies Mount Guang. Its summit abounds in azure stone, its base in timber. The spirit Jimeng dwells there, its form human-bodied with a dragon’s head, ever roaming the Zhang’s depths. Its comings and goings are always accompanied by whirlwinds and violent storms.

One hundred and fifty *li* east lies Mount Qi. Its southern slopes abound in red gold, its northern slopes in white *min*—a stone resembling jade—pronounced *min*. Its summit abounds in gold and jade, its base in green *□*. Its woods teem with *chu* trees. The spirit Shetuo dwells there—pronounced *tuhe*, or alternatively *tuoxiao*. Its form is human-bodied with a square face and three feet.

One hundred and thirty *li* east lies Mount Tong. Its summit abounds in gold, silver, and iron. Its woods teem with *guzuo*, *zu*, chestnuts, oranges, and *you*. Its beasts include many leopards.

One hundred *li* northeast lies Mount Mei. Its beasts include many *si* oxen, many *lüchen*, and many *shilu* deer. Its summit abounds in gold, its base in green *□*.

One hundred *li* northeast lies Mount Dayao. Its woods teem with pine and cypress, many catalpa and mulberry, and many *ji* trees. Its grasses include much bamboo. Its beasts include many leopards, tigers, and *ling□*.

Three hundred *li* northeast lies Mount Ling. Its summit abounds in gold and jade, its base in green *□*. Its woods teem with peach, plum, *mei*, and apricot trees. The *mei* resembles an apricot but is sour.

Seventy *li* northeast lies Mount Long, whose summit abounds in *yu* trees—parasitic trees, also called *wantong*, as recorded in the *Erya*. Its summit abounds in azure stone, its base in red tin. Its grasses include many *taozhi* and *gouduan*.

Fifty *li* southeast lies Mount Heng. Its summit abounds in *yu* trees and *guzuo*, much yellow and white chalk.

Seventy *li* southeast lies Mount Shi. Its summit abounds in gold, its base in green *□* and many *yu* trees.

One hundred and twenty *li* south lies Mount Ruo—also written as *Qian*. Its summit abounds in *fufu* jade and ochre—red earth. It holds much *guishi*, the nature of which is unclear. It also abounds in *yu* trees and *zhe* trees.

One hundred and twenty *li* southeast lies Mount Zhi. It holds many fine stones and many *zhe* trees.

One hundred and fifty *li* southeast lies Mount Yu. Its summit abounds in gold and jade, its base in azure stone and iron. Its woods teem with cypress.

Seventy *li* southeast lies Mount Huan. Its woods teem with sandalwood and many *bang* stones, and much white tin—now called white solder. The Yu River rises on its summit, flowing hidden beneath, and its waters hold much whetstone.

One hundred and fifty *li* northeast lies Mount Renju. Its woods teem with *guzuo*. Its southern slopes abound in red gold, its northern slopes in ochre.

Fifty *li* east lies Mount Shimei. Its southern slopes abound in whetstone, its northern slopes in green *□*. Its woods teem with cypress, sandalwood, and *zhe* trees. Its grasses include much bamboo.

Two hundred *li* southeast lies Mount Qingu. Its woods teem with *guzuo* and *jiaozhe*—*jiao* is a small, shrub-like tree that kills all plants beneath it. Its summit abounds in white *min*, its base in *xishi*. Its beasts include many *shilu* deer and many white rhinoceroses. Its birds include many *zhen*.

From the beginning of the Ke Mountains, from Mount Jing to Mount Qingu, there are twenty-three mountains spanning two thousand eight hundred and ninety *li*. The forms of their spirits are all bird-bodied with human faces. Their sacrifices: one male chicken is offered in prayer and buried. One *zaogui* is used, with *xu* of glutinous rice. For the household of Mount Jiao, the sacrifice involves a *shaolao* offering of wine and a lamb, with a jade *bi* and a lock of hair.

The first mountain of the Central Ninth Range, the Min Mountains, is Mount Nüji. Its summit abounds in *shinie*, its woods in *niu* and *jiang* trees, its grasses in chrysanthemums and *□*. The Luo River originates here, flowing east into the Yangtze. Its waters hold much realgar, which also emerges from the water. Its beasts include many tigers and leopards.

Three hundred *li* northeast lies Mount Min. The Yangtze River originates here—Mount Min now lies west of Guangyang County in Wenshan Prefecture, where the great Yangtze rises. It flows northeast into the sea, entering the sea at Guangyang County.

Within its waters abound fine turtles, of excellent quality. Many *tuo* crocodiles dwell here—creatures resembling large lizards, reaching two *zhang* in length, with scales and patterned hides, their skins used to cover drums. Its heights hold much gold and jade, while its lower slopes yield abundant white clay. Its woods teem with plum and crabapple trees. Its beasts include many rhinoceroses and elephants, as well as numerous *kui* cattle. In present-day Shu mountains, great oxen weighing several thousand *jin* are called *kui* cattle. In the first year of the *Da Xing* era of the Jin, such an ox emerged in Shangyong Prefecture; men shot it with crossbows and obtained thirty-eight *tan* of meat—this is the *wei* mentioned in the *Erya*. Its birds are mostly *han* pheasants and *bi* pheasants: white *han* and red *bi*.

One hundred forty *li* to the northeast lies Mount Lai. The Yangtze River originates here—Mount Qionglai now lies south of Yandao County in Hanjia, whence the Yangtze arises. The mountain has a nine-fold slope, where creatures called *□□* emerge, resembling bears but black, with white □, and they also consume copper and iron. The river flows eastward into the great Yangtze. Its southern face yields much gold, while its northern face abounds in *mi* dust. Its woods hold many sandalwood and cudrania trees, and its grasses include much wild garlic and leeks, as well as medicinal herbs—namely *□*. The name *Kong Duo* applies here.

The shedding of a snake’s slough.

One hundred fifty *li* further east lies Mount Ju. Its name is pronounced *Ju*. The Jiang River (Northern Jiang) originates here, flowing eastward into the great Yangtze. The mountain abounds in monstrous serpents—now in Yongchang Prefecture there are *gou* snakes, several *zhang* in length, with forked tails, which in the water hook onto people, cattle, and horses on the shore to devour them. These are also called "horse-fettering snakes." There are many *□* fish, pronounced *zhi* (sound unknown). Its trees include many *nau* and *niu* (hardwoods used for chariot parts, pronounced *qiu*), as well as plum and catalpa. Its beasts include many *kui* oxen, antelopes, *□*, rhinoceroses, and water buffalo. There is a bird here, resembling an owl but with a red body and white head, called the "grease-thief." Nowadays, small green birds with curved beaks that eat meat are called "grease-thieves," but this is likely not the same. It can be used to ward off fire.

Three hundred *li* further east lies Mount Gaoliang. Its summit abounds in chalk, and its base in whetstones. Its trees include many *taozhi* and *goudan*. There is a grass here resembling mallow, with red flowers, pod-like fruits, and white calyxes, which can be used to make horses run swiftly.

Four hundred *li* further east lies Mount She. Its summit abounds in gold, and its base in chalk. Its trees include many *xun* and *yuzhang*. Its grasses include much *jiarong* and little *xinxin*. There is a beast here resembling a fox but with a white tail and long ears, called *□*lang (pronounced *si*). Its appearance portends war within the state. Another version reads: "portends internal strife within the state."

Five hundred *li* further east lies Mount Ge. Its southern face abounds in metal, and its northern face in white *min* stone. The Pu*□* River (pronounced *hong*) originates here, flowing eastward into the Yangtze. The river contains much white jade. Its beasts include many rhinoceroses, elephants, bears, and brown bears, as well as many *yuan* and *wei* monkeys. The *wei* resembles the macaque but has an upturned nose and a tail four to five *chi* long, with a forked head and a grayish-yellow color. When it rains, it hangs from trees and uses its tail to plug its nostrils—or sometimes uses two fingers to do so.

Three hundred *li* to the northeast lies Mount Yuyang. Its summit abounds in gold and jade, and its base in green *□*. Its trees include many catalpa and mulberry, and its grasses include much purple grass. The Xu River originates here, flowing eastward into the Yangtze. The river contains much cinnabar grain.

Two hundred fifty *li* further east lies Mount Qi, now located west of Xianyang County in Fufeng. Its summit abounds in white *□*, and its grasses include much *jiarong*.

One hundred seventy *li* further east lies Mount Jiachao. Its southern face abounds in yellow chalk, and its northern face in fine ochre. Its trees include many *zha*, chestnut, orange, and pomelo, and it contains much *longxiu* (dragon’s beard). *Longxiu* resembles *guan* but is finer, growing in mountain rock crevices with stems hanging downward, and can be used to make mats.

From the beginning of the Min Mountains, from Mount Nüji to Mount Jiachao, there are sixteen mountains spanning three thousand five hundred *li*. Their spirits all have the bodies of horses and the heads of dragons. Their sacrifices: the offering consists of one male chicken buried alive, and the ritual grain is glutinous rice.

Mount Wenshan, Mount Gouni, Mount Fengyu, and Mount Chou are all tombs. Their sacrifices: wine is offered first, poured as a libation. The ritual uses a *shaolao* (minor offering of a sheep and pig), and the jade ornament is a *ji* jade. Mount Xiong is a *xi* (seat). A *xi* is where the spirit rests. Its sacrifice: wine is offered, and a *tailao* (major offering of an ox, sheep, and pig) is prepared, with a jade ornament of one *bi* jade. The *gan* dance is performed with weapons for the *rang* ritual—a ritual for exorcism and purification. The dance involves a martial performance with shields. For prayers, the *qiu* cap and *mian* robe are worn. The dance seeks blessings and auspiciousness, and jade is used in the sacrifice. The dancers wear ceremonial caps and robes. Fine jade is called *qiu* (pronounced *ji qiu*).

The beginning of the *Zhong Ci Shi Jing* (Central Mountains, Tenth Section) is Mount Shouyang. Its summit abounds in gold and jade, but it has no grasses or trees.

Fifty *li* to the west lies Mount Huwei. Its trees include many *jiao* and *ju*, and much *feng* stone. Its southern face abounds in red gold, and its northern face in iron.

Fifty *li* to the southwest lies Mount Fanhui (pronounced *kui*). Its trees include many *nau* and *niu*, and its grasses include much *zhigou*. This grass still exists in the mountains today.

Twenty *li* to the southwest lies Mount Yongshi. It has no grasses or trees but abounds in white gold and water.

Twenty *li* to the west lies Mount Fuzhou. Its trees include many sandalwood, and its southern face abounds in yellow gold. There is a bird here resembling an owl but with one foot and a pig’s tail, called *jiaozhong* (pronounced *qi*). Its appearance portends a great plague in the state. The *Ming* says: "The *qizhong* is a bird, one-footed like *kui*, bringing no joy but only sorrow."

Thirty *li* to the west lies Mount Chu. It abounds in *yu* trees, *jiao*, *ju*, and *zhe*, as well as chalk. Another version reads: Mount Zhuzhou.

Twenty *li* to the west lies Mount Youyuan. Its southern face abounds in green *□*, and its northern face in iron. Its birds include many storks and mynas. The *qu* is the myna. The *Zhuan* says: "The myna comes to nest." (Pronounced *qu*.)

Fifty *li* to the west lies Mount Zhuo. Its trees include many *gu*, *zuo*, and *niu*, and its southern face abounds in *□fu* jade.

Seventy *li* to the west lies Mount Bing. Its trees include many catalpa and sandalwood, as well as many *yinniu*. The meaning of *yin* is unclear.

From the beginning of the Shouyang Mountains, from Mount Shou to Mount Bing, there are nine mountains spanning two hundred sixty-seven *li*. Their spirits all have the bodies of dragons and human faces. Their sacrifices: the offering consists of one male chicken buried alive, and the ritual grain is the five grains.

Mount Chu is a tomb. Its sacrifice: a *shaolao* is prepared, wine is offered, and the jade ornament is a small *bi* jade buried with the offering. Mount Gui is an imperial mountain. Its sacrifice: wine is offered, a *tailao* is prepared, and two shamans and priests perform the dance, with a jade ornament of one *bi* jade.

The beginning of the *Zhong Ci Shi Yi Jing* (Central Mountains, Eleventh Section) is the Jing Mountains, starting with Mount Yiwang. The Tuan River originates here (pronounced *bo*), flowing eastward into the Ji. Now the Tuan River passes through Rang County in Nanyang and enters the Qing River. The Kuang River (pronounced *kuang*) also originates here.

The river flows southeast into the Han. Within it dwell many *jiao*—creatures resembling serpents but with four legs, small heads, and slender necks, their necks marked with white [markings]. The largest measure ten spans in girth, their eggs as large as one- or two-*dan* jars, and they are capable of swallowing humans. Upon its heights grow many pines and cypresses, while below thrive lacquer trees and catalpas. Its southern slopes abound in red gold, and its northern slopes in fine jade.

One hundred fifty *li* to the northeast lies Mount Chaoge. The Wu River originates here—now known as the Fu River in Wuyang County of Nanyang. (Pronounced *wu*.) It flows southeast into the Rong, where many *renyu* (human-fish) dwell. The mountain’s heights are rich in catalpas and oaks, and its beasts include many antelopes and elk. A herb grows here called *mangcao* (poison grass), which can be used to poison fish—now employed for fishing.

Two hundred *li* farther southeast stands Mount Dikun. (Pronounced *qun*.) Its southern slopes yield much *fufu* jade, while its northern slopes hold abundant iron. The Dikun River emerges from its summit, vanishing underground below, where many *mingshe* (singing serpents) dwell.

Fifty *li* to the southeast lies Mount Shi. Its heights are thick with wild leeks. A well named Heaven’s Well lies here, filled with water in summer but dry in winter. The mountain abounds in mulberry trees, fine chalk, gold, and jade.

Two hundred *li* farther southeast stands Mount Qian. Its forests teem with *zhu* trees (pronounced *zhu*), resembling oak but with edible seeds, evergreen and resistant to rot when used as pillars—sometimes called *chu*. Many cypresses also grow here. Its southern slopes yield much gold, while its northern slopes abound in ochre.

Three hundred *li* to the southeast lies Mount Feng. A beast dwells here, resembling an ape with red eyes, a red beak, and a yellow body, called *yonghe*. Its appearance foretells great terror for the state where wealth is seen. The spirit Gengfu resides here. The Supreme Emperor roams the Clear and Cold Abyss, emerging and entering with radiant light.

The Clear and Cold Waters rise in the west on Mount Jiaoxian, where when the spirit arrives, the waters turn crimson and gleam with radiance. A shrine now stands there. Its appearance portends the ruin of the state. Nine bells are found here, which foretell the onset of frost—when frost descends, the bells ring, hence their name. Such things arise from natural resonance and cannot be contrived. The mountain’s heights abound in gold, while below grow millet, oak, *niu* trees, and *jiang* trees.

Eight hundred *li* to the northeast stands Mount Tuchuang. Its southern slopes yield much iron, and its forests teem with *shumo* trees. The herb *jinjigu* grows here, its roots resembling chicken eggs, its taste both sour and sweet—those who eat it benefit. Sixty *li* farther east lies Mount Pi. It holds much chalk and ochre, and its forests are dense with pines and cypresses. Sixty *li* beyond that rises Mount Yaobi. Its woods are rich in catalpa and nanmu. Its northern slopes bear much green *qing* (mineral), while its southern slopes yield white gold. A bird dwells here, resembling a pheasant, that constantly feeds on *fei*—the *zhen* bird. *Fei* refers to the *fupan* (a creature resembling a flying serpent). This is a different species from the snake-eating *zhen*. Forty *li* farther east stands Mount Zhili. The Ji River originates here, flowing southward into the Han. (The present-day Ji River rises in the mountains northwest of Lixian County and flows south into the Han at Li; *Zhili* shares the same pronunciation as *Li*.) A bird resides here, called the *yingshuo*, resembling a magpie with red eyes, a red beak, and a white body, its tail shaped like a ladle, akin to a wine dipper. Its call echoes its name. The mountain also abounds in *xi* cattle and *xian* sheep.

Northeast fifty *li* further stands Mount Zhitong. (Pronounced *diao*.) Its summit abounds in pines and cypresses, *ji* and *huan*. The leaves of *huan* resemble a flail, its bark yellow and unpeeled; its fruit resembles that of the chinaberry, and when steeped in wine and drunk, it wards off malign *qi*. Used for washing clothes, it removes grime. Its pit is hard, straight, and black, and may be threaded into fragrant tassels. It is also called *guālou*. One hundred *li* northwest lies Mount Jinli. Its heights are rich in pines and cypresses, as well as fine catalpa and nanmu. Its northern slopes yield cinnabar *zhu* and abundant gold. Its beasts are mostly leopards and tigers. A bird dwells here, resembling a magpie with a blue-green body, white beak, white eyes, and white tail, called the *qinggeng*. It may be used to guard against pestilence, and its call echoes its name. Thirty *li* southeast stands Mount Yigu. (Pronounced *ku*.) Its summit abounds in *niu* and oak, as well as *ju*—the latter’s nature remains undetermined. (Pronounced *zu*.) A beast resides here, resembling a dog with tiger claws and armored scales, called the *lin*. (Its body bears both fur and scales; pronounced *lin*.) It excels at leaping and bounding, flinging itself about. (Pronounced *yang* or *fen*.) Those who consume its flesh are not afflicted by wind, nor do they fear the gales of heaven.

Thirty-five *li* southeast stands Mount Jigu. It yields fine jade and many dark leopards—black leopards, that is, for the mountains of present-day Jingzhou produce black tigers. It abounds in *lüchen* and *ling* deer. Its southern slopes yield *min* stone, while its northern slopes yield green *qing* stone.

Forty *li* southeast stands Mount Jishan. Its summit abounds in fine catalpa and mulberry, and its grasses are mostly wild leeks.

Fifty *li* southeast stands Mount Gaoqian. A spring rises upon its summit, so cold and clear that it seems to vanish underground. It is the nectar of the Terrace of the Thearch. Today, on Mount Tanshou south of Jie County in Hedong, a spring emerges and pools without flowing—commonly called "vat nectar"—of the same kind. Those who drink of it are spared heart pain. Its summit yields gold, and its base yields ochre.

Thirty *li* southeast stands Mount Youxi. It abounds in *niu*, oak, and grain; in jade; and in *feng* stone.

Thirty-five *li* southeast stands Mount Cong. Its summit abounds in pine and cypress, and its base in bamboo. The Cong River rises upon its summit and vanishes beneath its slopes. Within its waters dwell many three-legged turtles with branching tails—known as *zhi* turtles, as recorded in the *Erya*. Those who consume their flesh are spared from pestilence.

Thirty *li* southeast stands Mount Yingzhen. Its summit abounds in pine and cypress, and its base in catalpa and *xun* wood.
