Thirty *li* southeast stands Mount Bishan. The waters of the Thearch’s Park rise here and flow northeast into the Shi River. Within them dwell many water jades and many dragons. Its summit abounds in *fufu* jade.

Twenty *li* southeast stands Mount Lema. A beast dwells here, resembling a *hui* with fur red as cinnabar flame, called the *li*. Where it appears, the land suffers great pestilence.

Twenty-five *li* southeast stands Mount Zhenshan. The Shi River rises here—some say *Shi* should be read as *Chen*. The Qin River of today lies in Nanyang, flowing southeast into the Ru River. Within its waters dwell many *renyu* (human-fish), many dragons, and many *xie*—creatures resembling green dogs.

Forty *li* east stands Mount Yingshan. Its base abounds in green *qing* stone, and its summit in gold and jade.

Thirty *li* east stands Mount Hushou. It abounds in *ju*, *chou*, and *ju* trees—though the nature of *chou* remains undetermined.

Here the sound is *diao*.

Twenty *li* east stands Mount Yinghou. Its summit abounds in *feng* stone, and its base in red tin.

Fifty *li* east stands Mount Dashu. The Sha River rises here, flowing northeast into the Shi River. Within its waters lies much white chalk.

Forty *li* east stands Mount Beishan. Its summit abounds in peach, plum, *ju*, and catalpa trees, and many *lei*—now known as tiger beans and fox beans. *Lei* is also called *teng*, pronounced *lei*.

Thirty *li* east stands Mount Yidi. Its summit abounds in jade, and its base in gold. A beast dwells here, resembling a *fei*-rat—among the thirteen kinds of rats described in the *Erya*, though its exact form remains undetermined. Pronounced like a barking dog. It has white ears and a white snout, called the *juru*, pronounced like *qu*. Where it appears, the land faces great warfare.

Thirty *li* east stands Mount Nishan, pronounced *ni*. The Ni River rises at its summit and flows underground at its base. Within its waters lies much fine chalk; its summit abounds in gold, and its base in green *qing* stone.

Thirty *li* east stands Mount Yashan. The Li River rises here, pronounced *li*—today’s Li River rises in Nanyang. It flows east into the Shi River. Within its waters dwell many large fish; its summit abounds in fine mulberry trees, and its base in *ju* and much red gold.

Fifty *li* east stands Mount Xuanshan. The Lun River rises here, flowing southeast into the Shi River. Within its waters dwell many dragons. At its summit stands a mulberry tree, fifty *chi* in height, five *zhang* in girth. Its branches spread in four directions, meaning they extend outward in intersecting paths. Its leaves measure over a *chi* in length, with red veins, yellow blossoms, and green calyxes—called the Mulberry of the Emperor’s Daughter. The mouth governs silkworms; the mouth names it.

Forty-five *li* east stands Mount Hengshan. Today’s Hengshan lies in Hengyang, south of Xiangnan County—the Southern Marchmount. Now called Mount Xunlu. Its summit abounds in green *qing* stone and mulberry trees; its birds are mostly mynas.

Forty *li* east stands Mount Fengshan. Its summit abounds in *feng* stone; its trees are mostly mulberry and *yangtao*—resembling peach but with a square stem, also called ghost peach. It can be used to treat skin swelling.

Seventy *li* east stands Mount Yushan. Its summit abounds in fine jade, its base in gold, and its grasses in *jigu*.

Thirty *li* east stands Mount Xianshan. Its trees are mostly oak, *niu*, and *ju*; its grasses are mostly *weiwinter*. Its southern slopes abound in gold, and its northern slopes in iron. A beast dwells here, resembling a large *mo*, with a red snout, red eyes, and a white tail. Where it appears, the land suffers fire—called *yiji*, pronounced *yi*.

Thirty *li* east stands Mount Zhangshan, also called Mount Tongshan. Its southern slopes abound in gold, and its northern slopes in fine stone. The Gao River rises here, flowing east into the Li River. Within its waters lie many *wei* stones—unrecorded, pronounced like *yu* (fish) and *kui* (kneel).

Twenty-five *li* east stands Mount Dazhi. Its southern slopes abound in gold; its trees are mostly *gu* and oak, with no grasses.

Fifty *li* east stands Mount Quwushan. Its trees are mostly *ju*.

Fifty *li* east stands Mount Shenxiongshan. Its trees are mostly *gu*, and it abounds in jade. Its summit holds much *feng* stone.

Fifty *li* east stands Mount Dagui. (This mountain was mentioned earlier; it may share the same name.) Its southern slopes abound in red gold, and its northern slopes in whetstone.

Ten *li* east stands Mount Zhongjiushan. It bears no grasses or trees.

Seventy *li* northeast stands Mount Lishi, also called Mount Moshi. Its trees are mostly thorns and *qi*. Its southern slopes abound in yellow gold, and its northern slopes in whetstone. A beast dwells here, resembling a leopard but with a white head and tiger claws—called *liangqu*. Where it appears, the land faces great warfare.

One hundred *li* southeast stands Mount Qiushan. The Qiu River rises at its summit and flows underground at its base. Within its waters lies fine ochre; its trees are mostly *ju* and *xiao*—a kind of bamboo. Its southern slopes abound in gold, and its northern slopes in iron.

Two hundred *li* east stands Mount Chouyang. Its summit abounds in *chou* and *ju* trees. A bird dwells here, resembling a crow but with red feet—called *zhuyu*, pronounced like *zhi* (orange) in *songju*. It can ward off fire.

Three hundred *li* east stands Mount Aoshan. Its summit abounds in cypress, *niu*, and sandalwood; its southern slopes hold much *fufu* jade. The Ao River rises here, flowing east into the Shi River.

Thirty-five *li* east stands Mount Fushan. Its trees grow straight; its summit abounds in *feng* stone, and its base in red tin.

Three hundred *li* east stands Mount Yaoshan. Its summit abounds in fine *rong* grasses and much gold and jade.

Three hundred fifty *li* east stands Mount Jishan. Its trees are mostly oak and sandalwood; its grasses are mostly *niu*. A beast dwells here, resembling a pig, with a yellow body, white head, and white tail—called *wenlin*, pronounced *lin*. Where it appears, the land faces great winds. *Lin* is also written as *kou*, pronounced *kou*.

The total range of the Keshan mountains, from Mount Yiwang to Mount Jishan, comprises forty-eight mountains spanning three thousand seven hundred thirty-two *li*. Their spirits all take the form of swine-bodied, human-headed beings. For their sacrifices: one rooster is offered in prayer, one *gui* jade tablet is buried, and five kinds of grain are used as *xu*. Qiu Mountain is the abode of an emperor; its sacrifices require the full *tailao* ritual implements, with offerings buried and the sacrificial animal’s hair inverted. One *bi* jade disc is used, and the ox has no fixed standard. Mount Du and Mount Yu are tombs; all sacrifices there are inverted, with *shaolao* offerings of hair and *ji* jade.

The *Zhong Ci Twelve Classics* begins with the Dongting mountains, starting at Mount Pianyu (or Mount Jian). It bears no grasses or trees but abounds in yellow gold. Fifty *li* southeast stands Mount Yunshan. It has no grasses or trees but grows *jue* bamboo, which is highly poisonous—any injury from it is fatal. In present-day Guiyang County of Shixing Prefecture, *sheng* bamboo grows, with the largest reaching two *chi* in circumference and four *zhang* in length. In Jiaozhi, there is also *di* bamboo, hollow and exceptionally strong, its poison capable of piercing tigers to kill them. This is of the same kind. Its summit abounds in yellow gold, and its base holds much *fufu* jade.

Further southeast one hundred and thirty *li* stands Mount Gui. Its trees abound in *gu*, oak, *chou*, and *ju*. Its summit holds much yellow gold, its base much green realgar, and many *fu* bamboo—*qiong* bamboo, that is. Tall of node and solid within, it serves as a staff, hence called "elder-supporting bamboo."

Seventy *li* east lies Mount Bing. It abounds in *jue* bamboo, yellow gold, copper, and iron, but has no trees. Fifty *li* southeast stands Mount Fengbo. Its summit holds much gold and jade, its base many *suan* stones and patterned stones—*suan* stones remain undetermined in meaning. It abounds in iron, and its trees are mostly willow, *niu*, sandalwood, and paper mulberry. To its east lies a forest named Mangfu, rich in fine trees and birds and beasts.

One hundred and fifty *li* east stands Mount Fufu. Its summit holds much yellow gold, its base much green realgar. Its trees are mostly mulberry and paper mulberry, its grasses mostly bamboo. The spirit Jigu resides here, human in form but wielding two serpents, often roaming the depths of the Yangtze, emerging and entering with radiance.

One hundred and twenty *li* southeast lies Mount Dongting. Today, west of Yiling County in Changsha, there is also a Dongting embankment, submerged and connected to the Yangtze. The *Li Sao* says: "I turned my way by Dongting, / Where waves rose and leaves fell." Both refer to this place. The character is sometimes written as *tong* (copper), but the water radical is more fitting. Its summit holds much yellow gold, its base much silver and iron. Its trees abound in wild plums, pears, tangerines, and pomelos; its grasses abound in orchids, *miwu*, peonies, and *xiongqiong*. *Miwu* resembles *shechuang* but is fragrant.

The two daughters of the Celestial Thearch reside here, divine beings of the Yangtze. These are the two Jiang consorts of the *Liexian Zhuan*. The "Xiang Lady" of the *Li Sao*’s *Nine Songs*, addressed as "Imperial Child," refers to them. Yet the *Hetu Yuban* states: "The Xiang Lady was the daughter of Emperor Yao." When the First Emperor of Qin sailed the Yangtze to Xiang Mountain and encountered a great storm, he asked his scholars: "What god is the Xiang Lord?" The scholars replied: "We have heard they were Yao’s two daughters and Shun’s consorts, who died and were buried here." The *Lienü Zhuan* says: "The two women died between the Jiang and Xiang rivers, and the custom calls them the Xiang Lord." Zheng Sinong also took Shun’s consorts to be the Xiang Lord. Commentators all claim that when Shun died on his southern tour, his two consorts followed and drowned together in the Xiang River, hence their title as the Xiang Ladies.

Yet in the *Nine Songs*, the Xiang Lord and Xiang Lady are distinct deities. The presence of a Lady in the Jiang and Xiang is like the consorts of the Yellow and Luo Rivers. Their numinosity is coeval with heaven and earth—how could they be called Yao’s daughters? And if they were Yao’s daughters, how could they collectively be called the Xiang Lord? To verify this: the *Record of Rites* states: "Shun was buried in Cangwu, and his two consorts did not follow." Clearly, the two consorts did not accompany him in life or death; the principle is evident. Even if they had followed, the two women’s divine insight and boundless perception could have saved them from the crisis of the well and granary with bird-work and dragon robes—how could they not have escaped the wind and waves to suffer a double drowning?

If this were so, the *Tradition* says: "In life, they were high ministers; in death, noble spirits. Their rites equaled the Five Marshes and rivaled the Three Dukes, the Four Seas and the feudal lords." Yet the Xiang River does not rank among the Four Waterways, nor is it listed in the mandated sacrifices. As descendants of an emperor, how could the two women’s divine essence descend to a minor river as mere ladies? Comparing the accounts, the meanings are jumbled; weaving the principles, no basis remains. This cannot be so.

The root of the error lies in conflating the title "imperial daughter," confusing name and reality. No one corrected the mistake, and falsehood prevailed over truth, persisting unrecognized through the ages—how lamentable! They often roam the depths of the Yangtze. The winds of the Li and Yuan rivers mingle in the abyss of Xiao and Xiang. The *Sa* verse says the two women play in the river’s abyssal palace, where they can stir the Three Jiang and harmonize the winds and waves, expressing their numinous power.

The Yangtze, Xiang, and Yuan rivers all converge at the head of Baling, hence called the Mouth of the Three Jiang. The Li River joins them seventy or eighty *li* further on. The *Huainanzi* says: "Fowling and fishing on the Xiao and Xiang." Their present location remains unclear. *Xiao* is pronounced *xiao*. This lies between the Nine Jiang. The *Geographical Records* state: "The Nine Jiang are now south of Xunyang." The Yangtze divides into nine branches, all flowing east to join the great river. The *Book* says: "The Nine Jiang are most vast." This is it.

Their comings and goings are always with whirlwinds and violent storms.

Here the land teems with uncanny spirits—shaped like humans yet bearing serpents, each hand clutching a snake. Strange birds abound as well.

One hundred and eighty *li* to the southeast lies Mount Bao. Its forests are thick with □, nanmu, thorn, *qi*, arrow bamboo, □, and *jun*—all varieties of fine bamboo. The "Arrow" bamboo is noted in the *Tribute of Yu*. The mountain’s heights yield gold and jade, its depths abound in quartz, striped stone, and iron. Its beasts are mostly elk, river deer, and *jiu*—the *jiu* being the osprey, as recorded in the *Guangya*.

Two hundred *li* to the southeast stands Mount Jigong. Its heights are rich in gold, its depths in *fufu* jade. Its woods thrive with willow, *niu*, sandalwood, and mulberry. There dwells a creature named *gui*, shaped like a tortoise yet white-bodied, with a crimson head. Its name is pronounced *gui*. It can ward off fire.

One hundred and fifty-nine *li* to the southeast rises Mount Yao. Its northern slopes bear yellow ochre, its southern gold. Its forests grow thorn, *qi*, willow, and sandalwood, while its grasses include *shuyu* and □.

One *shi* to the southeast lies Mount Jiangfu. Its heights yield silver and whetstone, though no patterned timber grows there. Its beasts are mostly swine and deer.

Two hundred *li* to the east stands Mount Zhenling. Its heights abound in gold, its depths in jade. Its woods grow millet, oak, willow, and *niu*, while its grasses include *rong* grass.

One hundred and twenty *li* to the southeast rises Mount Yangdi. It holds fine copper. Its forests grow sandalwood, *niu*, paper mulberry, and *yan*—the *yan* being mountain mulberry. Its beasts are mostly antelope and musk deer.

Ninety *li* to the south lies Mount Chaisang—now the southern part of Chaisang County in Xunyang, connected to Mount Ting. Its heights yield silver, its depths jade and malachite, along with cold stone and ochre. Its woods grow willow, *qi*, paper mulberry, and mulberry, while its beasts are mostly elk and deer. White serpents and flying serpents—those that soar by gathering mist—abound here.

Two hundred and thirty *li* to the southeast stands Mount Rongyu. Its heights yield copper, its depths silver. Its woods grow willow and *qi*, while its creatures include monstrous serpents and strange insects.

From the first peak of the Dongting range, Mount Pianyu, to Mount Rongyu, there are fifteen mountains spanning two thousand eight hundred *li*. Their spirits all take the form of birds with dragon heads. For their sacrifices: one rooster and one sow are offered, the latter slit open (the act called *ci*). The sacrificial grain is glutinous millet.

Mounts Fufu, Jigong, Yao, and Yangdi are all burial mounds. Their sacrifices involve *siyi*—the display of offerings before burial. Wine is used in prayer, with a lamb as the animal offering, its wool adorned with one auspicious jade.

The gods of Dongting and Mount Rongyu receive *siyi* sacrifices as well. After the display, offerings are buried as before. Wine is used in prayer, with a grand sacrifice of a bull, and the ritual jades include fifteen *gui* and *bi* discs, adorned with five colors.

The "Central Mountains" section of the *Classic of Mountains* records a total of one hundred ninety-seven mountains spanning twenty-one thousand three hundred seventy-one *li*.

In all, the world’s renowned mountains number five thousand three hundred seventy, covering sixty-four thousand fifty-six *li*—this is the extent of the land. Yu said: "The world’s famed mountains total five thousand three hundred seventy, spanning sixty-four thousand fifty-six *li*, encompassing the earth." He spoke of the Five Treasuries, noting that the remaining lesser peaks were too numerous to record.

The earth spans twenty-eight thousand *li* from east to west and twenty-six thousand *li* from north to south. Mountains that give rise to rivers measure eight thousand *li*, while those that receive waters measure the same. Copper-bearing mountains number four hundred sixty-seven, iron-bearing mountains three thousand six hundred ninety. These are the lands where heaven and earth apportion soil and cultivate grain, where spears and halberds are forged, where blades and swords arise. The skilled have abundance, the unskilled want. Sacrifices at Mount Tai and Liangfu—seventy-two dynasties—hold the tally of gains and losses within them. This is called the wealth of the state. The *Guanzi: Earth Numbers* states: "Seventy-two rulers have performed the Feng and Shan sacrifices."

The five volumes of the *Classic of Mountains* total fifteen thousand five hundred three characters.

**Peach Grove**
The Peach Grove Valley is a true frontier wilderness.
When King Wu conquered Shang, he rested his oxen and let his horses roam.
Barred were the Three Perils, securing the west for Xia.

**Resonant Stone**
Metal and stone share a kind—hidden echoes lie within.
Struck, they roar like thunder, their sound heard afar.
If one’s calculations align, qi flows without obstruction.

**Spiral Turtle and Human Fish**
A sound like splintering wood—this is the spiral turtle.
Its long neck sways, its cry like an owl’s.
The human fish resembles □, emerging from the Luo and Yi rivers.

**Emperor’s Terrace Chess**
Vast is the Emperor’s Terrace, the treasure of the numinous.
Here lies a stone chessboard, its five colors radiant.
Libations and prayers to the hundred spirits harmonize heaven’s qi.

**Rue Flower and Sour Grass**
The fever-curing herb bears fruit like a melon.
The sour grass’s leaves, thrice transformed, bloom yellow.
It can be used as poison, fearing neither vipers nor serpents.

**□ Grass**
The □ grass’s yellow blossoms resemble dodder.
The noble wear it, and people find it charming.
Transformed from the Emperor’s daughter, its nature is hard to fathom.

**Mountain Grease and Yellow Thorn Beast**
Mountain grease resembles a pig, its nature fond of cursing.
It feeds on yellow thorn, neither child nor transformation.
Though lacking chastity, its principle is like one who never marries.

**Three-Legged Turtle**
Creation is impartial, without bias or favor.
Short is not too little, long not too much.
The *ben* turtle with three legs—how does it differ from the soft-shelled turtle?

**Fine Glory**
Thunder is heaven’s essence, stirring the heart and startling the eye.
How to master it? Wear fine glory.
It rectifies the spirit, serving the mouth, intestines, and belly.

**Heavenly *Pian* Ox, Wounded Ox, Patterned Beast, and Indigo Fish**
The wounded ox calms qi, the heavenly *pian* eases choking.
The patterned beast resembles a bee, its tail branching, its tongue inverted.

**Scaled Fish and Azure Mottled Fish**
The scaled fish and azure mottled fish dwell in crossroad caves.

**Divine Rest Tree**
The tree of *Di Xiu*—its branches intertwine in pairs.
Its trunk soars, its canopy sparse, its shade layered like clouds.
The noble one who wears it knows not anger, only love.

**Tai Chamber**
Song Peak stands as the sacred ancestor, Hua and Dai as the eternal pillars.
Its qi connects to the Celestial Han, its spirit penetrates the hidden and the bright.
Lofty and central, it is the essence of all mountains.

**Yi Tree**
There is a fine tree called the *yi*.
Pluck its leaves, wear its grace.
The noble one finds joy, and no discord stirs at home.

**□ Herb**
The □ herb bears a crimson stem, its fruit like the *yingyu*.
Eating it sharpens the mind, unaware of its own transformation.
Nearly equal to innate wisdom, its merit surpasses study.

**Hawk Bird**
The hawk bird thrives in drought, aiding its kind.
When its kin are harmed, it does not flee, even unto death.
Its feathers adorn warriors, fierce yet righteous.

**Chirping Snake and Transforming Snake**
Two snakes—one chirps, one transforms—of the same kind yet different forms.
They glide together on rippling wings, surging through waves.
Where they appear, dual calamities follow: excess or restraint.

**Red Copper**
On Kunwu Mountain lies the famed copper.
It cuts jade like clay, and when fired, gleams with color.
The corpse of *Shizi* marveled at it, proven by that minister.

**Divine Fumigation Pool**
*Tai Feng* with a tiger’s tail, *Wu Luo* with a human face.
The spirit of the Fumigation Pool—its form unseen.
Yet there is fine jade, river forests as radiant as madder.

**Divine Wu Luo**
There is the spirit *Wu Luo*, slender-waisted with white teeth.
Her voice rings like chiming pendants, piercing the ears.
She oversees the imperial inner chambers, befitting women.

**Yao Bird**
The *yao* bird resembles the *liang*, with emerald plumage and crimson eyes.
Its form is lovely, its flesh extraordinary.
Women who eat it bear many descendants.

**Xun Herb**
The *xun* herb bears red fruit, its form like *jian* grass.
Women who wear it refine their complexion, altering their visage.
Like Xia Ji’s allure, its charm endures through three returns.

**Horse-Belly Beast and Flying Fish**
The horse-belly creature has a human face and tiger’s likeness.
The flying fish resembles a piglet, crimson-marked and featherless.
Eating it averts weapons, fearing neither thunder nor drums.

**Divine Tai Feng**
The spirit *Tai Feng* delights in roaming the southern mountains.
He washes his feet across the nine provinces, moving in and out with flowing light.
He stirs the celestial qi, confounding Kong Jia in bewilderment.

**Jie Cypress**
The *jie* cypress blooms white, its seeds like cinnabar.

Fruit plump transforms qi, eating it dispels cold.
Things follow what they are dyed by—Mozi’s lament.

**Orange and Citron**
Their boughs bear orange and citron, the marvels supremely sweet.
Crimson fruit gleams like gold, leaves glow emerald and indigo.
Qu Yuan sang of them, making them a tale of beauty.

**Ping Herb**
On Mount Da Kui grows the *ping* herb.
Blue blossoms, white fruit—eating it averts early death.
Though it does not extend years, one may live to a ripe old age.

**Jiao Fish**
A distinct kind of fish, called the *jiao*.
Pearl-like skin, venomous tail—neither scale nor fur.
It may adorn horns, and also embellish swords and blades.

**Zhen Bird**
The viper is the chief of poisons, the *zhen* bird feeds on it.
Fluttering its wings, crying in the woods, grass withers and trees grow grim.
Its feathers bring hidden execution—its punishment is not to be defied.

**Pepper**
The pepper’s thickets flourish, its fruit abundant and ordered.
Brushing the tips, frost clings—crimson fruit, pungent and fragrant.
Consuming it grants penetrating vision, one may commune with spirits.

**Divine □, Wei, Ji Meng, and Chaocong**
Wading through □ with three legs, □ encircled by tiger claws.
Ji Meng with a dragon’s head, uniquely endowed in form.
Ascending and descending with wind and rain, vast and boundless.

**Min Mountains**
The essence of the Min Mountains links above to the Eastern Well.
Beginning as a single ladle, ending in boundless darkness.
It sets the record for the southern lands—heaven clears, earth stills.

**Kui Ox**
The colossal ox of the southwest emerges from the Jiang and Min.
Its body hangs like lowering clouds, its flesh weighs a thousand jun.
Though possessed of untamed strength, it cannot turn the wheel.

**Qionglai Mountains**
The Qionglai peaks are steep and perilous, their paths twist ninefold.
Wang Yang hesitated, Wang Zun displayed his resolve.
Yin had its Three Benevolents, Han its Two Sages.

**□ Wolf, Yonghe, and □ Beast**
When the □ wolf appears, weapons are not wielded abroad.
Yonghe incites terror, □ spreads plague.
Though their evil is shared, their disasters differ—each qi finds its own path.

**Wei Monkey**
Among the talents of the ape family, none surpass the *wei*.
When rain comes, it hangs by itself, plugging its nose with its tail.
Though its form adapts, it ranks among the ancestral emblems.

**Bear Cave**
On Bear Mountain lies a cave, whence divine beings emerge.
Alongside those stone drums, their forms differ yet their responses align.
Though omens appear first, the outcome is not auspicious.

**Qizhong**
Green plowmen ward off plague, *qizhong* brings down calamity.
Things oppose each other—each arrives with its own qi.
When seen, the people sigh, for it is truly a harbinger of disease.

**Jiao**
Neither snake nor dragon, its scales shimmer with radiant hues.

Swift it leaps through surging waves, winding through the Jiang and Han.
When Han Wu’s arrows drank deep, flying archers severed it in turn.

**Divine Plowman**
The limpid waters of Qingling lie atop the mountain’s crest.
There the Plowman roams, scattering radiance like splashed light.
The black-headed folk offer honor, to avert disasters and misfortunes.

**Nine Bells**
When peaks crumble and the Jing runs dry, when unicorns clash as the sun wanes,
the Nine Bells shall chime—only when frost descends will they fall silent.
Qi responds in kind, stirred to action by touch and resonance.

**Yingshao**
On the mountain of disarray, a bird like a magpie:
white-bodied, red-eyed, its tail shaped like a ladle.
Though it bears a dipper, none may draw from it.

**Lin**
A beast with tiger’s claws, named *lin*.
It delights in leaping and pouncing, clattering its armor in excitement.
Should one eat its flesh, the wind’s swiftness goes unfelt.

**Nectar of the Divine Terrace**
The waters of the Divine Terrace dispel the ailments of the heart.
They cleanse the numinous palace, harmonizing spirit and nurturing nature.
Drink, and wander carefree; bathe, and wash your hair in its flow.

**Ju Ru**
The tiny creature *ju ru* bears no harm in its body.
Yet when seen, armies rise—two hosts meet in battle.
For things respond to stimuli, regardless of size.

**Mulberry of the Divine Maiden**
Behold the great mulberry, growing by the rushing stream and deep pool.
Its girth spans five *zhang*, its branches intertwine.
The garden-dweller gathers its leaves, where the Divine Maiden tended silkworms.

**Liangqu and the Lin Beast**
When Liangqu deploys troops, □ brings forth disaster.
□ wards off fire—each thing has its own power.
At the sight of the *lin*, great winds arise.

**Shen Yu’er**
Yu’er resembles a man, yet bears a serpent’s head with two faces.
It often roams the river’s depths, appearing in vast caverns.
Now hidden, now revealed, its divine light flickers in and out of sight.

**Two Divine Maidens**
The two divine maidens dwell in Dongting.
They wander, transforming the Five Rivers, drifting through the ethereal and obscure.
Called the White Ladies, they are the spirits of the Xiang.

**Flying Serpent**
The soaring serpent matches the dragon, leaping forth with the mist.

Though one may wish to ascend the heavens, clouds disperse and land offers no foothold.
To crouch without awakening the body—how could one rely on clouds for support?

*The Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Five* ends here.

(Annotations from the Tan collated edition:
1. "Therein are many *hao*-fish" originally read "Therein are many *hao*-fish."
2. "Red beak, red tail, red feathers" was missing one "red"; Tan edition emends to "red beak, red tail, red feathers."
3. "Some write *kou*-tail" emended in Tan edition to "some write *sparrow*-tail."
4. "Xia mouth, mouth mouth mouth mouth *bei* mouth below, heaven great wind, dark and obscure, Kong Jia lost mouth, mouth mouth mouth mouth" emended in Tan edition to "When the Xia ruler Kong Jia hunted below Huang Mountain, heaven sent a great wind, dark and obscure; Kong Jia became lost and entered a commoner’s dwelling."
5. "Ten *li* eastward is called mouth Mountain" emended in Tan edition to "Ten *li* eastward is called Corpse Mountain."
5. "Pronounced like *xiang* mouth’s *xiang*" emended in Tan edition to "Pronounced like *xiangwei*’s *xiang*."
6. "Mouth woman presides over silkworms, mouth to name mouth" emended in Tan edition to "Women preside over silkworms, hence the name *mulberry*.")

*The Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Six*
Guo Pu’s Commentary.

*The Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Ten: The Southern Lands Beyond the Seas*

The earth sustains all within the Six Directions—above and below, the four quarters—these are the Six Directions. Within the Four Seas, the sun and moon cast their light, the stars mark their paths, the four seasons order time, and the Great Year governs the cycles. Spirits and numinous beings arise here, their forms strange and varied; some die young, others live long, yet only the sage can fathom their Way.

Truly, unless one exhausts principle and fulfills nature, one cannot trace the origins of their transformations.

From the southwest corner to the southeast corner beyond the seas:

*Corner* (陬) means *nook*; pronounced *zōu*.

The Kingdom of Bound Chests lies to its southwest; its people have bound chests, their breasts protruding like knotted throats.

South Mountain stands to its southeast. From this mountain onward, creatures become serpents, and serpents are called fish—thus creatures turn to serpents, and serpents to fish. Another account places South Mountain to the southeast of the Kingdom of Bound Chests.

The Pair-Winged Birds dwell to its east, their plumage blue and red, resembling wild ducks. Two birds fly wing to wing. Another account places them east of South Mountain.

The Feathered People’s Kingdom lies to its southeast; its people have elongated heads and bodies covered in feathers. They can fly but not far, reproduce by eggs, and their depictions resemble immortals. Another account places them southeast of the Pair-Winged Birds, describing them as long-cheeked.

*The Divination of Qi* states: "The Feathered People have bird beaks, red eyes, and white heads."

Sixteen divine beings, linked arm to arm, serve the Thearch as guardians of the night in these wilds. Their painted forms appear in nocturnal visions. To the east of the Feathered People, their people have small cheeks and red shoulders, the space between their shoulder blades a vivid crimson. All number sixteen. This passage may have been added by later hands.

The *Bifang* Bird dwells to its east, west of the Blue Waters. It has a human face and a single foot. Another account places it east of the sixteen spirits.

The Kingdom of Clamorous Heads lies to its south. Its people have human faces, wings, and bird-like legs, and they can catch fish. *Huandou*, a minister of Yao, committed a crime and cast himself into the southern sea to die. The Thearch pitied him and commanded his descendants to dwell by the southern sea and offer him sacrifices. Their depictions also resemble immortals. Another account places them east of the *Bifang* Bird. Some call it the Kingdom of Clamorous Vermilion.

The Kingdom of Fire-Suppression lies to its south. Its inhabitants have the bodies of beasts and are black in color, spouting fire from their mouths. It is said they can exhale flames, and their depictions resemble black macaques. Another account places it east of the Kingdom of Clamorous Vermilion.

The Three Mulberry Trees stand to the north of Fire-Suppression, growing by the Red Waters. These trees have leaves like cypress, all bearing pearls. Another account describes them as resembling comets, with the appearance of a comet’s tail.

The Kingdom of the Three Miao lies east of the Red Waters. Its people follow one another in succession. In ancient times, when Yao abdicated the realm to Shun, he consulted the lord of the Miao, who disapproved. The Thearch executed him, and the people of the Miao rebelled, fleeing into the southern sea to become the Kingdom of the Three Miao. Another name for it is the Kingdom of the Three Mao.

The Kingdom of Zhi lies to its east (pronounced *zhi* or *ti*). Its people are yellow and skilled at drawing bows to shoot serpents. The *Classic of the Great Wilderness* states that this kingdom naturally possesses five kinds of garments. Another account places the Kingdom of Zhi east of the Three Mao.

The Kingdom of Pierced Chests lies to its east. Its people have holes in their chests. The *Master Shi* says: Among the western barbarians, there are those with pierced chests, deep-set eyes, or long arms—all drawn by the virtue of the Yellow Thearch. The *Record of Strange Things* states: In the land of pierced chests, when their garments are removed, no natural openings remain. This seems to imitate the people of Pierced Chests. Another account places it east of the Kingdom of Dai.

The Kingdom of Crossed Shins lies to its east. Its people have crossed shins. Their legs and shins twist and intertwine—these are the so-called "tattooed foreheads" and "crossed toes." Some write it as "crossed necks," describing people who walk with their necks entwined. Another account places it east of the Kingdom of Pierced Chests.

The People of Immortality dwell to its east. They are black in color and do not die of old age. There is a Round Hill Mountain, upon which grows the Tree of Immortality; eating its fruit grants longevity. There is also a Red Spring, and drinking its waters prevents aging. Another account places them east of the Kingdom of Pierced Chests.

The Kingdom of Forked Tongues lies to its east. Its people all have forked tongues, also called "branched tongues." Another account places it east of the People of Immortality.

The Kunlun Mound lies to its east, a square foundation at the mountain’s base. Another account places it east of the Kingdom of Forked Tongues, describing it as a square mound.

Yi battled the Chisel-Tooth at the wilds of Shouhua, where Yi shot and killed him. This took place east of the Kunlun Mound. Yi wielded bow and arrows, while Chisel-Tooth held a shield. Chisel-Tooth was also human, with teeth like chisels, five or six *chi* in length, hence his name. Another name is *Ge*. The details remain unclear.

The Kingdom of Three Heads lies to its east, where each person has one body but no voice. Another account places it east of Chisel-Tooth.

The Kingdom of Zhourao lies to its east, where the people are short in stature but wear caps and belts. Their horses stand three *chi* tall, dwell in caves, and are skilled in craftsmanship, possessing five distinct abilities. Another account places the Kingdom of Jiaoyao east of the Kingdom of Three Heads. The *Waizhuan* states: "The people of Jiaoyao are three *chi* tall, the shortest of all." The *Shi Han Shenwu* says: "Four hundred thousand *li* east of the Central Plains, one finds the people of Jiaoyao, one *chi* and five *cun* in height."

The Kingdom of Long Arms lies to its east, where the people catch fish in the water, each hand holding a fish. An old account says their hands hang down to the ground. During the Huangchu era of the Wei, Governor Wang Xu of Xuantu pursued the palace of the King of Goguryeo to its end. Passing through the Kingdom of Wooju, its eastern border reached the great sea, near where the sun rises. He asked the elders: "Are there people beyond the sea?" They replied that once, in the sea, they found a cloth garment, its body like that of a middle-sized person, with sleeves three *chi* long—this was the clothing of the Long-Armed people. Another account places it east of Jiaoyao, where they catch fish in the sea.

Mount Di: Emperor Yao was buried on its sunny side. The *Lüshi Chunqiu* says: "Yao was buried in Gulin." Today, in the western part of Yangcheng County, near the city of Dong’e County, and south of Xiangting in Zheyang County, there are all said to be Yao’s tombs. Emperor Ku was buried on its shady side. Ku, Yao’s father, was called Gaoxin; his tomb now lies in the Kuaiyin wilds south of Dunqiu County. *Ku* is pronounced like *kù*. There are bears, brown bears, striped tigers, and leopard-like tigers. The *Shizi* says: "Zhonghuang Boyu, with his left hand, seized a great ape of the Taihang Mountains, and with his right, grappled a leopard-like tiger." *Wei* are a kind of macaque. *Lizhu* is the name of a tree; see the *Zhuangzi*. Modern illustrations depict it as a red crow. *Shirou* ("seeing flesh") and *jurou* ("gathering flesh") resemble ox liver, with two eyes. They can be eaten endlessly, for as soon as they are consumed, they regenerate as before. *Xuyan* and *suozhi* are unclear. King Wen buried them all in their respective places. Today, King Wen’s tomb lies in the eastern altar of Hao in Chang’an. According to the records, the tombs of the kings all have fixed locations, yet the *Classic of Mountains and Seas* often repeats them. This is because the sages ruled for long periods, their benevolence spreading widely, their kindness extending even to birds and beasts. When they passed away, the Four Seas mourned as if they had lost their parents, and there was no grief unexpressed. Thus, people from distant lands and foreign customs, upon hearing of the Son of Heaven’s death, would sit and offer sacrifices, weeping and wailing, and raise mounds of earth as tombs. This is why such tombs appear in many places. It is like the temples of the Son of Heaven found in all the distant commanderies and kingdoms of the Han—this is their legacy. Another account calls it Mount Tang. Another says there are bears, brown bears, striped tigers, *wei*, leopards, *lizhu*, *□jiu*, *□jiu*, and creatures like owls. *Shirou*, *hujiao*, and *suozhi* remain unclear. Its Fan Forest spans three hundred *li*, meaning its trees spread and flourish abundantly.

**Illustrations and Eulogies from the *Haiwai Nanjing***

From this mountain, insects become snakes, and snakes are called fish.
The base has no fixed tribute; the noble have no constant treasures.
Things do not define themselves—self-definition comes from man.
All matters are thus; how could it be only scales and fins?

**Kingdom of Feathered People**
With beaks like birds and long cheeks, they hatch from eggs.
They soar on wings, but their dragon-like flight does not carry them far.
They belong to the naked kind—how strange their form!

**Divine People of Two Eights**
East of the Feathered People, there are spirits who govern the night.
Two eights link arms, harnessing themselves.
By day they hide; by night they emerge, transforming with the strange hours.

**Kingdom of Huantou**
The people of Huan have beaks like birds and walk with feathered staffs.
They dwell by the sea, feeding on □*ju*.
Truly fine grain, the so-called glutinous millet.

**Kingdom of Yanhuo**
People with beastly bodies, their forms strange and cunning.
They exhale and inhale fiery essence, flames following their breath’s intensity.
Though it seems unremarkable, in principle, it cannot be hot.

**Three Pearl Tree**
Where the three pearls grow, by the banks of the Red Waters.
Its leaves stand tall like cypress, its beauty and strength like a comet.
Bathed in crimson waves, they reflect each other like rosy clouds.

**Kingdom of Zhie**
They do not cultivate silkworms or weave silk, nor do they sow or reap.
A hundred beasts lead the dance; flocks of birds clap their wings.
These are called the people of Zhie, naturally clothed and fed.

**Kingdoms of Pierced Chests, Crossed Legs, and Forked Tongues**
A great furnace melts metal, casting myriad forms.
Creation is impartial, each endowed with its nature.
Returning to the bending and shaping, one sees the signs of destiny.

**Kingdom of Immortality**
People dwell here on Yuanqiu.
The Red Springs halt aging; the divine tree nurtures life.
Observe this distant longevity—endless and boundless.

**Chisel-Tooth**
Chisel-Tooth was human, with extraordinary teeth.
Fiercer than Jiuying, more harmful than the long serpent.
Yao commanded Yi to slay him at Shouhua.

**Kingdom of Three Heads**
Though they share one *qi*, their breaths follow different paths.

Beholding, all are seen; eating, all are sated.
Forms complete themselves—creation is no artifice.

**Kingdom of the Diminutive**
A thousand pipes blow discordant notes, each *qi* distinct in myriad ways.
Giants stand three *zhang* tall; the diminutive barely a *chi*.
Yet in the great confluence, even this is but a passing semblance.

**Kingdom of Long Arms**
Their arms stretch three *chi* long, though their bodies match common folk.
What are they? The people of long arms.
They carry their elongated legs on their backs, fishing along the seashore.

**Di Mountain**
Emperor Yao was buried on its southern slope; Emperor Ku on its northern.
Virtue so vast it embraced all things, none untouched by its grace.
Now fallen, their tombs stand as monuments of mourning.
If even alien kinds grieve thus, how much more the noble Hua and Li?

**Living Flesh**
A mass of flesh with eyes, yet no intestines or stomach.
Like the horse-fungus, it bears a rough resemblance.
Its marvel lies in never diminishing, though those who eat it find it bland.

**Zhurong of the South**
Zhurong, fire spirit, rides clouds with dragons in harness.
His *qi* governs the vermilion radiance, dwelling in the height of yang.
He serves as consort to the Flame Emperor, enthroned in the southern realm.

*End of Scroll Six of the Classic of Mountains and Seas*
*Scroll Seven of the Classic of Mountains and Seas*

**Guo Pu’s Commentary on the *Classic of Regions Beyond the Seas: The West***

The lands beyond the seas from the southwest corner to the northwest corner:

The **Mie-Meng Bird** dwells north of the **Jiexiong Kingdom**, its plumage azure, its tail crimson.

**Dayun Mountain** rises three hundred *ren* high, standing north of the Mie-Meng Bird.

In the **Wilds of Great Joy**, **Xia Hou Qi** once performed the **Nine Dances**—the *Nine Dances* named for the steed *Nine Generations*. The dance was a grand spectacle of whirling motion. He rode two dragons, his cloud-canopy layered thrice. In his left hand, he held a feathered banner; in his right, a jade ring—crafted from hollowed nephrite. A jade *huang* hung at his waist, the *huang* being a half-disc of jade. This took place north of Dayun Mountain.

The *Gui Zang Zheng Mu Jing* states: *"When Xia Hou Qi divined, he rode a flying dragon to ascend the heavens—auspicious."* Thus it is clear Qi, too, was a transcendent.

Another name for this place is the **Wilds of Great Legacy**. The *Classic of the Great Wilderness* calls it the **Wilds of Great Solemnity**.

**The Land of Three Bodies** lies north of Xia Hou Qi, its people bearing one head and three torsos.

**The Land of One Arm** lies to its north, its people possessing one arm, one eye, and one nostril.

There are **yellow horses** there, striped like tigers, with one eye and one hoof.

**The Land of Extraordinary Skill** (or *Qi*, pronounced *ji*) lies further north. Its people have one arm and three eyes, embodying both yin and yang, and ride patterned steeds—yin above, yang below, the patterned steed being a white horse with dark markings.

A bird dwells nearby, two-headed and of reddish-yellow hue. The people of this land excel in craftsmanship, snaring all manner of fowl. They fashion flying chariots, traveling far on the wind. In the time of Tang, one was found within the borders of Yuzhou; the king had it ground to dust, refusing to show it to others. Ten years later, when the west wind returned, he had another made and sent it away.

**Xingtian** contended with the Thearch for divine authority here. The Thearch severed his head and buried it on **Changyang Mountain**, whereupon Xingtian made eyes of his nipples and a mouth of his navel, brandishing shield and axe in dance—the shield being a buckler, the *qi* a battle-axe. Thus he became the **Headless People**.

**Nü Ji** and **Nü Qi** dwell to their north, between two rivers. Nü Qi holds a fish—of the eel family—while Nü Ji wields a sacrificial platter.

The **Jing Bird** and the **□ Bird** (pronounced *zhan* or *liang*) follow in their wake, their feathers blue-yellow. Wherever they pass, kingdoms perish. These are birds of plague and calamity, akin to the modern owl and horned owl.

North of Nü Ji lies the **Jing Bird**, a man-faced creature dwelling upon mountains. Another name for it is the **Wei Bird**, gathering place of azure birds and yellow crows.

**The Land of Husbandmen** lies north of the Wei Bird. Its people wear robes, caps, and girded swords.

When **Emperor Da Wu** of the **Ao Clan** sent **Wang Meng** to gather herbs, he journeyed from the **Queen Mother of the West** to the north, where his path was cut off by impassable roots. He sustained himself on tree fruits and clad himself in bark. Though he lived his life without a wife, two sons were born from his body; when their father died, they became the **People of Husbandmen**.

The corpse of **Nü Chou** was born and scorched to death by ten suns. North of the Land of Husbandmen, she shields her face with her right hand. The ten suns hover above, while Nü Chou stands upon the mountain.

The **Land of Wu Xian** lies north of Nü Chou. Its people hold a green serpent in the right hand and a red serpent in the left, dwelling upon **Dengbao Mountain**, where drought shamans ascend and descend. They come and go gathering herbs.

**Bingfeng** resides east of Wu Xian, its form like a boar with heads before and behind, black in color. The modern crossbow-string snake belongs to this kind.

The **Land of Women** lies north of Wu Xian, where two women dwell, encircled by water. There is a **Yellow Pool**; when women bathe in it, they emerge with child. Should a son be born, he dies at three years of age. *Zhou* means to encircle, as in the *Li Sao*: "Water encircles the hall below." Another account says they dwell within a single gate.

The **Land of Xuanyuan** lies at the edge of these desolate mountains, where even the short-lived reach eight hundred years. This land rests at the southern border of the mountains, as the *Classic of the Great Wilderness* states: "South of Mount Min." It lies north of the Land of Women. Its people have human faces and serpent bodies, their tails entwined above their heads.

**Qiong Mountain** stands to its north. None dare shoot westward, for they revere the **Mound of Xuanyuan**, fearing the majesty of the Yellow Emperor. Thus, they do not dare aim their arrows to the west.

North of the Land of Xuanyuan, the mound is square, encircled by four serpents, coiling and twining. This is the **Wilds of Yao**, *yao* pronounced as *wo*. Here, the **Luan Bird** sings of its own accord, and the **Feng Bird** dances spontaneously. The people eat the eggs of the phoenix and drink the sweet dew, their desires fulfilled as they wish. All flavors are present, and every wish is granted—this is what is meant by a fertile wild.

All beasts gather here in harmony. North of the four serpents, the people hold eggs in both hands to eat, while two birds guide them from the front.

The **Dragon-Fish**, dwelling upon mounds, lies to the north, its form like a wildcat. Some say the dragon-fish resembles a wildcat with a single horn. Another name is *xia*. Pronounced *xia*.

Here ride the divine and sagely, traversing the Nine Wilds—the wilds of the Nine Regions.

One is called the **Turtle-Fish**, dwelling north of the Wilds of Yao. Its form resembles a carp. *Turtle* is pronounced *you*.

The **Land of the White People** lies north of the Dragon-Fish, its inhabitants pale-bodied with unbound hair. Their flesh is said to be translucently white.

There is the **Chariot-Yellow**, its form like a fox, with a horn upon its back. Whoever rides it gains a lifespan of two thousand years. The *Book of Zhou* states: "The White People ride the Chariot-Yellow, fox-like, with two horns upon its back—this is the Flying Yellow." The *Master Huainan* says: "When the world follows the Dao, the Flying Yellow rests among the black steeds."

The **Land of Sushen** lies north of the White People, where grows a tree called **Xiongchang** (or **Luochang**). When a new emperor is to succeed the throne, its bark is harvested here. Their custom eschews clothing, but when a sagely emperor arises in the Central Kingdom, this tree yields bark for garments.

The **Land of the Long-Legged** lies north of Xiongchang, its people with unbound hair. The land is east of the Red Waters, its inhabitants long-armed, their bodies like those of ordinary men, yet their arms span two *zhang*. By analogy, their legs must exceed three *zhang*. They appear in the time of the Yellow Emperor. Some say the long-legged carry the long-armed into the sea to catch fish. Another name is **Long-Jiao**. Some say there is a **Land of Qiao**, and the acrobats’ "Qiao people" likely imitate these figures.

In the west, **Bohu**, the Metal Spirit, has a human face, tiger claws, white fur, and wields iron. A serpent coils from his left ear, and he rides two dragons. As recorded in the *Outer Traditions*.

*(See the* Illustrated Eulogies of the *Classic of Mountains and Seas: Outer Seas, Western Regions*.)

The Xia sovereign Qi divined and rode flying dragons, his dance fruiting across nine generations. He wielded the cloud-fused banner, wore jade pendants at his girdle. Facing the Yang Emperor’s virtue, he received Heaven’s numinous instruction.

**Land of Three Bodies, Land of One Arm**
The ten thousand things flow into form, scattering primordial chaos. Increase brings no excess, decrease no loss. Their transformations defy tracing—seek rather their root.

**Land of Marvelous Naves**
Wondrous the craft, the people of Marvelous Naves! Harnessing the wind’s intent, they fashioned flying wheels. Ascending ruin’s path, they became guests of Emperor Tang.

**Xingtian**
Contending for divinity and failing, he was slain by the Emperor. Thus his form was severed: navel became mouth, nipples became eyes. Still he brandished shield and axe—though transformed, he did not submit.

**Nüji and Nüqi**
Those fair maidens—whose daughters were they? Why dwell amid the waters, grasping fish and bearing platters? Where lies their mate? They dwell apart, sundered from the flock.

**Jing Bird and □ Bird**
There are birds of blue and yellow, called □□. They gather with omens, assembling wherever summoned. Their kind resembles the owl, yet their form resists charm.

**Land of Husbands**
Yin bears partial transformation, Yang lacks the principle of generation. In the Land of Husbands, Wang and Meng were the first. Moved by numinous communion, the mulberry and stone bore no child.

**Nüchou’s Corpse**
Ten suns scorched in unison, and Nüchou perished. Exposed upon the mountain’s bend, she waved her sleeves to shield herself. That beauty—whose daughter was she? She met Heaven’s calamity.

**Wu Xian**
Of the ten shamans, Wu Xian was their leader. He scoured the arts of healing, synthesized the techniques of practice. Gathering herbs on the sacred mountain, he ascended and descended with the seasons.

**Bingfeng**
A dragon without a head, Bingfeng conjoined in tandem. Their forms diverge as a steed split at the spine. Though their nature finds its own way, the more one seeks, the more obscure they become.

**Land of Women**
Jiandi swallowed the egg, Jiang Yuan trod the footprint.

**Land of Women**
In the Land of Women, they bathe in the Yellow Waters.
Conceiving, they bear children—
if a son is born, he dies.

**Land of Xuanyuan**
The people of Xuanyuan,
blessed by Heaven’s favor.
In winter, they wear no layered robes;
in summer, they fan no heat.
Their qi harmonious,
each household lives as long as Pengzu.

**Cheng Huang**
The flying yellow steed, rare and swift—
who rides it defies old age.
Its horns light, its leaps soaring,
suddenly like a dragon’s twist.
Truly, it mirrors virtue—
only then does it alight early.

**Mie Meng Bird, Great Motion Mountain, and Male Constant Tree**
Azure-bodied, crimson-tailed,
it is called the Mie Meng Bird.
On Great Motion Mountain,
a hundred *ren* high, triple-tiered.
The Male Constant Tree
responds to virtue and connects.

**Dragon-Fish**
The dragon-fish, one-horned,
like a wildcat dwelling on hills.
Awaiting the time, it emerges—
divine sages ride upon it.
Soaring across the nine realms,
mounting dragons to ascend.

**Rushou of the West**
Rushou, the Metal Spirit,
white-furred with tiger claws.
Grasping a serpent and battle-axe,
he oversees the lawless.
Established in the Western Reaches,
he executes Heaven’s punishment with reverence.

*End of the Seventh Scroll of the Classic of Mountains and Seas*
*Beginning of the Eighth Scroll of the Classic of Mountains and Seas*

**Guo Pu’s Commentary on the *Classic of Mountains and Seas: The Northern Lands Beyond the Seas***

The lands beyond the seas extend from the northeast corner to the northwest corner.

**The Land of Wuqiong**
The land of Wuqiong—its name pronounced as *Qǐ* (or written as *Qǐng*)—lies east of the Long-Legged People. Its inhabitants lack kidneys (here meaning the intestines). They dwell in caves, consume earth, and know no distinction of male or female. When one dies, they bury the body; the heart does not decay, and after one hundred and twenty years, the dead revive.

**Zhong Mountain’s Deity**
The deity of Zhong Mountain is named *Zhu Yin* (also called *Zhu Long*, the Torch Dragon). It illuminates the Nine Yin, hence its name. When it opens its eyes, it is day; when it closes them, it is night. Its breath brings winter; its exhalation, summer. It neither drinks nor eats nor rests; its breath becomes wind (here, *qi* breath). Its body stretches a thousand *li*. To the east of Wuqiong, this being has the face of a human, the body of a serpent, and a crimson hue, dwelling at the foot of Zhong Mountain. The *Huainanzi* states: "A dragon with one foot." The Land of One-Eye lies to its east, where people have a single eye centered in their faces. Another account says they possess hands and feet.

**The Land of Rouli**
The Land of Rouli lies east of the One-Eyed People. Its inhabitants have one hand and one foot, with knees bent backward and feet curled upward (one foot and one hand, twisted and inverted). Another version calls it the Land of Liuliu, where people’s feet fold backward.

**Xiangliu, Minister of Gonggong**
Xiangliu was a minister of Gonggong (the usurper who seized the Nine Provinces). It had nine heads, each devouring the creatures of a different mountain—an image of insatiable greed and violence. Where Xiangliu pressed its body, marshes and streams formed (*dǐ* means "to touch"; *jué* means "to dig"). Pronounced *jué*.

Yu slew Xiangliu, but its blood reeked so foully that no five grains could be sown in the tainted soil. Yu dug deep into the earth, excavating three times to a depth of three *ren*, yet the blood still seeped upward, saturating the land with its greasy foulness. Thus, he raised the Terrace of the Many Sovereigns upon it—a place where the sodden earth could only bear piled soil for a platform. This terrace lies north of Kunlun (the Kunlun beyond the seas) and east of the Land of Rouli.

Xiangliu had nine human-faced heads, a serpent’s body, and was blue in hue. None dared shoot arrows northward, for they feared the Terrace of Gonggong, which stood to its east. The terrace was square, with a serpent at each corner—tiger-striped, their heads all facing south.

East of the terrace lies the Land of Deep Eyes, where people raise one hand to shield a single eye. Another version calls it the Land of *Yi*. It is said to be near Gonggong’s [missing text].

East (or south, in another version) of the Land of Deep Eyes lies the Land of the Gutless, where people are tall but lack intestines—all they consume passes straight through. Further east is the Land of Clasping Ears, where tigers bring rain in patterns, and its people’s ears are so long they must hold them with their hands (*nie*, pronounced *nuò jiá*).

There is a settlement in the sea, a city amidst the waters and the strange creatures that emerge from them—a place where all marvels are gathered. East of it roam two tigers.

Kua Fu raced the sun, chasing it into the horizon. As the sun neared the land of Jiang Ren, he pursued it still. Thirst overtook him, and he drank from the Yellow River and the Wei; when these were not enough, he turned north to drink from the Great Marsh. Before he reached it, he died of thirst upon the road. He cast aside his staff, which transformed into the Forest of Deng.

Kua Fu was the name of a divine being. That he could overtake the sun’s shadow and drain the Yellow River and Wei—was this truly about running and drinking? It was but a metaphor for such acts. Nearly did he achieve the speed of thought, arriving without moving. Here, the one becomes the ten thousand, life and death alternate, and his form vanishes into the Forest of Deng. How could one trace the workings of his numinous transformation?

The State of Bofu lies east of Nie’er. Its people are large, holding a green serpent in the right hand and a yellow serpent in the left. Deng Forest stands to its east—two trees. Another name for it is Bofu.

The Mountain of Piled Stones, where Yu amassed them, lies to its east, where the Yellow River enters. The River issues from Kunlun, flows hidden beneath the earth, and reemerges at Congling. It pours into the Salt Marsh, then flows on, emerging southward at this mountain to become the River of the Central Lands, and thus empties into the sea. The *Book of Documents* states: "Yu guided the River past the Piled Stones." This speaks of times when it was blocked, so he dredged it to restore its flow.

The State of Juying lies to its east, where people grasp their cap-strings with one hand. Some say *ying* should be written as *ying* (goiter). Another name is the State of Liying.

The Xun Tree, a thousand *li* tall, stands south of Juying, growing northwest of the River. *Qi* is pronounced as *qi* (to stand on tiptoe).

The State of Qizhong lies east of Juying. Its people are large, with large feet, walking so that their heels do not touch the ground. The *Xiao Jing Gou Ming Jue* states: "The Jiao Yao and Qi Zhong peoples, who come through interpreters to offer tribute." Another name is the State of Dazhong.

The Wilds of Ou Silk lie east of Dazhong. A woman kneels beneath a tree, spitting silk. The tree produces silk—this speaks of eating mulberry leaves and spitting silk, akin to silkworms.

The Three Mulberry Trees Without Branches stand east of Ou Silk. These trees are a hundred *ren* tall, without branches. All their trunks face upward, each a hundred *ren* in height.

Fan Forest, three hundred *li* square, lies east of the Three Mulberries, encircled by islets. These are habitable lands amid the waters, surrounding it.

Mount Wuyu: Emperor Zhuanxu was buried on its southern slope. Zhuanxu was called Gaoyang, and his tomb now lies in Puyang, the former Imperial Mound. Another account places it outside the city gate of Poqiu County, in Guangyang Ward. His nine consorts were buried on the northern slope. Some say there are bears, brown bears, striped tigers, Li Zhu, Chi Jiu, and *shirou* (flesh that sees).

Pingqiu lies east of the Three Mulberries. Here are found *yi* jade and *yi* jade (jade stones), blue horses, *shirou*, poplars, *ganzu* trees—whose branches and trunks are red, with yellow blossoms, white leaves, and black fruit. The *Lüshi Chunqiu* states: "East of this mountain grows the *ganzha*." Its sound is like *zha* (a kind of pear). The *ganhua* tree also has red branches and trunks with yellow blossoms. A hundred fruits grow in the valley between two mountains, where two great mounds stand in the center, called Pingqiu.

Within the Northern Sea is a beast resembling a horse, called the *taotu*. In the *Er Ya*, it is pronounced *tao tu*.

There is a beast named *ju*, shaped like a white horse, with saw-like teeth that devour tigers and leopards. The *Book of Zhou* states: "The Yi Qu people have white beasts, white as horses, with saw-like teeth that devour tigers and leopards." Comparing these accounts, they align with the *Er Ya*.

There is a white beast resembling a horse, called the *qiongqiong*. This is the *qiongqiong ju xu* (a swift beast), which can run a hundred *li* in one dash, as recorded in the *Mu Tianzi Zhuan*. It is pronounced *qiong*.

There is a green beast resembling a tiger, called the *luoluo*.

Yuqiang of the North has the face of a man and the body of a crow, encircled by two green serpents, treading on two red serpents. His name is Xuanming, the Water Spirit. Zhuang Zhou said: "Yuqiang stands at the North Pole." Another name is Yu Jing. One version states: "Yuqiang of the North has a black body, hands and feet, and rides two dragons."

**Illustrated Praises from the *Classic of Mountains and Seas: Overseas North***

**The State Without Kidneys**
All things are passed down—without offspring, there are no roots.
Without kidneys, the heart constructs flesh and generates the soul.
That this can be so is because those who honor form endure.

**Zhu Long**
The northwest is incomplete, where the dragon clashes with fire’s essence.
Its breath becomes cold and heat, its eye creates dusk and dawn.
A thousand *li* long, it may be called most divine.

**The State of One Eye**
Among the many, this one is not lacking.
In the wilds, the blind see clearly, perceiving what lies beyond.
The body travels as a guest—what is prized is the subtle.

**The State of Rou Li**
The people of Rou Li have bent legs and elbows turned backward.
If one seeks a model of grace, none here are ugly.
What is valued is the spirit—what does form matter?

**Xiang Liu, Minister of Gong Gong**
A minister of Gong Gong, named Xiang Liu.
Endowed with this strange form, a serpent’s body with nine heads.
Relying on strength and tyranny, he was ultimately captured by the Xia Lord.

**The State of Deep Eyes**
The Deep-Eyed resemble the Hu, but their mouths are extremely sunken.
When the Way of the Yellow Emperor descended, they came through interpreters to submit.
Those with pierced chests and long legs gather together, though of different clans.

**The State of Nie’er**
The State of Nie’er hangs upon the sea islets.
The carved tigers serve as envoys—all strange things are seen here.
Their forms are mutually dependent, their hands never leaving their faces.

**Kua Fu**
Divine Kua Fu, difficult to fathom by reason.
Draining rivers to chase the sun, his form delayed in Deng Forest.
Transforming by touching kind, responding without fixed intent.

**The Xun Tree**
Towering is the Xun Tree, growing by the River’s edge.
Its branches rise a thousand *li*, piercing the clouds and sky.
Its shade stretches to the four limits, its canopy covering Yu Yuan below.

**The State of Qi Zhong**
Though their forms are large, their feet stand on tiptoe.
Leaping like sparrows, their heels never touch the ground.
Responding to virtue, they come through interpreters to submit to righteousness.

**The Wilds of Ou Silk**
A woman like the *jiao* people, her body akin to silkworms and clams.
She produces pearls without shells, spits silk without cocoons.
Transformations arise without pattern—how could things have fixed natures?

**The State Without Intestines**
The people without intestines have hollow bodies.
The heart is the true abode of the spirit; the rest is for external use.
Attaining the One, they preserve themselves—this principle is shared by all.

**Pingqiu**
Between two mountains, the mound is called Ping.
Here lie *yi* jade, fine blue horses, *shirou*, and *ganhua*.
Strange fruits grow here.

**Taotu**
The wild *taotu* steed comes from the northern lands.
Necks intertwined, they rub against each other; backs turned, they prance apart.
Though there were Sun Yang, none could ever tame them.

**Yuqiang of the North**
Yuqiang, the Water Spirit, with a dark face.
Riding dragons and treading serpents, soaring through clouds with wings.
Spirit of the mysterious and dark, standing at the North Pole.

Thus concludes *The Classic of Mountains and Seas*, Scroll Eight. #1 At "in Gong Gong’s...": Collated editions supplement this as "east of Gong Gong’s terrace."

#2 The edition collated by Tan notes: "The Gutless Land lies east of the Deep-Eyed People."

**Scroll Nine of *The Classic of Mountains and Seas***
*Guo Pu’s Commentary*
**The Classic of Regions Beyond the Seas: East**
*(From the southeast corner to the northeast corner of the lands beyond the seas.)*

**Cuo Hill**
*Cuo* sounds like *jie* (sigh); some write it as *fa* (hair).
Here lie *yi* jade, blue horses, *shirou*, poplars, *ganzu*, and *ganhua*—where sweet fruits grow.
In the Eastern Sea, two mountains flank a mound. Upon it stand trees. One name is Jie Hill; another, the Land of a Hundred Fruits. It lies east of Yao’s tomb.

**The Land of Giants**
To its north lies the Land of Giants. Its people are tall; they sit and carve boats.
One account places it north of □□ Hill.

**The Corpse of Shebi**
To its north lies the Corpse of Shebi, also the name of a spirit.
It has the body of a beast, the face of a man, and large ears, each pierced by a green snake.
*Er* (pierced) resembles a snake threading the ear; pronounced like the *er* in *eryu* (bait).
One account says the Corpse of Ganyu lies north of the Giants.

**The Land of Gentlemen**
To its north lies the Land of Gentlemen. Its people wear caps and sashes, bear swords, and eat beasts. They keep two striped tigers at their sides. These people prize yielding and disdain strife.
There grows *xun* (or *jin*) grass, which blooms at dawn and withers by dusk.
One account places it north of the Corpse of Ganyu.

**The Land of the Two-Headed**
*Xue*□ lies to its north. Its people each have two heads.
The rainbow is called *didi*.
One account places it north of the Land of Gentlemen.

**The Valley of the Morning Sun**
In this valley dwells the spirit Tianwu, the Water Lord.
It lies between two waters in Chongchong.
As a beast, it has eight human-faced heads, eight feet, and eight tails, all blue and yellow.
*The Classic of the Great Wilderness: East* says ten tails.
To its north lies the Land of Green Hills. Its people eat the five grains and wear silk and hemp.
Their foxes have four legs and nine tails.
One account places it north of the Valley of the Morning Sun.
*The Bamboo Annals of Ji Commandery* states: "Boyi of Bozhu campaigned in the Eastern Sea and, at the age of three lifespans, captured a nine-tailed fox—this was its kind."

The Lord commanded Shu Hai to pace from the easternmost to the westernmost extremity: five *yi* and ten *xuan* of steps. Shu Hai was a man of vigorous stride.
*Xuan* means ten thousand.
Nine thousand eight hundred steps.
Shu Hai held a counting rod in his right hand and pointed to the north of Green Hills with his left.
One account says Yu ordered Shu Hai; another, five *yi*, ten *wan*, nine thousand eight hundred steps.
*The Esoteric Poems* states: "East to west, the heavens span two *yi*, thirty-three thousand *li*. North to south, two *yi*, fifteen hundred *li*. The distance between heaven and earth is one *yi*, five *wan li*."

**The Land of Black-Teeth**
To its north lies the Land of Black-Teeth.
*The Records of the Eastern Yi* says: "East of the Wa Country, over four thousand *li*, lies the Land of the Naked. Southeast of the Land of the Naked lies the Land of Black-Teeth, reachable by ship in a year."
*The Record of Strange Things* says: "The Western Tu dye their teeth black." This likely refers to the same people.
Its people are black, eat rice, and devour snakes—one red, one green (or one green snake).
It lies beside them.
One account places it north of Shu Hai: its people have black hands, eat rice, and employ snakes, one of which is red.

**The Scalding Valley**
Below lies the Scalding Valley, where the waters run hot.
Above the Scalding Valley stands Fusang, a tree.
The ten suns bathe here, north of the Land of Black-Teeth.
It stands in the water, a great tree with nine suns dwelling on its lower branches and one on its upper.
Zhuang Zhou says: "In ancient times, ten suns appeared together, scorching the grasses and trees to withering."
*The Master of Huainan* also says: "Yao ordered Yi to shoot the ten suns. He struck nine, and the crows within the suns all perished."
This is what *The Li Sao* means by "Yi shot the suns to completion, and the crows fell in feathers."
*The Scripture of the Returning to the Hidden* states: "In ancient times, Yi was skilled at archery. He shot the ten suns and indeed finished them."
*The Bamboo Annals of Ji Commandery* says: "When Yin Jia ascended the throne and ruled from the Western River, a portent appeared: ten suns emerged together."
Thus, this natural anomaly has its origins.
The *Traditions* say: "Heaven has ten suns; the number of suns is ten."
Here it is said that nine suns dwell on the lower branches and one on the upper.
*The Classic of the Great Wilderness* further says: "One sun is just arriving; one sun is just departing."
Though heaven and earth possess ten suns, they are meant to appear in orderly succession, taking turns to illuminate the world. Now, their simultaneous appearance is a celestial omen of disaster. Thus, Yi, entrusted with Yao’s command, poured forth his numinous sincerity, drew his bow to the heavens, and the nine suns withdrew in retreat.
If tools can be used to spur water and kindle fire, and if concentrated essence can summon frost and reverse the sun’s course, then Yi’s brilliance in slaying the solar crows was no great difficulty.
Yet by the standards of common reason, this would be deemed impossible.
But if one follows the reckoning of numbers, there is nowhere it does not reach.
Those who perceive with clarity should grasp its profound subtleties, returning them to the depths of understanding. Then, transcendent meanings will flow without obstruction, and marvelous words will not be discarded.

**The Concubine of the Rain Master**
To its north lies the Concubine of the Rain Master. The Rain Master is called Pingyi.
She is black, holding a snake in each hand. A green snake coils on her left ear, a red snake on her right.
One account places her north of the ten suns: she has a black body and a human face, each hand grasping a turtle.

**The Land of Dark Thighs**
To its north lies the Land of Dark Thighs. Its people are black from the thighs down—hence the name.
They wear fish-skin garments.
They eat □ (*you*), a water bird; pronounced like *you* (melancholy).
Two birds flank them.
One account places it north of the Concubine of the Rain Master.

**The Land of Hairy People**
To its north lies the Land of Hairy People. Its inhabitants are covered in body hair. Today, some two thousand *li* southeast of Linhai Prefecture, there are hairy people dwelling on the islands of the great sea. They are short in stature, their faces and bodies entirely covered in bristles like swine. They dwell in caves and wear no clothing.

In the fourth year of the Yongjia era of the Jin, Dai Feng, the Salt Commissioner of Wu Prefecture, found a boat adrift at sea. On board were four men and women, all of this same appearance. Their speech was unintelligible. They were sent to the Chancellor’s residence, but before arriving, three died along the way, leaving only one survivor. The court granted him a wife, and they bore a child. He frequented the markets and gradually learned the human tongue. He claimed that his homeland was the Land of Hairy People. The *Classic of the Great Wilderness* states: "The Hairy People who eat millet are they." Another account places it north of the Land of Dark Thighs.

**The Land of Toiling People**
To its north lies the Land of Toiling People. Its inhabitants are black. They eat fruits, grasses, and seeds. One bird has two heads. Some call them the "People of Slaughter." Another account places it north of the Land of Hairy People. Their faces, eyes, hands, and feet are entirely black, and they ride upon two dragons.

**Gou Mang of the East**
Gou Mang of the East has the body of a bird and the face of a man, riding two dragons. He is the assistant of Ling Weiyang, the spirit of wood. Clad in white, he governs the eastern direction.

*Mozi* states: "In ancient times, Duke Mu of Qin possessed illustrious virtue, and the Supreme God sent Gou Mang to bestow upon him nineteen additional years of life."

In the first year of the Jianping era, on the *bingxu* day of the fourth month, Attendant Gentleman Wang of the Chamberlain for Ceremonials submitted this collation. The compilation was overseen by Palace Attendant, Bearer of the Golden Mace, Gentleman of the Palace, and Grand Master of Splendid Happiness Gong, and Chamberlain for the Palace Garrison, Gentleman of the Palace, and Grand Master of Splendid Happiness Xiu.

**Illustrations and Eulogies of the *Classic of Mountains and Seas: Overseas Eastern Regions***

**Land of Gentlemen**
The qi of the East is benevolent; the land bears gentlemen.
They feast on fragrant blossoms, command carved tigers as servants.
Refined in courtesy and deference, they let propriety dictate reason.

**Tian Wu**
The vigilant Water Lord, styled the Valley Spirit.
Eight heads and ten tails, a man’s face on a tiger’s body.
Dragons harnessed to twin rivers, his might shakes all without exception.

**Nine-Tailed Fox**
A marvelous beast of Green Mound, the nine-tailed fox.
When the Dao prevails, it soars into view, emerging with a scroll in its jaws.
It heralded auspicious omens for King Wen of Zhou, marking divine portents.

**Shu Hai**
Yu commanded Shu Hai to the north of Green Mound.
Eastward to the farthest reaches, westward to the land of Bin.
Striding across the cosmos, he illuminated the numinous power of virtue.

**Ten Suns**
Ten suns rose as one, scorching grasses and trees to withered husks.
Yi drew his bow, shooting down the solar crows from on high.
Truly a profound resonance, a celestial sign for mankind.

**Land of Hairy Folk**
The sea crows of Lao Bei, the startled elk of Xi Zi.
Some esteem cave-dwelling nakedness, others honor robed attire.
Creature and self vie in esteem—who discerns right from wrong?

**Land of Black-Teeth, Concubine of the Rain Master, Land of Dark-Thighed Folk, Land of Toiling Folk**
Upon Yanggu Mountain lies the land of Black-Teeth.
The Rain Master’s concubine wears serpents as earrings.
The Dark-Thighed eat [lacuna], the Toiling Folk have blackened feet.

**Gou Mang of the East**
A spirit with a man’s face, a bird’s body clad in white.
Bearing the August Emperor’s mandate, he bestowed longevity upon Duke Mu of Qin.
Heaven is impartial to none; those who act with goodness reap fortune.

*Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Nine* concludes

#1 "Sweet fruits are born": The character *gan* (甘) is emended to *bai* (百) in Tan Jiao’s collated edition.

*Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Ten*
*Guo Pu’s Commentary*

**The Southern Classic of Lands Within the Seas**

From the southeastern corner of the lands within the seas westward, beginning at the southern extremity:

The Ou dwell in the midst of the sea. The present-day Yongning County in Linhai is the Eastern Ou, situated in the Qi Sea (pronounced *qu*). The Min reside in the sea (pronounced *min*). To their northwest lies a mountain. The Min-Yue are the Western Ou, now corresponding to Jian’an Commandery, also within the Qi Sea. Another account states that Mount Min-Zhong stands in the sea.

**Mountain of the Three Heavenly Sons** (pronounced *zhang*)
Located north of the Min and west of the sea. Today, it lies east of She County in Xin’an, now called Three Kings Mountain, from whose flank the Zhe River issues. The *Land Records of the Zhang Clan* states: Four *li* south of Yongkang County in Dongyang stands Stone City Mountain, atop which is a small stone city. It is said that the Yellow Emperor once visited this place, which was the capital of the Three Heavenly Sons. Another account places it in the sea.

**Eight Trees of Guilin**
Stand in the east of Fan-Yu. Eight trees form a forest, attesting to their great size. Fan-Yu is the present-day Fan-Yu County.

**State of Bo-Lü**
Unverified.

**State of Li-Er**
Their ears are pierced and elongated, hanging down as pendants—these are the Dang-Er. They dwell on the islets of Zhu-Ya Sea, subsisting not on the five grains but on clams and *shu-mo*.

**State of Diao-Ti**
Their faces are stained with black dye, their bodies painted with scale-like patterns—these are the *jiao* people.

**State of Bei-Xiong**
Unverified (pronounced *qu*). All lie south of the Yu River, which rises in Mount Xiang-Ling. Another name for it is Xiang-Lü.

**State of Xiao-Yang**
Located west of Bei-Qu. Its people have human faces, long lips, black bodies covered in hair, and reversed heels. When they see a person, they laugh, and the person laughs in return. In their left hand, they hold a tube. The *Book of Zhou* states: The people of Zhou-Mi resemble humans but have reversed heels. When they laugh, their upper lip covers their faces. The *Erya* also calls them *fang-fei*. The *Great Commentary* records that during the reign of King Cheng of Zhou, the State of Zhou-Mi presented one such being. The *Classic of Lands Within the Seas* refers to them as the Gan-Chen people. Today, in the deep mountains of Nankang Commandery in Jiaozhou, such creatures are still found. They stand over a *zhang* tall, with heels turned backward, swift runners, hair unkempt, and fond of laughter. The females can secrete a fluid that causes illness if it touches a person. The local custom calls them *shan-du*. Nankang is now named after the Gan River, which took its name from these people. This is akin to the *Great Wilderness*’s account of the Yu people, after whom Mount Yu was named—an analogous case.

**The Si**
Lies east of Shun’s tomb, south of the Xiang River. Its form resembles an ox, with a single black horn.

**Mount Cang-Wu**
Where Emperor Shun was buried on its yang side—this is Mount Jiu-Yi. The *Book of Rites* also states that Shun was buried in the wilds of Cang-Wu. Emperor Dan-Zhu was buried on its yin side. Today, Dan-Yang County still has Dan-Zhu’s tomb. The *Bamboo Annals* likewise records: Hou Ji exiled Emperor Zhu to Dan-Shui. This aligns with the present account. Dan-Zhu’s posthumous title of "Emperor" is akin to the Han-era Lord of Shanyang being granted the posthumous name of Emperor Xian.

**Si-Lin**
Spans three hundred *li* square, located east of the *xing-xing*. Some write it as *sheng-sheng*, though the character is the same. The *xing-xing* know human names. Their form resembles a pig but with a human face. The *Book of Zhou* states: The *xing-xing* of Zheng-Guo resemble yellow dogs but have human faces, heads like roosters, and eating them prevents insomnia. Today, in Feng-Xi of Jiaozhi, *xing-xing* are found. Local lore describes them as resembling pigs yet also like dogs, their cries like those of a wailing child. They dwell west of Shun’s tomb.

To the northwest of the *xing-xing* lies the rhinoceros, its form like an ox but black. The black rhinoceros resembles a water buffalo with a pig’s head, located northwest of the *xing-xing* that know human names. It has three horns on its □ feet.

Meng-Tu, a minister of the Xia ruler Qi, served as the divine overseer of Ba. When disputes arose among the people, he acted as their spiritual arbiter.
