Those seeking judgment in disputes would present their cases at Meng Tu’s abode, where he would render his verdict. If blood appeared upon a litigant’s garments, that person was seized—only the guilty would bleed upon their clothes. Thus did he plead for life, for he cherished all living things. He dwelt upon a mountain west of Dan Mountain. Dan Mountain lies south of Danyang, which is part of the Dan Commandery. Today, seven *li* east of Zigui County in Danyang City of Jianping Prefecture stands the site of Meng Tu’s former residence.

The *yayu* has the head of a dragon and dwells in the Weak Waters west of the *xing-xing* that know human names. Its form resembles a dragon’s head, and it devours humans. Originally a serpent with a human face, the *yayu* was slain by the minister of Erfu, only to transform into this creature.

There is a tree whose form resembles an ox. The *He Tu Yu Ban* states that *zhi* grasses grow horizontally, some like chariots and horses, others like dragons and serpents—all of this kind. When pulled, its bark peels away like the tassels of a cap or the skin of a yellow serpent. Its leaves resemble gauze, like fine silk. Its fruit resembles the *yue* tree—a tree with a yellow trunk, crimson branches, and verdant leaves, growing on Yunyu Mountain. Some write it as *mao*, others as *ma*, and still others as *yinyue*. This tree is called the *Jian* Tree, standing west of the *yayu* upon the Weak Waters. The *Jian* Tree has azure leaves, a purple stem, black flowers, and yellow fruit. Beneath it, no sound echoes, and no shadow is cast when one stands there.

The *Shiren* State—*Shi* pronounced as in *chudi* (clashing). It lies west of the *Jian* Tree. Its people have human faces and fish bodies, without feet. From the chest upward, they are human; below the chest, they are fish.

The Ba Serpent devours elephants, disgorging their bones after three years. The noble who wears these bones suffers no ailments of the heart or abdomen. Today, in the south, pythons swallow deer, and once the deer has rotted, the bones pierce through the serpent’s scales as they coil within the tree’s hollow. This is of the same kind. The *Chu Ci* states: “There is a serpent that swallows elephants—how vast must its belly be?” Commentators say it measures a thousand *xun* in length. This serpent is blue, yellow, red, and black. Another account says it is a black serpent with a blue-green head, dwelling west of the rhinoceros.

The *mao* horse resembles a horse but has hair upon its four joints. The *Mu Tianzi Zhuan* refers to it as the “fine-maned horse,” and there are also *mao* oxen. It dwells northwest of the Ba Serpent, south of the high mountains. The Xiongnu—another name is *Xianyu*. The Kingdom of Kaiti—*Kai* pronounced as in *ti*. The Kingdom of Lieren both lie in the northwest. These three kingdoms are all situated northwest of the *mao* horse.

*From the "Nan Jing" of the* Hai Nei Tu Zan*: The* Xiaoyang*.*

The *fufu* is a strange beast, its hair wild, clutching bamboo. When it captures a person, it laughs, its lips covering its eyes. In the end, it wails in lament, only to be slain by its prey.

The *xingxing*—its form resembles a dog at first glance. Its nature remembers the past, and it serves as a warning to other creatures. By luring misfortune with wine, it brings ruin upon itself.

Meng Xu, a minister of King Qi of Xia, presided over the lands of Ba, judging disputes and discerning right from wrong. When his rulings were unjust, blood would stain his robes. The judgments he sought were divinely inspired—alas, how subtle the workings of the gods!

There stands the *Jianmu*, bearing yellow fruit and purple branches. Its bark resembles a serpent’s scales, its leaves like white silk. Beyond its shade lie the Weak Waters, which only the righteous may cross.

The *Shi* people are descendants of the Flame Emperor. When they die, they revive, their bodies transforming into scales. They dwell in the south of the clouds, drifting upon the Celestial Ford.

The Ba Serpent—an elephant is a mighty beast, yet this serpent swallows it whole. Its bones emerge only after three years. How vast must its girth be? Even Qu Sheng would doubt it.

*End of the Tenth Scroll of the* Classic of Mountains and Seas
*Eleventh Scroll of the* Classic of Mountains and Seas

Guo Pu’s Commentary on the *Inner Classic of the Seas*

The regions from the southwest corner of the inland seas to the north:

The minister of Erfu was named Wei. Wei and Erfu slew Yayu. The Thearch then shackled him in the Mountain of Sparse Kinsfolk—*gù* meaning to strike and bind, pronounced *huò*. He fettered his right foot with a wooden restraint. His hands were bound behind his back, his hair tied together with them. Thus was he tethered to a tree upon the mountain, which lies northwest of Kaiti.

During the reign of Emperor Xuan of Han, men were sent to Shang Commandery to split open a slab of stone. Within the stone chamber they found a man, naked and barefoot, his hair unbound, his hands bound behind him, one foot in fetters. They questioned the assembled ministers, but none could identify him. Liu Zizheng examined this account and matched it to the description. Emperor Xuan was greatly astonished. From that time onward, people vied to study the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*.

Many commentators have held that this was but a simulacrum of his corpse, not the true binding. Yet if one considers the transformations of numinous marvels, they defy rational measurement. All things are endowed with qi, arising from the impossible. They cannot be inferred by ordinary processes, nor gauged by proximate reckoning.

During the Wei dynasty, someone opened the ancient tomb of a Zhou king and discovered a woman buried as a sacrificial victim. Neither dead nor alive, she would occasionally draw breath. After several months, she could speak, her appearance that of a woman of twenty or so. She was sent to the capital, where the Empress Dowager took a liking to her and kept her by her side. For over a decade, she remained in attendance. When the Empress Dowager passed away, the woman grieved and wept for ten days before gradually dying. This is of the same category.

At heart, a great marsh spans a hundred *li*, where flocks of birds are born and shed their feathers.

Here the hundred birds give birth and shed their down and feathers. This lies north of Yanmen. The Yanmen Mountains are where wild geese emerge, situated north of Gaoliu. Gaoliu is in the land of Dai.

The tomb of Hou Ji is encircled by mountains and rivers. It lies in the wilds of Guangdu, west of the State of Shi. The State of Liuhuang Fengshi spans three hundred *li* at its center—this refers to the area within the city walls. Roads extend in the four directions, and at the center stands a mountain with thoroughfares.

To the west of Hou Ji’s tomb flows the Mystic Stream, which issues from Mount Zhong, travels west and then south to the wastes of Kunlun, and finally enters the sea in the southwest from the Blackwater Mountains. Today, the Western Sea lies in the Ting Marsh—the "flowing sands" described in the *Book of Documents*—its shape like the crescent moon five days after its birth.

The Eastern Hu dwell east of the great marsh; the Yi people lie east of the Eastern Hu. The State of Mo is northeast of the Han River. Today’s Fuyu Kingdom occupies the former lands of the Hui-Mo, north of the Great Wall, a thousand *li* from Xuantu. It produces famed horses, red jade, sable pelts, and large pearls the size of sour jujubes. As the land lies near Yan, it was destroyed.

Mengwu is also the name of a bird. Northeast of the State of Mo, this bird has a patterned beak—yellow and green—and faces east.

Within the seas lies the wastes of Kunlun in the northwest. The phrase "within the seas" clarifies that beyond the seas there is yet another Kunlun Mountain. This is the lower capital of the Thearch.

The wastes of Kunlun span eight hundred *li* and rise ten thousand *ren* in height. These measurements refer only to the breadth and height of its foundations. From this point upward, for over two thousand five hundred *li*, there are sweet springs and flowered pools. Five myriad *li* above the mugwort rises the center of Heaven and Earth. See the *Yü’s Basic Annals*.

There grows the Tree-Grain, five *xun* in height and five spans in girth. The Tree-Grain is a kind of millet that grows by the bend of the Blackwater and is edible. See the *Tale of King Mu’s Journey*.

Nine wells are arrayed on its face, their balustrades made of jade.

The balustrades are jade-railed. Nine gates stand upon its face, each guarded by the Openbright Beast; here dwell the hundred spirits. They reside in the corner crags, within the clefts of the rock. At the verge of the Redwater, none but the benevolent Yi could ascend the cliffs of the ridge—meaning that only a man of virtue or one with skills like Yi’s could scale these peaks and crags. Yi once sought the elixir from the Queen Mother of the West, which also speaks to his attainment of the Dao. Some texts render Yi as "the Sage."

The Redwater issues from the southeast corner, flowing northeast, then southwest to empty into the Southern Sea, quenching the fires of Yan in the east. The Yellow River rises in the northeast corner, flowing north, then southwest into the Bohai Sea, emerging again beyond the sea, turning west and then north to enter the Accumulated Stone Mountain where Yu dredged it. Yu regulated the waters, clearing and channeling them, hence it is said he "guided the river from the Accumulated Stone." The Xiang River (pronounced *xiáng*).

The Blackwater emerges from the northwest corner, flowing east, then northeast, and south into the sea near the Feathered People. The Weakwater and Bluewater issue from the southwest corner, flowing east, then north, then southwest, passing east of the *bifang* bird. The *Records of the Western Regions* state: Wuyi Kingdom lies over fifteen thousand *li* west of Chang’an, a journey of over a hundred days to the west. The Kingdom of Tiaozhi borders the Western Sea. Elders have passed down tales of Weakwater near the Queen Mother of the West. The *Records of the Eastern Yi* also mention: beyond the Great Wall by several thousand *li*, there is Weakwater—all unseen lands. The *Huainanzi* says: Weakwater flows from Exhausted Stone. Exhausted Stone is present-day Naran in the Western Commandery, likely one of its tributary sources.

South of Kunlun lies a deep abyss, three hundred *ren* in depth—the Spirit Abyss. The Openbright Beast resembles a tiger in body (some texts say "upright"), with nine heads, each bearing a human face, standing eastward upon Kunlun. It is a celestial beast. The *Rong* states: "The Openbright Beast, endowed with the essence of *Qian*, gazes upon Kunlun, its might shaking the hundred spirits."

West of the Openbright Beast are phoenixes and *luan* birds, all bearing serpents, treading upon serpents, with red serpents coiled upon their breasts. North of the Openbright Beast stand the Flesh That Sees, the Pearl Tree, the Veined Jade Tree, and the Five-Colored Jade Tree. The *yúqí* tree and *yúyīng* are varieties of red jade. In the first year of the Tianxi era of the Wu Kingdom, Wu Yao, an official of Linhai Commandery, found a stone tree at the edge of the sea, over three *chi* tall, its stem and leaves purple, twisting and bending with a radiant luster—this was a type of jade tree. There also grows the Tree of Immortality, meaning it lives forever.

Phoenixes and *luan* birds all bear shields (pronounced *gē*). There are also the Crimson Light, the Tree-Grain, the Cypress Tree, Sweetwater (the same as the sweet spring), the Sage Tree (eating its fruit grants wisdom), and the *mànduì* (meaning unclear; one text says "intertwining trees"). The *Huainanzi* calls it the *xuán* tree, a type of jade.

East of the Openbright Beast dwell the shamans Peng, Zhi, Yang, Lü, Fan, and Xiang—all divine physicians. The *Genealogical Origins* states: "Shaman Peng invented medicine." The *Chu Ci* says: "The Thearch commanded Shaman Yang." They flank the corpse of *Yayu*, each holding the elixir of immortality to repel death, striving to revive it. *Yayu* has the body of a serpent and the face of a man, slain by the minister of Erfu.

The *Fuchang* tree has atop it a three-headed man who watches over the *langgan* tree. The nature of the *Fuchang* tree is unclear. The *langgan* fruit resembles pearls. The *Erya* says: "Among the finest things of the northwest is the *langgan* of Kunlun." Zhuang Zhou speaks of a three-headed man who takes turns sleeping and waking to guard the *langgan* and *yúqí* fruit—this refers to that man.

South of the Openbright Beast are six-headed tree-birds, *jiao* (serpent-like creatures with four legs, akin to dragons), pythons, *wei* monkeys, leopards, the *zhishu* tree (name unclear), and the *wūbiǎo* trees that mark the edges of the pools (the trees are arrayed to border the pools, which are the Flowered Pools). There are also the Reciting Bird (a bird of unclear form), the *zūn* (a type of eagle), and the *diāo*. The *Tale of King Mu’s Journey* says: "Here there are white *zūn* and blue *diāo*" (pronounced like *bamboo shoots*). And the Flesh That Sees.

*Illustrated Praise of the "Classic of Mountains and Seas: The Lands Within the Seas, West"*

The Minister of Erfu Strikes the Stone
Within its depths lies peril—
Liu Sheng alone discerned it,
while none among the ministers knew.
Truly a master of broad learning,
for the wonders of *Mountains and Seas* are strange indeed.

The Land of Liúhuáng and Fēngshì
Its walls span three hundred *li*,
joined by rivers, roofed by beams.
Movement stirs dust to dusk,
steaming *qi* thick as fog.
How might one wander here,
to roam and revel free?

The Great Marsh of a Hundred *Li*
Called the "Feather-Heaped Land,"
its expanse stretches a hundred *li*.
Flocks of birds gather like clouds,
their wings beating thunder.
King Mu turned his chariot,
there to honor *Lù’ěr*.

The Flowing Sands
A boundary within and without,
divided by the Flowing Sands.
They gird the westernmost reaches,
tumbling and winding in endless coils,
pouring into the Black Waters,
forever drowning in their lingering waves.

The Wooden Grain
On the southern slopes of Kunlun,
by the coves of the swan and egret,
there grows a fine grain
called the Wooden Grain.
Not planted, not cultivated,
it scatters its sacred seeds by nature’s hand.

The Openbright Beast
The Openbright, celestial beast,
endowed with the essence of metal.
Tiger-bodied, human-faced,
it embodies this towering form.
Glaring at Kunlun,
its might cowers the hundred spirits.

The Veined Jade and *Yúqí* Trees
Veined jade and *yúqí*,
clustered by kind.
Emerald leaves lush and trailing,
crimson branches delicate and bright.
Jade’s radiance vies in splendor,
their hues as dazzling as fiery dragons.

The Tree of No Death
All things appear but briefly,
human life is but a sojourn.
The Tree of No Death,
its longevity eclipses heaven and earth.
Seek its elixir from the Western Crone—
how could one compare to Yi?

The Sweetwater Sacred Tree
Sweet springs and jade-like trees,
they nurture longevity to its fullest nature.
They harmonize the *qi*’s balance,
and deepen the pine’s spirit in obscurity.
Why must one be born with knowledge
to be called a sage?

The *Yàyǔ*

The *Yàyǔ* was innocent,
yet slain by Èr Fù.
The Thearch commanded the assembled shamans
to grasp their medicines and stand guard in pairs.
Thus it sank into the abyss,
transforming into a dragon-headed being.

The *Fúcháng Lánggān* Tree
The *fúcháng lánggān*,
a wondrous tree of Kunlun.
Crimson fruits hang like scattered pearls,
verdant leaves spread like jade-green cloth.
Three heads keep watch in turn,
gazing and regarding one another.

*Classic of Mountains and Seas*, Scroll Eleven concludes.
#1 Its crows bear vermilion markings. (Tan’s edition supplements: “Its crows bear red markings.”)

*Classic of Mountains and Seas*, Scroll Twelve
Guo Pu’s Commentary
*Canon of the Northern Regions Within the Seas*

Beyond the northwest corner of the seas lies the Mountain of the Serpent Shamans,
where a figure stands holding a cup, facing east.
Another name: Turtle Mountain.
(The character for “cup” may also be written as *bàng*, though the meaning is the same.)

Queen Mother of the West leans upon a stool, wearing a *dàishèng* headdress and holding a staff.
(*Tī* here means “to lean upon.”)
To her south are three azure birds,
who gather food for the Queen Mother.
There is also a three-legged crow,
who serves as her attendant.
This is north of the Kunlun Wilderness.

A figure called the Earl of the Great Road holds a halberd.
To his east lies the Land of the Dog Seals.
Long ago, *Pánhù* slew the Rong king; Gao Xin gave him a beautiful woman in marriage. As he could not be civilized, he was set adrift upon the southeastern sea near Kuaiji, where he obtained three hundred *li* of land to rule. His male descendants became dogs, his female descendants fair women—thus the people of the Dog Seal Land.
The corpse of Èr Fù lies east of the Earl of the Great Road.

The Land of the Dog Seals is also called the Land of the Dog Rong. Bian Ming, a descendant of the Yellow Thearch, begot two white dogs, male and female, who coupled and gave rise to this nation. Their form resembles that of dogs. (This is the so-called “Dog Nation.”)
A woman kneels to offer food in a cup—
she feeds the dogs.

There are patterned horses with white bodies and crimson manes,
their coats as pale as raw silk.
Their eyes gleam like yellow gold; they are called *Jíliàng* (or *Jíliáng*).
One who rides them may live a thousand years.
The *Book of Zhou* states: “The Dog Rong presented a patterned horse with a white body and red mane, eyes like yellow gold, called the *Jíhuáng* steed. It was offered to King Cheng.”
The *Six Strategies* says: “A horse with a patterned body, crimson mane, eyes like yellow gold, and a head like a rooster’s tail is called the *Jīsī* steed.”
The *Great Commentary* says: “A dappled body, crimson mane, and rooster’s eyes.”
The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* also mentions the *Jíhuáng* steed, which grants a thousand years of life to its rider. Though the names differ slightly and the accounts vary in minor details, they refer to the same creature.
Here, we broadly cite them to expand the realm of marvels.

The Ghost Country lies north of the corpse of Èr Fù, its beings having human faces and a single eye. Another account says the Èr Fù spirit resides to its east, its beings having human faces and serpentine bodies. The *Táo* dog resembles a dog, is blue-green, and makes a sound like "táo." It devours humans starting from the head. Some write it as *qú*. *Qú* means "head *qú*." The *Qióngqí* resembles a tiger, has wings, and bristles like a hedgehog. It devours humans starting from the head, and those it eats have disheveled hair. It dwells north of the *Táo* dog. Another account says it begins from the feet.

The Terrace of Emperor Yáo, the Terrace of Emperor Kù, the Terrace of Emperor Dānzhū, and the Terrace of Emperor Shùn—each has two terraces, all four-sided—stand northeast of Kūnlún. These were likely places where the Son of Heaven passed during his tours of inspection. The barbarians, admiring the sagely virtue and power, jointly built these terraces and towers to mark and honor his traces. Another version states: Where Xiāngliǔ was slain, the land reeked so foully that the five grains could not be planted, hence the terraces of the many emperors were built there.

The *Great Wasp* resembles a *lí* (gourd-shaped wasp). The *Zhū É* resembles a moth. *É* refers to the *pífú* (ant). The *Chu Ci* says: "The dark wasp is like a pot, the red floss like an elephant," referring to these. The *Jiǎo* has a human form with tiger stripes and calves with bulging muscles—its legs have *bó* sinews—sounding like "qiáo." It dwells east of the *Qióngqí*. Another account says it resembles a human and is found north of Kūnlún’s ruins. These are the matters recorded above.

The *Tàfēi* has a human face and a beast’s body, and is blue-green in color, sounding like "tà." Some say it resembles a *chuán* beam. *Zhīshī* has a human form with a broken neck, disheveled hair, and lacks one hand. The *Huánjū* has a beast’s head and a human body. Another account says it resembles a hedgehog-like dog and is yellow. The *Mǒ* has a human body, a black head, and vertical eyes. *Mǒ* is the same as a *mèi* (specter). The *Róng* has a human head with three horns.

The State of the Lín Clan possesses a rare beast, large as a tiger, with all five colors fully present, its tail longer than its body. It is called *Líwú*, and riding it allows one to travel a thousand *lǐ* in a day. The *Six Strategies* states: "When King Zhòu imprisoned King Wén, Hóng Yǎo and his followers went to the State of the Lín Clan and obtained this beast, presenting it to Zhòu, who was greatly pleased and thus released Wén." The *Book of Zhou* says: "In Jiā Lín, there is the *Qiú’ěr*, which resembles a tiger, its tail three times the length of its body, and it eats tigers and leopards." The *Great Commentary* calls it the *Zhí* beast. *Wú* should be written as *Yú*.

South of Kūnlún’s ruins lies the *Fàn* Forest, spanning three hundred *lǐ*. The *Cóngjí* Abyss is three hundred *rèn* deep, where *Bīng Yí* (Píng Yí) eternally resides. *Bīng Yí* is *Féng Yí*. The *Huainanzi* says: "Féng Yí attained the Dao and submerged himself in the great abyss," referring to the River Earl. This is the *Hébó Wúyí* mentioned in the *Tale of King Mu*. The *Bamboo Annals* writes it as *Féng Yí*. The character may also be written as *Bīng*. *Bīng Yí* has a human face and rides two dragons. In paintings, he has four faces, each riding a spirit chariot drawn by two dragons. Another account calls it the *Zhōngjí* Abyss.

The *Yángwū* Mountain is where the Yellow River originates, as is the *Língmén* Mountain. Both are sources of the river’s tributaries. The corpse of *Wáng Yèyè* has its hands, thighs, chest, head, and teeth all severed and scattered in different places. This is likely a case of bodily dissolution yet spiritual connection, where form diverges but *qi* unites—united yet not intimate, separated yet not distant.

Shùn’s wife, Lady *Dēngbǐ*, bore *Xiāomíng* and *Zhúguāng*, the names of her two daughters, so named for their ability to illuminate. They dwell by the great river and marshes, where the waters overflow. The numinous power of the two daughters can illuminate a hundred *lǐ* in all directions. Another account calls her Lady *Dēngběi*.

The *Gài* State lies south of *Jùyàn* and north of *Wō*, with *Wō* subject to *Yàn*. The *Wō* State is in the great sea east of *Dàifāng*, ruled by a queen. Their custom is to wear their hair loose, their clothing lacks stitching, they paint their bodies with cinnabar, and they are not jealous—one man may have dozens of wives.

*Cháoxiǎn* lies east of *Lièyáng*, north of the sea and south of the mountains. *Lièyáng* belongs to *Yàn Jùlǔ Xiān*, now *Lèlàng* County, the fief of Jīzǐ. *Liè* is also the name of a river, cold in *Dàifāng*, where *Dàifāng* has a *Lièkǒu* County. *Liègūshè* is in the river isles of the sea—a mountain name, where divine beings dwell. The river isles lie in the sea where the river flows. This is the *Miǎo Gūshè* Mountain mentioned in the *Zhuāngzǐ*. The *Gūshè* State is in the sea, belonging to *Liègūshè*, encircled by mountains to the southwest.

The *Great Crab* dwells in the sea, likely a crab spanning a thousand *lǐ*. The *Língyú* has a human face, human hands and feet, and a fish’s body, dwelling in the sea. The *Great Biān* resides in the sea. *Biān* is the *fáng* fish, sounding like "biān." *Míngzǔ* Village lies in the sea, sounding like "zǔ." *Pénglái* Mountain is in the sea, where immortals’ palaces and chambers are all made of gold and jade. Birds and beasts are entirely white, and from afar, it appears like clouds, lying in the *Bóhǎi* Sea. The *Market of the Great Ones* is in the sea.

*Illustrated Eulogies of the Inner Seas: Northern Classic* — *Jíliáng*

The golden essence gleams with crimson mane, dragons stride in majestic poise.
With stately steps, the wild geese rise and fall, dust swirls beneath their flight.
This is called *Jíhuáng*, dwelling in the sacred precincts of *Yǒulǐ*.

**Snake Shamans, Mountain Spirits, and the Great Swarm of the Celestial Terrace**
The mountain of *Jiǎzhī* harbors one who holds a cup.
The spirits and *táo* demons bring misfortune and calamity.
The great bees and crimson moths gather at the terrace of the host of emperors.

**Tàfēi, Jùbǐ, Corpse Spirits, and Róng Demons**
Man-faced and beast-bodied, this is called *Tàfēi*.
With disheveled hair and broken neck, the corpse of *Jùbǐ*.
The *Róng* bears three horns, the demon’s brows stand erect.

**Zōuyú**
A wondrous beast of five colors, its tail as long as its body.
Its swift hooves traverse a thousand *lǐ*, fleet as a spirit—this is the *Zōuyú*,
lauded in poetry for its benevolence.

**Bīngyí**
Endowed with the essence of radiant splendor, refining and consuming the eight stones.
Riding dragons in hidden depths, coming and going with the sea’s tides—
this is the *Shuǐshàn*, known as the River Earl.

**Wángyǔ and the Night Corpse**
The corpse of the night, its body divided into seven.
Though parted, they are not distant; though joined, they are not crowded.
If governed by spirit, form returns to unity.

**Xiāomíng and Zhúguāng**
In the waters dwell fair ones, *Xiāomíng* and *Zhúguāng*.
Their radiance flows along the river’s edge, bearing this wondrous auspice.
These are the two daughters of Shun, dwelling apart in separate realms.

**Liègūshè, the Great Crab, and the Mound Fish**
The *Gūshè* Mountain is the true abode of the western transcendents.
The great crab spans a thousand *lǐ*, and the scaled *líng* fish also dwells here.
Vast is the boundless sea, harboring marvels and treasures.

**Pénglái Mountain**
The mountain of *Pénglái*, its forests wrought of jade and azure.
Golden terraces and cloud-borne pavilions, how resplendent its beasts and birds!
Truly a divine realm, where the heart delights in jade.

*The Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Twelve—End*
*The Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Thirteen*

**Guō Pú’s Commentary on *The Classic of Regions Within the Seas: Eastern Lands***

**The Eastern and Northeastern Regions**
Beyond the northeastern corner lies the land of *Jùyàn*. The states within the Flowing Sands include *Zhǔnduān* (pronounced *dūn*). *Xǐ* (pronounced *huàn*, also written as *茧蠕*) lies southeast of *Kūnlún’s* ruins. Some say it is *Méinèi* Prefecture, though it is not governed as a commandery or county, but rather lies within the Flowing Sands. The states beyond the Flowing Sands include *Dàxià*, whose capital is square and spans two to three hundred *lǐ*, divided into dozens of smaller domains. Its land is fertile and rich, yielding the five grains in abundance. *Jiānshā* and *Jūyáo* (pronounced *yáo*) are part of the *Yuèzhī* kingdom. The *Yuèzhī* are famed for their fine horses and luscious fruits, and their great-tailed sheep, whose tails resemble those of donkeys—these are the *xián* sheep. The *Xiǎoyuèzhī* and *Tiānzhú* (India) are its tributary states.

**The Western Hu and Jade Mountains**
The White Jade Mountain of the Western Hu lies east of *Dàxià*, while *Cāngwú* is southwest of the White Jade Mountain. Both are situated west of the Flowing Sands and southeast of *Kūnlún’s* ruins. *Kūnlún* Mountain lies west of the Western Hu, in the far northwest. According to the *Treatise on Geography*, *Kūnlún* Mountain is west of *Línqiāng*, and there stands a shrine to the Queen Mother of the West.

**The Thunder Marsh and Divine Traces**
Within the Thunder Marsh dwells the Thunder Spirit, dragon-bodied and human-headed, who drums his belly. It lies west of *Wú*. Today, in *Chéngyáng*, there is *Yáo’s* tomb and the *Língtái* Terrace, with the Thunder Marsh to its north. The *River Chart* states: "The great footprint lies in the Thunder Marsh, and *Huáxū* trod upon it, giving birth to *Fúxī*."

**Islands and Coastal Realms**
*Dūzhōu* lies in the sea. Another name is *Yùzhōu*. Today, it is within the borders of *Xiōng* County in the Eastern Sea. Legend holds that this mountain migrated from *Cāngwú* in the south, and its summit bears the flora of the southern lands. *Yù* is pronounced *yù*.

The *Lángyá* Terrace stands between the *Bóhǎi* Sea and the east of *Lángyá*. Today, *Lángyá* is by the sea, where a towering peak rises abruptly, resembling a lofty terrace—this is the *Lángyá* Terrace. *Lángyá* was the capital where *Gōujiàn*, King of *Yuè*, established his hegemony over the Central States. To its north lies a mountain. Some say it is amid the sea.

*Hányàn* is in the sea, south of *Dūzhōu*. *Shǐjiū* is in the sea, south of *Yuánlì*. It is either a state’s name or the name of a bird. *Kuàijī* Mountain lies south of *Dàchǔ*.

**The Three Rivers of Mín**
The first of the Three Rivers is the Great River, which rises in *Wèn* Mountain. Today, the river originates in *Kūn* Mountain of *Shēngqiān* County in *Wènshān* Prefecture, flowing southeast through *Jiànwèi* in *Shǔ* Prefecture. It passes northeast of *Jiāngyáng*, then through *Bādōng*, *Jiànpíng*, *Yídū*, *Nánjùn*, *Jiāngxià*, *Yìyáng*, and *Ānfēng*, reaching the southern border of *Lújiāng*. From there, it flows northeast through *Huáinán* and *Xià’nà*, entering the sea at *Guǎnglíng* Prefecture.

The Northern River rises in *Màn* Mountain, and the Southern River in *Gāo* Mountain. *Gāo* Mountain lies west of *Chéngdū*, and its waters enter the sea south of *Chángzhōu*. The *Zhè* River rises in the *Three Son-of-Heaven Peaks* to its east. According to the *Treatise on Geography*, the *Zhè* River originates in the southern wilds of *Xīn’ān* and *Yí* County, flowing east into the sea. Today’s *Qiántáng* *Zhè* River is the same. *Yí* is now *Shè*, and *Zhè* is pronounced *zhé*.

In the northwest of *Mǐn*, its waters enter the sea south of *Yújì*. *Yújì* County belongs to *Kuàijī* Prefecture and is now *Yǒngxīng* County. The *Lú* River rises in the *Three Son-of-Heaven Peaks* and flows into the river west of *Péngzé*. *Péngzé* is today’s *Pénglǐ*, in *Péngzé* County of *Xúnyáng*. Some call it *Tiānzǐguō*.

The *Huái* River rises in *Yú* Mountain, which lies east of *Cháoyáng* and west of *Yìxiāng*, entering the sea north of *Huáipǔ*. Today, the *Huái* River originates in *Tóngbó* Mountain northeast of *Píngshǐ* County in *Yìyáng* Prefecture. It flows through *Rǔnán*, *Rǔyīn*, *Huáinán*, *Qiáoguó*, and *Xià’nà*, entering the sea at *Guǎnglíng* County. *Cháoyáng* County now belongs to *Xīnyě*.

The *Xiāng* River rises southeast of *Shùn’s* burial site, winding westward around it. *Huán* means "to encircle." Today, the *Xiāng* River originates in *Yángshuò* Mountain of *Yíngdào* County in *Línglíng*, flowing into the river and then into *Dòngtíng* below. *Dòngtíng* is an earthen cavern in *Bālíng* of *Chángshā*. Today, in the great lake south of *Wú* County, there is *Bāo* Mountain, beneath which lies *Dòngtíng*. Its subterranean passages course beneath the water, said to connect everywhere—it is called the "earth’s veins." Some say it is the "Marsh of the Southeast and West."

The *Hàn* River rises in *Fùyú* Mountain. The *Book of Documents* states: "*Bōzhǒng* guides the *Yàng*, which flows east to become the *Hàn*." According to the *Water Classic*, the *Hàn* River originates in *Lánggǔ* east of *Jǔ* County in *Wǔdū*, flowing through *Hànzhōng* and *Wèixīng* to *Nánxiāng*. It then flows east through *Xiāngyáng* and enters the river at *Ānlù* County in *Jiāngxià*. It branches into the *Miǎn* River and is also called the *Cāngláng* Waters.

**Burial Sites and Sacred Waters**
*Dì Zhuānxū* is buried on the yang side of the mountain, while his nine consorts are buried on the yin side, guarded by four serpents—meaning four serpents watch over the mountain’s base.

The *Méng* River rises west of *Hànyáng* and flows into the river west of *Nièyáng*. *Hànyáng* County belongs to *Zhūtí*. The *Wēn* River rises in *Kōngtóng*. *Kōngtóng* Mountain lies south of *Línfén*, and its waters enter the river north of *Huáyáng*. Today, the *Wēn* River is in *Yīnpán* County of *Jīngzhào*, its waters always warm. *Línfēn* County belongs to *Píngyáng*.

The *Yǐng* River rises in *Shǎoshì*. *Shǎoshì* Mountain lies south of *Yōngshì*, and its waters enter the *Huái* west of *Yān* and north of *Biàn*. Today, the *Yǐng* River originates in *Gān* Mountain northeast of *Yángchéng* County in *Hénán*, flowing southeast through *Yǐngshī* and *Rǔyīn* to *Huáinán*, then entering the *Huái* at *Xiàcài*. *Yān* is now *Yānlíng*, a county of *Yǐngchuān*. Some say it is *Gōushì*. The county belongs to *Hénán* and is pronounced *gōu*.

The *Rǔ* River rises in *Tiānxī* Mountain, southwest of *Liángmiǎn* Township, and enters the *Huái* at its extreme northwest. Today, the *Rǔ* River originates northeast of *Dàmèng* Mountain in *Lǔyáng* County of *Nányáng*, flowing southeast through *Liáng* County in *Hénán*. It then passes through *Xiāngchéng*, *Yǐngchuān*, and *Rǔnán* to *Bāoxìn* County in *Rǔyīn*, where it enters the *Huái*. *Huáijí* is a place name. Some say the *Huái* is north of *Qīsī*. *Qīsī* County belongs to *Yìyáng*.

The *Jīng* River rises in the northern mountains of *Chángchéng*, which lie in *Yùzhì*. *Chángyuán* is to its north—both are county names. *Nà* is pronounced *zhì*. Its waters flow north into the *Wèi* north of *Xì*. Today, the *Jīng* River originates in *Shēngtóu* Mountain west of *Cháonà* County in *Āndìng*, flowing southeast through *Xīnpíng* and *Fúfēng* to *Gāolíng* County in *Jīngzhào*, where it enters the *Wèi*. *Xì* is a place name, now *Xīnfēng* County.

The *Wèi* River rises in *Niǎoshǔtóngxué* Mountain and flows east into the river north of *Huàyīn*.

The *Niǎoshǔtóngxué* Mountain now lies in *Shǒuyáng* County of *Lǒngxī*. The *Wèi* River issues from its eastern slopes, flowing through *Nán’ān*, *Tiānshuǐ*, *Luèyáng*, *Fúfēng*, *Shǐpíng*, *Jīngzhào*, *Yǐnnóng*, and *Huàyīn* counties before entering the Yellow River.

The *Bái* River rises in *Shǔ* and flows southeast into the Yangtze, its waters faintly white and turbid; it now lies in *Báishuǐ* County of *Zǐtóng*. Its source comes from *Xīqīng* Mountain west of *Líntáo*, passing through *Tàzhōng*, flowing eastward through *Yīnpíng*, and reaching *Hànshòu* County before entering the *Qián*. It then enters beneath the walls of *Jiāngzhōu* city. *Jiāngzhōu* County belongs to *Bā* Commandery.

The *Yuán* River rises west of *Tán* City in *Xiàng* Commandery—*Xiàng* Commandery is now *Rìnán*. *Tán* City County now belongs to *Wǔlíng*; its pronunciation is *yín*. It flows eastward into the Yangtze, entering west of *Xiàjuàn*; *Xiàxiù* County now belongs to *Chángshā*, pronounced *zuòyǎn*. It converges in the midst of *Dòngtíng*. The *Shuǐjīng* states: "The *Yuán* River rises in *Jùlán* County of *Xiángkē*, then flows northeast to *Tán* City County, becoming the *Yuán* River. It then flows east past the south of *Línyuán* County and further east to *Xiàjuàn* County of *Chángshā*."

The *Gàn* River issues from the eastern mountains of *Nièdū*; now it rises northwest of *Nányě* County in *Nánkāng*, pronounced *gǎn*. It flows northeast into the Yangtze, entering west of *Péngzé*.

The *Sì* River rises northeast of *Wú* and flows south, then southwest past the west of *Húlíng*, before turning southeast into the Eastern Sea, entering north of *Huáiyīn*. Now the *Sì* River rises southwest of *Biàn* County in *Lǔ* Commandery, flowing southeast through *Húgǔ* County of *Gāopíng*, then through *Péngchéng* of *Pèi* Commandery, *Xiàpī*, and reaching *Xiàxiāng* County of *Línhuái* before entering the *Huái*.

The *Yù* River rises in *Xiàng* Commandery and flows southwest into the Southern Sea, entering southeast of *Xūlíng*.

The *Yì* River rises southwest of *Línjìn*, pronounced like the *yì* in *yìxí*. It flows southeast into the sea, entering west of *Pānyú*. *Pānyú* County belongs to *Nánhǎi* and lies beneath the city of *Yuè*.

The *Huáng* River rises in the northwestern mountains of *Guìyáng*, pronounced *huáng*, and flows southeast into the *Yì* River, entering west of *Dūnpǔ*.

The *Luò* River issues from the western mountains of *Luò*, flowing northeast into the Yellow River and entering west of *Chénggāo*. The *Book of Documents* states: "The *Luò* follows its course from *Xióng’ěr*." According to the *Shuǐjīng*: "The *Luò* River now rises in *Zhǒnglǐng* Mountain of *Shàngluò*, flowing northeast through *Hóngnóng*, and reaching *Gǒng* County of *Hénán* before entering the Yellow River." *Chénggāo* County also belongs to *Hénán*.

The *Fén* River rises north of *Shàngyǔ*, pronounced *yù*, and flows southwest into the Yellow River, entering south of *Píshì*. Now the *Fén* River rises in the former *Fényáng* County of *Jìnyáng* in *Tàiyuán*, flowing southeast through *Jìnyáng*, then southwest through *Píngyáng* of *Xīhé*, and reaching *Fényīn* of *Hédōng* before entering the Yellow River. *Píshì* County belongs to *Píngyáng*.

The *Qìn* River issues from the eastern slopes of *Jǐngxíng* Mountain and flows southeast into the Yellow River, entering southeast of *Huái*. *Huái* County belongs to *Hénèi*. North of *Huái* lies *Jǐngxíng* Mountain.

The *Jǐ* River rises south of *Gòng* Mountain in *Dōngqiū*—*gòng* is interchangeable with *gōng*. It cuts across *Jùlù* Marsh—*jué* meaning "to cross" or "to traverse." *Jùlù* now lies in *Gāopíng*. It flows into the *Bóhǎi* Sea, entering northeast of *Lánghuái* in *Qí*. Now the *Jǐ* River flows east from *Juǎn* County of *Róngyáng*, passing through *Chénliú* to the north of *Qiányīn*, then northeast through *Gāopíng*, further northeast through *Jǐnán*, and reaching *Bóchāng* County of *Lè’ān* before entering the sea—now *Jiéshí*.

The sources of these rivers often diverge from the accounts in the *Shuǐjīng*.

Considering the rivers and mountains may share names yet differ in reality, or share reality yet differ in names, or one reality may bear multiple names—what seems correct may be false, and what seems false may be correct. Moreover, with the passage of ages, ancient and modern names have shifted, and regional languages vary between *Chǔ* and *Xià*, making precise identification difficult.

The *Liáo* River rises east of *Wèi Gāo*, emerging from beyond the frontier at *Wèi Gāo* Mountain in *Xuántú*. *Gāogōulí* County has *Liáo* Mountain, which is also a source of the *Liáo* River. It flows west into the *Great Liáo*. (*Liáo* is pronounced like *liáo*.) It then flows southeast into the *Bóhǎi* Sea, entering at *Liáo*yáng. *Liáo*yáng County belongs to *Liáo*dōng.

The *Hū* Pond River rises south of *Jìnyáng* City, flows west to the north of *Yángqǔ*, then east into the *Bóhǎi* Sea, passing through *Lè* City in *Héjiān*, and northeast into the *Bóhǎi* Sea. *Jìnyáng* and *Yángqǔ* Counties belong to *Tàiyuán* Prefecture. It enters north of *Yuèzhāng* Commandery. (*Yuèzhāng* is the name of a commandery.)

The *Zhāng* River rises east of *Yáng*, flows east into the *Bóhǎi* Sea, entering south of *Zhāngwǔ*. *Xīnchéng* and *Biànyīn* Counties also have a *Zhāng* River.

In the fourth month of the first year of *Jiànpíng*, on the *bǐngxū* day, Expectant Appointee and Attendant Gentleman of the Ministry of Ceremonies, your servant *Wàng*, collated this text.

Overseen by the Palace Attendant, *Guānglùxūn*, your servant *Gōng*; the Palace Attendant, Chariot Master, and *Guānglù Dàfū*, your servant *Xiù*, who directed the review.

*Map of the Eastern Realm Within the Seas*
*Yù* Prefecture

The Southernmost Mountains extend beyond the Eastern Sea.
Without journeying, they arrive; without movement, they transform.
This is the work of the divine—things have no permanence.

*Hán-Yàn* begins as a *jiū*, the spirit of *Léizé*:
*Hán-Yàn* begins as a *jiū*, dwelling on an island in the sea.
The spirit of *Léizé* drums its belly, roaming in leisure.
*Lángyá* soars aloft, distant as cloud-borne towers.

*Jiānshā Jūyáo*, *Zhǔnduān Xǐ*□ Kingdom:
*Jiānshā Jūyáo*, *Zhǔnduān Xǐ*□—
A land of desert sands, a lodge at the world’s end.
Some were hostages to *Qín*, others guests of *Hàn*.

The Great River, the Northern River, the Southern River, the *Zhè* River,
*Lú*, *Huái*, *Xiāng*, *Hàn*, *Méng*, *Wēn*, *Yǐng*, *Rǔ*,
*Jīng*, *Wèi*, *Bái*, *Yuán*, *Gàn*, *Sì*, *Yù*, *Yì*,
*Huáng*, *Luò*, *Fén*, *Qìn*, *Jǐ*, *Lǎo*, *Hūchí*, the *Zhāng* River—
Waterways crisscross, waves unfurling like sashes.
Channels flow downward, weaving through the land beyond the *Huá*.
Diverging in source, converging in return, they merge in the eastern confluence.

*Classic of Mountains and Seas*, Scroll Thirteen—End
*Classic of Mountains and Seas*, Scroll Fourteen—Collated and Supplemented

**Guo Pu’s Commentary on *The Classic of Mountains and Seas: The Eastern Wilderness***

Beyond the Eastern Sea lies a great chasm. *The Odes, Containing the Spirit’s Mist* states: "It pours eastward into a bottomless valley." This refers to that chasm.

The land of **Shaohao**. Shaohao was the **Jintian Clan**, an epithet of **Emperor Zhi**. Shaohao nurtured **Emperor Zhuanxu** here—*nurtured* (*ru*), its meaning unclear. He abandoned his zither and se there, for the chasm is said to contain such instruments.

There is **Gan Mountain**, from which the **Gan River** flows, forming **Gan Abyss**—where waters gather to become a deep pool.

In the southeastern corner of the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Pimu Diqiu**.

Beyond the Eastern Sea, within the Great Wilderness, there is a mountain named **Dayan**, where the sun and moon rise.

There is **Bogu Mountain**, home to the **Land of Giants**. In the second year of the **Yongjia** era of Jin, a **crane-hawk** alighted in **Aobei Pond**, twenty *li* south of **Shian County**, where the local Zhou Hu captured it. A wooden arrow, tipped with an iron point, pierced it—six and a half *chi* in length. Judging by the arrow’s proportions, the archer must have stood fifteen or sixteen *chi* tall. Furthermore, **Gao Hui**, Vice Prefect of **Pingzhou**, recounted: Travelers from **Wa** were once blown by wind across the sea, where they encountered a land whose people all stood over ten *chi* tall, their forms resembling Hu. These must have been a branch of the **Tall Di**. The arrow likely came from that land.

*The Outer Traditions* state: The **Jiaoyao** people stand three *chi* tall, the shortest of all; the tallest do not exceed ten *zhang*. This is the limit of human stature. According to *The River Chart: Jade Tablet*: Nine thousand *li* north of **Kunlun**, one finds the **Longbo** people, thirty *zhang* tall, who live eighteen thousand years before dying. Ten thousand *li* east of Kunlun lies the land of the **Great Qin**, whose people stand ten *zhang* tall and all wear silk. Another ten thousand *li* east lies the **Tiao** people, three *zhang* and five *chi* tall. Ten thousand *li* beyond them is the **Middle Qin** land, where people stand one *zhang* tall.

*The Guliang Commentary* says: The **Tall Di** measure nine *mu* across the body; when they carry their heads, their brows appear above the crossbar of a chariot. These are the people of several *zhang* in height. In the Qin era, a giant appeared in **Lintao**, five *zhang* tall, with footprints six *chi* long. Judging by these accounts, the stature of such giants cannot be precisely measured.

There is a **Market of Giants**, called the **Hall of Giants**—also a mountain, shaped like a hall, where giants gather to trade. One giant crouches atop it, stretching his ears wide. *Crouches* (*cun*), sometimes written as *jun*, is an ancient form of *dun*. *The Zhuangzi* says: "Crouching on **Mount Kuaiji**."

There is a **Land of Little People** called the **Jing People**. *The Odes, Containing the Spirit’s Mist* states: "In the far northeast live people nine *cun* tall." This likely refers to these little people. Sometimes written as *Jing*, the pronunciation is the same.

There is a spirit with a human face and beast’s body, named **Li Qin** (pronunciation uncertain), the corpse of **Zhi**.

There is **Jue Mountain** (pronounced like *jue* in *deceit*). The **Yang River** flows from it.

There is the **Wei Kingdom**, whose people eat millet—this land contains only millet fields. *Wei* is pronounced *wei* (as in *false*).

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **He Xu**, where the sun and moon rise.

There is the **Zhongrong Kingdom**. **Emperor Jun** begot **Zhongrong**—*Jun* is a phonetic loan for *Shun*. The Zhongrong people eat beasts and the fruits of trees. This land has **red trees** and **black trees**, whose blossoms and fruits are beautiful, as recorded in *The Annals of Lü Buwei*. They employ four beasts: leopards, tigers, bears, and brown bears.

There is **Eastern Mouth Mountain**.

There is the **Land of Gentlemen**, whose people wear caps, sashes, and swords. They also employ tigers and leopards, for they value humility and deference.

There is the **Siyou Kingdom**. **Emperor Jun** begot **Yanlong**, Yanlong begot **Siyou**. Siyou begot **Thoughtful Scholars**, who take no wives, and **Thoughtful Women**, who take no husbands. Their people conceive through mutual longing and the communion of qi, bearing children without union—this is what *The Zhuangzi* describes as "white cranes gazing at one another, their pupils unmoving," eating millet and beasts, and employing four birds. They are transformed by the wind’s influence.

There is **Great Al Mountain**.

In the midst of the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Bright Star**, where the sun and moon emerge.

There is the **Land of the White People**. **Emperor Jun** begot **Emperor Hong**, and Emperor Hong begot the White People. The White People bear the surname Xiao, eat millet, and employ four beasts: tigers, leopards, bears, and brown bears. There is also the **Chariot-Yellow Beast**; riding it grants long life. There is the **Land of Green Hills**, where foxes with nine tails dwell. They appear as auspicious omens in times of peace.

There is the **Land of the Gentle Servants**, which is a land of fertile soil. "Fertile" here means rich and abundant. There is the **Land of Black Teeth**, where **Emperor Jun** begot the Black-Teeth People, whose teeth are black as lacquer. The sagely and divine transform without bound, thus their descendants often take on strange forms and unusual appearances. Those said to be "begotten" are generally their descendants, not necessarily their direct offspring. They bear the surname Jiang, eat millet, and employ four beasts.

There is the **Land of Xiazhou**. There is the **Land of Gaiyu**. There is a divine being with eight heads, a human face, the body of a tiger, and ten tails, named **Tianwu**, the Water Lord.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Juling Reaching Heaven**, also known as **Mount Ju**. **Eastern Extremity** and **Limou** are the names of three mountains. The sun and moon emerge here.

There is a spirit named **Zhedan**, a divine being. In the east, he is called **Zhe**; when summoned, he is called **Xu**. The east wind is called **Jun**, though the location of the east wind remains unclear. He dwells at the eastern extremity, controlling the entry and exit of the wind, regulating its flow in due time.

On an islet in the Eastern Sea resides a spirit with a human face and a bird’s body, encircled by two yellow serpents, with serpents threaded through its ears. It treads upon two yellow serpents and is named **Yu□**. The Yellow Emperor begot Yu□, and Yu□ begot **Yujing** (also known as **Yujian**). Yujing dwells in the Northern Sea, while Yu□ dwells in the Eastern Sea—both are sea gods, each governing a portion of the sea as its deity.

There is **Mount Zhaoyao**, from which the **Rong River** flows. There is a land called **Xuangu**, where the people are black as lacquer from the waist down. They eat millet and employ four beasts.

There is the **Land of the Yin People**, who bear the surname Gou and eat *yin*. There is a man named **Wang Hai**, holding birds in both hands while devouring one’s head. Wang Hai entrusted himself to the **Youyi** and the **River Lord’s Oxherd**, both of whom are human names, as recorded in the *Bamboo Annals of Ji Commandery*. The Youyi killed Wang Hai and seized the oxherd. The *Bamboo Annals* state: "Prince Hai of Yin, visiting the Youyi, indulged in debauchery. The lord of Youyi, Mianchen, killed him and flayed his body. Thus, King Jiawei of Yin borrowed troops from the River Lord to attack the Youyi, annihilating them and slaying their lord, Mianchen."

The River Lord remembered the Youyi, who secretly escaped and transformed into a land of beasts, devouring one another—this is the **Land of the Bo People**. **Emperor Shun** begot **Xi**, and Xi begot the Bo People. The Youyi were once close allies of the River Lord, but King Jiawei of Yin, a virtuous ruler, borrowed troops in righteous punishment. The River Lord, compelled by duty, aided in their destruction. Later, moved by pity for the Youyi, he allowed them to secretly transform into the Land of the Bo People.

Within the seas dwell two beings, transformations of the Youyi, named **Lady Chou**. This is the corpse of Lady Chou, for her transformations are boundless. Thus, those who traverse the ford of change and enter the realm of spirits find no place they cannot go, their traces manifesting wherever they are stirred. Even the likes of Fan Li have heard of such wonders.

Lady Chou possesses a great crab, a thousand *li* wide!

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **□Shaking Ram’s Head**, upon which grows the **Fu Tree**, its trunk rising three hundred *li* high, its leaves like those of the mustard plant. There is a valley called **Warm Spring Valley**, which is the **Boiling Valley**. Atop the Boiling Valley stands the Fu Tree, with the **Fusang** upon it. As one sun arrives, another departs, their movements alternating in succession, all borne by crows—these are the three-legged crows.

There is a spirit with the face of a man, the ears of a dog, and the body of a beast, who wears two blue-green serpents as earrings—this is **Shebishi**. There is a five-colored bird that faces its kin and discards sand. The meaning of "discarding sand" is unknown, nor is it known why **Di Jun** befriended it below. The Emperor descended to two altars, where the five-colored crows preside. It is said that beneath the mountain lie two altars of Shun, overseen by the five-colored crows.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Yitian Sumen**, where the sun and moon are born. There is the **Xuanmin** nation, their speech resembling clamor. There is **Qishan** (pronounced *ji*), as well as **Yaoshan**. There is **□shan** (pronounced like the *zeng* of a steamer), **Menhushan**, **Shengshan**, and **Daishan**. There are five-colored birds.

In the Eastern Wilderness lies a mountain named **Heming Junji**, where the sun and moon emerge. There is the **Zhongrong** nation. Beyond the northeastern sea are blue-green horses, three **zhui** (horses with mixed black and white manes), and **Ganhua**. Here lie **Yiyu**, three blue-green birds, three *zhui*, **Shirou**, **Ganhua**, **Ganzu**, and the hundred grains—all said to grow spontaneously. There is the nation of **Nühe Yuemu**.

There is a being named **□** (pronounced *wan*). The north is called **□**, and the wind that comes from it is called **□**. This is to say, it has two names (pronounced *yan*).

At this place in the easternmost corner, the sun and moon are restrained, preventing their alternating emergence and setting—thus governing their length. It is said that **□** oversees and examines the paired months’ ingress and egress, ensuring they do not intermingle or err, thereby discerning the short and long of their cycles.

In the northeastern corner of the Great Wilderness lies a mountain named **Xiongli Tuqiu**. **Yinglong** dwells in the southern extremity—**Yinglong** being a dragon with wings. He slew **Chiyou** and **Kua Fu**—**Chiyou** being the forger of weapons. Denied ascent thereafter, the world below suffered frequent droughts, for **Yinglong** remained beneath the earth, and none above could summon rain. When drought strikes, crafting an effigy of **Yinglong** brings forth great rain. Today’s earthen dragons trace their origin to this. The resonance of *qi* follows a natural, numinous response—beyond human contrivance.

In the Eastern Sea stands **Liubo Mountain**, seven thousand *li* from the shore. Upon it dwells a beast resembling an ox, its body azure, hornless, and possessing but a single foot. Whenever it enters or emerges from water, wind and rain arise; its radiance rivals the sun and moon, its form like thunder—its name is **Kui**. The **Yellow Emperor** captured it, fashioning its hide into a drum, striking it with the bones of the **Thunder Beast**—the **Thunder Beast** being the **Thunder Deity**, a being with a human face and dragon’s body who drums upon its belly. *Jue* here means "to strike." The sound carries five hundred *li*, instilling awe throughout the realm.

*End of Scroll Fourteen of the Classic of Mountains and Seas*
*Scroll Fifteen of the Classic of Mountains and Seas*

**The Southern Wilderness Classic**

Beyond the Southern Sea, west of the **Red Waters** and east of the **Flowing Sands**, the Red Waters issue from **Kunlun Mountain**, and the Flowing Sands from **Bell Mountain**. There is a beast with heads on both left and right, named **□Ti**. It emerges from the **□Ming Nation**—*Chuti* (two readings: *chu* and *ti*). Three azure beasts stand side by side, called **Shuangshuang** ("Paired"), their bodies merged as one. This is what the *Gongyang Commentary* refers to as "arriving in pairs."

There is **A Mountain**. In the midst of the Southern Sea lies **Floating Heaven Mountain**, where the Red Waters reach their end, their flow culminating at this peak. East of the Red Waters lies the **Cangwu Wilds**, where **Shun** and **Shu Jun** are buried. Shu Jun is **Shang Jun**. When Shun died on his imperial tour in Cangwu, he was interred there, and Shang Jun, who remained, was also buried in the same place. Their tombs now lie within the **Nine Doubts**. Here are found **patterned cowries** (purple cowries), **Li Yu** (the same as **Li Zhu**), **□Jiu**, **Ying Jia** (*Jia* being a kind of hawk), **Wei Wei**, bears, brown bears, elephants, tigers, leopards, wolves, and **gazing flesh**.

There is **Xing Mountain**, from which the **Xing River** flows. South of the **Black Waters** is a **dark serpent** that feeds on dust—modern **South Mountain**’s **Tiao serpents**, which swallow deer, belong to this kind. There is **Wu Mountain**, to its west the **Yellow Bird**. The **Emperor’s Medicines**, eight in number, are stored here—the divine elixirs of the **Celestial Emperor**. The Yellow Bird on Wu Mountain oversees this dark serpent, acting as its guardian.

Within the Great Wilderness stands **Untended Mountain**, where the Xing River reaches its end. There is a man with three bodies; **Emperor Jun**’s wife, **E Huang**, gave birth to this **Three-Bodied Nation**, likely the origin of their descendants. They bear the **Yao surname**, eat millet, and employ four birds. *Yao* is Shun’s clan name.

There is a square abyss, its four corners all connected. To the north, it joins the Black Waters; to the south, the Great Wilderness—*shu* here means "to link." Its northern side is called **Shaohe Abyss**, its southern side **Cong Abyss** (*cong* pronounced like the horse *cong*). This is where **Shun bathed**, where he once cleansed himself in its waters.

There is also **Cheng Mountain**, where the **Sweet Waters** reach their end. The Sweet Waters issue from **Gan Mountain**, their flow culminating here. There is the **Ji Yu Nation**, descendants of **Zhuan Xu**, who eat millet. This land’s people are the progeny of Zhuan Xu. There is the **Feathered People’s Nation**, whose inhabitants are all born with feathers. There is the **Egg-Born Nation**, whose people all hatch from eggs—meaning they are oviparous.

Within the Great Wilderness stands **Bujiang Mountain**, where the Black Waters reach their end. The Black Waters issue from Kunlun Mountain. There is also **Jia Mountain**, from which the **Qi River** flows. Further, there is **Yan Mountain**, and **Dengbei Mountain** (also called **Dengbao Mountain**, where the multitude of shamans ascend and descend). There is **Jiejie Mountain** (*jie* pronounced like the *qi* in "contract"). There is **Pu Mountain**, from which the **Li River** flows, and **Wei Mountain** (*Wei* pronounced like the *wei* in "Wei Ao"). To its west lies cinnabar; to its east, jade.

Further south is a mountain from which the **Piao River** flows (*piao* pronounced like "whirlwind"). There is **Wei Mountain** and **Cui Mountain**—this mountain is home to the **kingfisher bird**. There is the **Ying People’s Nation**, of the **Yu surname**, who eat millet. There are also people who feed on tree leaves.

There is the **Land of Immortals**, of the **A surname**, who eat the **sweetwood**—the sweetwood being the **tree of immortality**, whose consumption prevents aging. Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain named **Qu Chi** ("Departing Convulsions"). To the south, it bears fruit; to the north, it does not—*qu chi* is pronounced like the *chi* in "wind convulsions," though its meaning is unclear.

On an islet in the Southern Sea dwells a spirit with a human face, wearing two azure serpents as earrings, named **Bu Ting Hu Yu** (a spirit’s name). There is a spirit named **Yin Yin Hu**, called **Yin Hu** in the south and **Hu Min** by the boastful winds—it has two names. It dwells at the southern pole, controlling the entry and exit of winds.

There is **Xiang Mountain**, and **Chongyin Mountain** ("Double Shade"). There are people who eat beasts, called **Ji Li**. Emperor Jun fathered Ji Li, hence the name **Ji Li Nation**. There is **Min Abyss** (*min* pronounced like "dusk"). **Shaohao** fathered **Bei Fa**, who descended to dwell in Min Abyss.

There are square waters named **Jun Altar**. The waters resemble an earthen altar, hence the name **Shun Altar**. There is the **Zai People’s Nation**. Emperor Shun fathered **Wu Yin**, who descended to dwell here, known as the **Wu Zai People**. The Wu Zai People bear the **Pan surname**, eat grain, yet neither spin nor weave their clothing, nor sow nor reap their food—meaning they have natural cloth and self-growing grains. What is sown is called *jia*; what is reaped is called *se*.

Here are birds that sing and dance: the **luan bird** sings of its own accord, the **phoenix** dances of its own accord. Here gather a hundred beasts, living in herds. Here the hundred grains converge.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain named **Rongtian** ("Heaven-Fusing"), where the sea’s waters enter from the south. There is a man called **Zaochi** ("Chisel-Teeth"), whom **Yi** slew—meaning Yi shot and killed him. There is **Yu Mountain**. There is the **Yu People’s Nation** (*yu* pronounced like the same). They bear the **Sang surname**, eat millet, and feed on **yu**—*yu* being the **short fox**, resembling a turtle, which spits sand to shoot people, causing illness and death. This mountain produces them, hence its name.

There is a man drawing a bow, shooting a yellow serpent, named the **Yu Archer**. There is **Song Mountain**, home to a red serpent called the **Green Serpent**. A tree grows upon this mountain, named **maple**—the maple is what **Chi You**’s shackles transformed into after Chi You was captured by the Yellow Emperor, bound, and executed. His discarded bonds became this tree, now called the maple—today’s **fengxiang tree**.

There is a man with tiger’s teeth and a tiger’s tail, named **Zu Zhuang’s Corpse** (*zu* pronounced like the *za* in "wild pear"). There are little people, the **Jiao Yao Nation**, of the **Ji surname**, all three *chi* tall, who eat **fine grains**.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain named **□Tu Mountain** (*tu* pronounced like "rot"). The **Azure Waters** reach their end here. The Azure Waters issue from Kunlun Mountain. There is **Cloud Rain Mountain**, upon which grows a tree named **Le** ("Joy"). **Yu** attacked Cloud Rain—*gong* here means "to fell its forests."

Here the **Redstone** gives birth to Joy—its roots yellow, branches crimson, leaves azure. The **August Ones** gather medicine from it, for its blossoms and fruits are all divine drugs.

There is a land called **Zhuan Xu**, where **Bo Fu** is born, who eats millet. There is a land of the **You** surname (*you* pronounced like the *yu* in "citron"). There is **Tiao Mountain**. There is **Zong Mountain**. There is **Xing Mountain**. There is **He Mountain**. There is **Chen Zhou Mountain**. There is **Dong Zhou Mountain**. There is **Whitewater Mountain**, from which the **Whitewater** issues, giving rise to the **White Abyss**, where the disciples of **Kun Wu** bathe. **Kun Wu** is the title of an ancient king. The *Pronunciation and Meaning* states: **Kun Wu** is a mountain name, whose streams yield fine metal. The two accounts differ, and none can discern which is correct.

There is a man named **Zhang Hong**, who fishes upon the sea. Within the sea lies the **Land of Zhang Hong**; some say it is the land of the **Qi Gong** people, though this is doubtful. He eats fish and commands four birds. There is another man, beaked like a bird and winged, who enters the sea to catch fish.

Within the Great Wilderness there is a man named **Huan Tou**. **Gun**’s wife was **Shi Jing**, and **Shi Jing**’s son was **Yan Rong**, who begot **Huan Tou**. **Huan Tou** has the face of a man, the beak of a bird, and wings. He eats fish from the sea and walks with the aid of his wings, for though he has wings, they cannot bear him in flight—he relies on them only as a staff. He eats **white millet**, **broomcorn millet**, and **black millet**. *The Master Guan* speaks of the crops suited to the land, saying: its grains are **broomcorn**, **chaste tree**, and **black millet**, all kinds of millet. *Ju* is black millet; the modern character adds the "grain" radical. It has three pronunciations: *qi*, *ju*, and *qiu*. There is the **Land of Huan Tou**.

The **Divine Yao**, **Divine Ku**, and **Divine Shun** are buried upon **Yue Mountain**, which is **Di Mountain**. Here dwell **patterned cowries**, **Li Yu**, **□ Jiu**, **hawk merchants**, **Yan Wei**, **flesh that sees**, bears, brown bears, tigers, and leopards. There is a tree with crimson branches and azure blossoms, bearing black fruit.

There is **Shen Mountain**. Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain named **Heaven’s Terrace** (*a lofty mountain*), into which the sea’s waters flow.

Beyond the **Eastern Sea**, between the **Sweet Waters**, lies the **Land of Xi He**. There is a woman named **Xi He**, who bathes the sun in the **Sweet Abyss**. **Xi He** is she who, at the dawn of heaven and earth, presided over the sun and moon. Thus *The Oracle of Qi* says: "The verdant vastness of Hollow Mulberry, the Eight Poles now spread wide—then came **Xi He**, who governs the sun and moon, whose duty is their ingress and egress, creating dusk and dawn." It also says: "Gazing upon the heavens above, now bright, now dark, there is the child of **Xi He**, emerging from **Yang Valley**." Therefore, **Yao** established the office of **Xi He** to oversee the four seasons, and her descendants became this land. They fashion images of the sun and moon and tend to them, bathing and revolving them within the **Sweet Waters** to mimic their rising from **Yang Valley** and setting in **Yu Abyss**—so the world does not lose its proper order.

**Xi He** is the wife of **Di Jun** and mother of the **Ten Suns**. She bore ten children, each named after a sun, hence the saying "mother of the Ten Suns," meaning ten in number.

There is **Yi You Mountain**, upon which grows the **Sweet Ancestor**, its branches and leaves all crimson, its leaves yellow, blossoms white, and fruit black. To the east grows the **Sweet Blossom**, its trunk and branches all crimson, its leaves yellow. There are **azure horses** and **crimson horses**, called the **Three Bays**. There is **flesh that sees**. There are little people called **Fungus Folk** (*jun* pronounced like the *jun* in "morning fungus").

There is **Southkind Mountain**, where dwell **leftover jades**, **azure horses**, the **Three Bays**, **flesh that sees**, **Sweet Blossoms**, and the **Hundred Grains**.

*Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Fifteen ends*
*Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Sixteen*

Guo Pu’s Commentary on the *Classic of Mountains and Seas: The Great Wilderness West*

Beyond the Northwest Sea, at the corner of the Great Wilderness, stands a mountain that does not join, called **Unjoined Child**. The *Huainanzi* states: "Of old, Gonggong contended with Zhuanxu for the throne. In rage, he struck **Unjoined Mountain**. The heavenly cords snapped, the earthly pillars broke." Thus, this mountain remains cleft and incomplete.

Two yellow beasts guard it.

There is a body of water called **Cold-and-Heat Waters**. To its west lies **Damp Mountain**, and to its east, **Screen Mountain** (*Mu*, pronounced like the *mo* in "screen").

There is **Yu’s Attack on Gonggong’s State Mountain**. It is said that Yu attacked Gonggong’s state and slew its ministers here, suppressing them at this mountain. The *Qi Shi* states: "Gonggong had the face of a man, the body of a serpent, and crimson hair. He ruled a state called **Refined Scholar**, and was a son of Zhuanxu." It is also said that he descended from the Gaoyang clan.

There are ten spirits called **Nüwa’s Intestines**, transformed into gods and dwelling in the **Chestnut Expanse** wilds. Nüwa was an ancient goddess and sovereign, with the face of a human and the body of a serpent, who transformed seventy times in a single day. Her intestines became these spirits. **Chestnut Expanse** is the name of the wilds. (*Jiao*, pronounced like the *gua* in "melon.") They dwell athwart the path, said to be severed remnants.

There is a being named **Stone Yi**, whose wind is called **Wei** (or *Ben*, in some versions). He dwells in the northwest corner, governing the length of days and nights, overseeing the measurements of the sun and moon’s shadows.

There is a five-colored bird with a crest, called the **Mad Bird**. The *Erya* states: "The *wangliang* is this bird."

There is **Great Ford’s Long Mountain**, and the **Land of the White People**.

Beyond the Northwest Sea, east of the **Red Waters**, lies the **Land of Long Victory**, where people’s strides span five feet.

There is the **State of Western Zhou**, of the Ji clan, who eat grains.

There is a man plowing, named **Shu Jun**. **Di Jun** begot **Ji** (also called **Ku**; Ku’s second consort bore **Hou Ji**). Ji descended with the Hundred Grains. Ji’s younger brother was **Tai** (*Tai*, pronounced like the *tai* in "fetus"). **Xi** begot **Shu Jun**, who took his father’s and Ji’s place to sow the Hundred Grains, thus beginning agriculture.

There is the **Red State’s Wife Clan**, and **Twin Mountains**.

The waters of the Western Sea lie within the Great Wilderness.

There is **Square Mountain**, upon which grows a verdant tree called the **Cabinet-Frame Pine** (*Juge* pine; *ju* pronounced like the *ju* in "measure"). The sun and moon enter here to rest.

Beyond the Western Sea, west of the **Red Waters**, lies the **Land of the Ancestral People**, who eat grains and command the four birds.

There is the **State of the Northern Di**, descended from **Shi Jun**, grandson of the **Yellow Emperor**. **Shi Jun** begot the Northern Di.

There is **Mang Mountain**. There is **Cassia Mountain**. There is **Yao Mountain**, so named for its abundance of cassia and *yao* trees. Upon it dwells one called **Crown Prince Long Qin**. **Zhuan Xu** begot **Lao Tong**—*The Genealogies* states: "Zhuan Xu took a wife from the Tengfen clan, called **Lady Lu**, who bore **Lao Tong**." **Lao Tong** begot **Zhu Rong** (also called **Chong Li**), who served as the **Fire Minister** under **High Lord Xin** and was titled **Zhu Rong**. **Zhu Rong** begot **Crown Prince Long Qin**, who dwelled on **Yao Mountain** and first created the music of the wind. He composed the melody *Retained Wind*.

There are three five-colored birds: one called the **Phoenix Crow**, one the **Luan Bird**, and one the **Fenghuang**.

There is a creature shaped like a hare, its torso bare where it is bare—its skin’s blue-green hue renders it invisible, exposing only its bare flesh. It resembles an ape, its movements slow, much like a simian.

In the heart of the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Fengju Jade Gate**, where the sun and moon descend. There are the **Spirit Mountain**, and the shamans **Wu Xian**, **Wu Ji**, **Wu Xi**, **Wu Peng**, **Wu Gu**, **Wu Zhen**, **Wu Qi**, **Wu [missing character]**, **Wu Xie**, and **Wu Luo**—ten shamans who ascend and descend this peak, where all the hundred herbs abide. It is said that the multitude of shamans gather upon this mountain to harvest them.

There are the **Mountain of the Queen Mother of the West**, the **Sea Mountain**, and the **Gorge Mountain**—all renowned peaks of the vast emptiness. There is the **Land of Fertility**, so named for its rich and abundant soil. The **Fertile People** dwell here, feasting upon the eggs of the **Phoenix Crow** and drinking the **Sweet Dew**. Whatever flavors they desire, all are present; whatever tastes they crave, none are absent.

Here grow the **Sweet Blossoms**, the **Sweet Hawthorn**, the **White Willows**, the **Sight-Flesh**, the **Three Steeds**, the **Xuan Gui** (a kind of jade), the **Yao Bi** (precious jade), the **White Wood** (a tree with pure white bark, now known as the **Southern Veined Wood**, which is also black), the **White Cinnabar**, the **Green Cinnabar**, and the **Black Cinnabar**. *The* **Ying of the Filial Classic** *states: "When a ruler’s virtue reaches the mountains and hills, Black Cinnabar emerges."* Thus, these are distinct minerals, just as white and black are distinguished. There is also **Silver Iron**.

The **Luan Birds** sing of their own accord, and the **Phoenixes** dance spontaneously. Here, a hundred beasts gather and dwell together—this is called the **Wilds of the Fertile People**. There are three **Azure Birds**, with red heads and black eyes. One is called the **Great Harmony Crow**, another the **Lesser Li**—both are messengers of the **Queen Mother of the West**.

There is the **Terrace of the Xuanyuan**, where archers dare not face west, out of reverence for the **Terrace of Xuanyuan**—a homage to the **August Emperor**.

In the Great Wilderness lies **Dragon Mountain**, where the sun and moon descend. There are three turbid waters called the **Three Tossings**, which the **Kun Wu** consume. *The* **Tale of King Mu** *states: "The Taoshui River, consumed by the turbid Rong people, belongs to this same category."*

There is a figure clad in blue, sleeves covering its face—*袂 (sleeves), pronounced as "zhi"*—named the **Corpse of the Queen**. There is the **Land of Women**. When the lords of the **Land of Fertile Ancestors** reached its easternmost border and inquired of its elders, they were told: "Our people once set sail to fish and were blown by the wind for dozens of days. To the east, we sighted a land amidst the great sea where only women dwell, with no men—this is that very land."

There are the **Peach Mountain**, the **Yu Mountain**, the **Cassia Mountain**, and the **Dry Earth Mountain**. There is the **Land of Husbands**, where no women reside. There is the **Mountain of Yan Zhou**. A five-colored bird pecks at the heavens, gaping to exhale upon the sky—it is called the **Singing Bird**. Here, the winds carry a hundred kinds of music, song, and dance. It is said that a hundred varieties of performing arts and dances all possess their own wind-borne melodies.

There is the **Land of Xuanyuan**, whose people have human faces and serpentine bodies. South of the **Jiang Mountains**, dwelling in the mountains is auspicious—this refers to the edge of the **Barren Mountains**. Those who reside in the mountains are said to be free from misfortune and calamity. The long-lived may reach eight hundred years, while some endure for thousands.

In the shallows of the Western Sea dwell divine beings with human faces and avian bodies, entwined with two green serpents and treading upon two red serpents—they are called the **Yan Zi**.

In the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called the **Moon Mountain**, the **Pole of Heaven**. The **Gate of Heaven of Wu Ji** is where the sun and moon descend. There is a divine being with a human face but no arms, its two feet bent backward to attach to its head—it is called the **Xu**. *Xu* signifies wailing.

**Zhuan Xu** begot **Lao Tong**, and **Lao Tong** begot **Chong** and **Li**. *The* **Genealogies** *states: "Lao Tong took a wife from the Genshui clan, called Jiao Fu, who bore Chong and Li."* The Emperor commanded **Chong** to ascend to heaven and **Li** to descend to earth. In ancient times, humans and spirits intermingled without order, so **Zhuan Xu** appointed the **Southern Regulator** to govern the heavens and the affairs of the spirits, and the **Great Regulator Li** to govern the earth and the affairs of humanity. **Chong** indeed ascended to heaven, and **Li** indeed governed the earth—though the details of their offerings remain unclear. From this descent to earth arose **Ye**, who dwells in the westernmost reaches, overseeing the movements of the sun, moon, and the hundred [celestial bodies]. It is said that he observes the degrees and lodgings of the sun, moon, and stars.

There is a figure with arms bent backward, called the **Heavenly Warden**. There is a **Red Corpse**, also called the **Warden**. There is a woman bathing the moon. **Di Jun’s** wife, **Chang Xi**, bore twelve moons—this is the origin of their bathing. Together with **Xi He**, she bathes the sun and moon.

There is the **Mountain of Black Cinnabar**, which produces **Black Cinnabar**. There is a five-colored bird with a human face and hair. Here dwell the **Azure Bird**, the **Yellow Bird**, and the **Ao Bird**—*Ao, pronounced as "ao"*—whose gathering of blue and yellow foretells the fall of a nation.

There is a pool called the **Meng Yi’s Attack on Zhuan Xu’s Pool**. **Meng Yi** is a person’s name.

In the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Chen Ao**—*Ao, pronounced as "ao"*—a colossal peak where the sun and moon descend. There is a beast with heads on both sides, called the **Ping Feng**—that is, the **Bing Feng**, a name that shifts in pronunciation.

There is the **Gorge Mountain**, the **Golden Gate Mountain**, where dwells one named the **Corpse of Yellow Ji**. There are the **Biwing Birds**. There is a white bird with azure wings, a yellow tail, and a black beak—an extraordinary bird.

There is a red dog called the **Heavenly Dog**; wherever it descends, war follows. *The* **Book of Zhou** *states: "Where the Heavenly Hound halts, the land is scorched. Its radiance flies through the heavens as a meteor, stretching dozens of yards long, swift as the wind, resounding like thunder, flashing like lightning."* The howling of the **Heavenly Dog** once passed through the lands of **Wu** and **Chu**, dividing the wilds of the seven states.

South of the Western Sea, beyond the **Flowing Sands**, behind the **Red Waters**, and before the **Black Waters**, stands a mountain called the **Kunlun Mound**. There dwells a divine being with a human face and a tiger’s body, adorned with patterns and a tail, all marked in white—its tail is speckled with white. Below it lies the abyss of **Weak Waters**, encircling the mountain, whose waters cannot even bear a goose feather. Beyond it rises the **Mountain of Blazing Fire**, where anything that touches it instantly ignites.

Now, ten thousand *li* east of **Funan**, there lies the kingdom of **Qibo**. Five thousand *li* east of Qibo lies the kingdom of **Fire Mountain**. Though thunder and rain assail the mountain, its flames never cease. Within the fire dwell white rats, which at times emerge along the mountainside to seek food. When captured by humans, their fur is woven into cloth—this is the famed **fire-washed cloth** of today, a product of such fire mountains.

There is a being with tiger’s fangs worn as a crown and a tail dwelling in a cave, known as the **Queen Mother of the West**. The *Hetu Jade Tablet* also states: "The Queen Mother of the West resides on **Kunlun Mountain**." The *Classic of Mountains: The West* says: "The Queen Mother of the West dwells on **Jade Mountain**." The *Tale of King Mu* records: "Thus, he inscribed his name upon the stone of **Yanshan**, calling it the Mountain of the Queen Mother of the West." Therefore, though the Queen Mother of the West makes Kunlun her palace, she also has separate chambers and wandering retreats, never confined to a single mountain. Hence, chroniclers each describe what they have witnessed.

This mountain possesses all things under heaven. In the midst of the **Great Wilderness**, there is a mountain called **Changyang**, where the sun and moon set. There lies the kingdom of **Hanhuan**, where two figures dwell: **Nüji** and **Nümie**, who hold eels and sacrificial stands. The kingdom of **Shouma**—the *Master Lü’s Spring and Autumn Annals* states: "In the western reaches lies Shouma, and to the north, **Dadong**." There are two others: **Nanyue** and **Quzhou**, named **Nüqian**. Nüqian bore **Jige**, who in turn begot Shouma. Shouma stands upright without shadow and shouts without echo, for his form and *qi* differ from those of ordinary men. The *Biographies of the Immortals* says: "Xuan Su, also called Yuan Jing." Here, the heat is so intense that none may approach, for its scorching blaze would slay a man.

There is a headless figure wielding shield and spear, called the **Corpse of Yougeng**—akin to the **Corpse of Xingtian**. Thus, when **Cheng Tang** vanquished **Xia Jie** at **Zhang Mountain**, he severed Yougeng’s head from his front. Though decapitated, Yougeng stood upright, fleeing his guilt to evade punishment, and descended upon **Wushan**. He concealed himself in Wushan, which now lies in **Wuxian County** of **Jianping**. There is one named **Wu Hui**, whose son **Zi Qi** had no right arm—this is **Qigong**. Wu Hui was the younger brother of **Zhurong**, the fire minister.

There is the kingdom of **Gaishan**, where grows a tree with red bark, green branches, and verdant leaves, called the **Zhu Tree**—or sometimes the **Sanqi Tree**. There is also the **One-Armed People**. Beneath the North Pole, too, dwell one-legged men, as seen in the *Hetu Jade Tablet*. In the midst of the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Great Wilderness Mountain**, where the sun and moon set. There is a three-faced being, a son of **Zhuanxu**, with three faces and one arm—his name is **Qi**, and he lacks a right arm. The three-faced man does not die, for his head bears a

**Mountain Records of the Classic of Mountains and Seas, Scroll Seventeen**
*Guo Pu’s Commentary*
**The Classic of the Great Wilderness: North**
