Beyond the northeast sea, amid the Great Wilderness, between the waters of the River, lies **Fuyu Mountain**, where **Emperor Zhuanxu** and his nine consorts are buried. This is likely a tomb built according to the customs of a foreign land.

Here abound **long-lasting turtles**, **patterned cowries**, **liyu birds**, **luan birds**, **phoenixes**, and creatures great and small—all things are fully present. **Azure birds**, **lang birds**, **dark birds**, **yellow birds**, **tigers**, **leopards**, **bears**, **brown bears**, **yellow serpents**, **flesh that sees**, **xuangui jade**, and **yaobi jade** all emerge from **Weiyu Mountain**, which lies at its edge.

There is a mound, three hundred *li* in circumference. South of the mound stands **Emperor Jun’s Bamboo Grove**, where the bamboo is so large a single segment may serve as a boat. South of the bamboo grove lies a crimson marsh, its waters dyed red, called **Fengyuan**—*feng* meaning "great crimson." There are three mulberry trees without branches, each a hundred *ren* tall to the south.

West of the mound lies **Chenyuan**, where the customs of **Zhuanxu’s Hu Buyu Kingdom** persist—a land later known only by name. Today, the small tongues of the Hu and Yi peoples all speak thus. They bear clan names and eat millet.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Bu Xian**. There lies the kingdom of **Sushen**. Today, the land of Sushen lies over three thousand *li* from Liaodong, where its people dwell in caves, wear no clothing, and clothe themselves in pigskins. In winter, they anoint their bodies with grease, several *fen* thick, to ward off wind and cold. Their people are all skilled in crafting red bows, four *chi* long and exceedingly strong. Their arrows are made of *hu* wood, one *chi* and eight *cun* long, with arrowheads of green stone—these are the arrows received by Marquis Chi of Chen in the Spring and Autumn period. In the second year of the Taixing era of Jin, Governor Cui Kun of Pingzhou dispatched his aide Gao Hui to present bows and arrows of the Sushen people, whose arrowheads were made of bone. When asked, they said these were obtained through trade with inland kingdoms. Today, it is called the **Yiling Kingdom**, which produces fine sable and red jade—perhaps these come from the outer sea. The *Book of Later Han* refers to it as the **Yilou Kingdom**.

There are **fei zhi**, creatures with four wings. *Fei* is pronounced *zhi* in another reading. There are also insects with the heads of beasts and the bodies of serpents, called **qin insects**—a kind of serpent. There is a man called the **Elder**. There is the **Kingdom of the Elder**, whose people bear the clan name Li and eat millet.

There is a great green serpent with a yellow head that devours deer. Today, in the south, the python also eats deer, and deer belong to the same kind as the *tang* beast. There is **Yu Mountain**. There is **Xuan Gun’s Attack on Chengzhou Mountain**—both mountains are named after their legends.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Hengtian**. There is **Xianmin Mountain**. There is a **pan tree** a thousand *li* in extent. There is the kingdom of **Shu Chu**—*chu* is pronounced *gan*, or *chu* in another reading. They are descendants of **Zhuanxu**, with millet for heads, and they employ four beasts: **tigers**, **leopards**, **bears**, and **brown bears**. There is a black creature resembling a bear, called the **zhu zhu**—or sometimes written as *lang*, pronounced *xi*.

There is the kingdom of **Qi**, of the Jiang clan, which employs **tigers**, **leopards**, **bears**, and **brown bears**.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Guang Kan’s Great Feng Mountain**, where the **River** and the **Ji** enter, flowing north into the sea. After the River and Ji empty into the sea, they re-emerge beyond the sea in this mountain. To its northwest lies **Yu’s Accumulated Stones**, also called **Qingyang Mountain**.

There is **Shun Mountain**, from which the **Shun River** flows. There is the kingdom of **Shizhou**. There is **Cinnabar Mountain**, which yields pure cinnabar. The *Bamboo Annals* state: "He Jia conquered and obtained a mountain of cinnabar." Today, cinnabar is also mined from mountains and earthen caves.

There is a great marsh a thousand *li* in extent, where the **Zhang Bird** sheds its feathers. The *Tale of King Mu* says: "Arriving at the vast plains, where flying birds shed their feathers, he hunted birds and beasts, taking the finest specimens, and loaded a hundred chariots with feathers." The *Bamboo Annals* also records: "King Mu marched north, traversing a thousand *li* of flowing sands, amassing feathers for a thousand *li*—all referring to this marsh."

There is the **Kingdom of the Hairy People**, whose inhabitants all have human faces and bodies covered in hair. They bear the clan name Yi, eat millet, and employ four beasts. **Yu** begot the kingdom of **Jun**, **Jun** begot **Yi Lai**, **Yi Lai** begot **Xun Jia**—*jia* is pronounced *dan xia*. **Xun Jia** killed the **Chuo people**—*Chuo* being their name. The Emperor, moved by this, secretly established a kingdom for them, concealing its origins. These are the Hairy People.

There is the **Kingdom of the Pendulous-Eared**, of the Ren clan, whose people have large ears that hang down, resting on their shoulders. The Vermilion Bird’s **Dian Er** carvings depict ears reaching the shoulders, imitating these people. **Yu Hao’s son** eats grain. It is said that on the islets of the Northern Sea, they cultivate millet to sustain themselves—this refers to **Yu Qiang**.

There is a spirit with a human face and a bird’s body, wearing two green serpents as earrings and treading upon two red serpents, named **Yu Qiang**.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **North Pole’s Heavenly Accumulation**—*ji* is pronounced *yi*—where the northern waters of the sea flow. There is a spirit with nine human heads and a bird’s body, named the **Nine Phoenixes**. There is another spirit that holds serpents in its mouth and grasps serpents in its hands, with the head of a tiger, the body of a man, four hooves, and long elbows, named **Qiang Liang**—also among the dreaded beasts.

Within the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Chengdu’s Loaded Heaven**. There is a man who wears two yellow serpents as earrings and holds two yellow serpents in his hands, named **Kua Fu**. **Houtu** begot **Xin**, **Xin** begot **Kua Fu**. Kua Fu, overestimating his strength, sought to chase the sun’s shadow and pursued it to **Yu Valley**—the ravine where the sun is said to set.

Here now is the translation:

---

Now it is called Yu. He was about to drink from the Yellow River, but it was not enough. He then raced toward the Great Marsh, yet before he arrived, he perished here—dying of thirst. After **Yinglong** had slain **Chiyou**, he also killed **Kua Fu**. Above it is said that Kua Fu, overestimating his strength, competed with the sun and died; here it is again said that he was slain by Yinglong. His death has no fixed account, for events are recorded as they arise, making clear that his transformations are boundless and beyond measure.

Yinglong then departed to dwell in the south, and so the south has abundant rain. It speaks of dragons as creatures of water, for like attracts like. There is the **Land of the Gutless**, which belongs to the **Ren** clan. Its people are tall. They have no heirs, and they eat fish. *Ji* is also written as *qi*, referring to the intestines. **Gonggong’s** minister was named **Xiangyao**—also called **Xiangliu**, with the name’s sound shifting in speech. He had nine heads and a serpent’s body, coiling upon himself, meaning he was twisted and spiraled. He fed upon nine mountains, speaking of his greed and savagery. Wherever he dwelled, it became barren—*ni* and *ya*, like the sound of spitting and choking, meaning he stopped up the land. Thus it became a source of marshes, speaking of his great strength. His misfortune brought suffering, for his qi was harsh and fierce. No beast could inhabit the same place, speaking of the fear he inspired.

When **Yu** dammed the floodwaters, he killed Xiangyao. Yu blocked the vast deluge, drowning him in the process. His blood was foul and reeking, unable to nurture grain, and his land was waterlogged, unfit for habitation. It speaks of how his fat and blood flowed and pooled into abysses. Yu dammed it, piling earth three times and sinking three times—speaking of Yu’s earthen barriers and the serpent’s collapse into the soil. He then turned it into a pool. The land was parched, so the **Di** emperors took advantage of this to build a terrace, for low ground is suitable for accumulation. Thus the many emperors came together here to construct the terrace. It lies north of **Kunlun**.

There is a mountain called **Yue Mountain**, where **Xun bamboo** grows—great bamboo. In the midst of the Great Wilderness stands a mountain named **Buju**, where the northern sea waters flow in. There is **Xikun Mountain**, where **Gonggong’s Terrace** stands. Archers dare not face north toward it, speaking of the fear it inspires. There is a person clad in blue-green robes, named **Ba**, the daughter of the **Yellow Emperor**—*ba* is pronounced like *drought*. When Chiyou forged weapons to attack the Yellow Emperor, the Yellow Emperor commanded Yinglong to strike him in the wilds of **Jizhou**—Jizhou being the central land. The Yellow Emperor also trained tigers, leopards, bears, and brown bears to battle **Yan Di** in the wilds of **Banyuan**, annihilating him. See the *Records of the Grand Historian*.

Yinglong stored up water, while Chiyou summoned the **Wind Earl** and **Rain Master**, bringing forth a great storm. The Yellow Emperor then sent down the heavenly maiden **Ba**, and the rain ceased, allowing him to slay Chiyou. Ba could not return above, and wherever she dwelled, there was no rain, for the drought’s qi remained. **Shu Jun** spoke of this to the emperor, who later banished her to the north of the **Red Waters**, exiling her far away. Shu Jun then became the **Field Ancestor**, overseeing the fields. As the *Book of Songs* says: *“The Field Ancestor is divine.”* Ba was at times lost, for she feared being driven away. Those who wished to expel her commanded the Field Ancestor to proceed northward toward the water’s position. First, they cleared the waterways, dredging and connecting ditches and channels. It speaks of how driving her away would bring rain, so they first improved the waterways—this is the origin of today’s practice of expelling Ba.

There is a people who are eating fish, named the **Land of the Deep-Eyed**. They belong to the **Xi** clan and eat fish. They are also of the **Hu** kind, though their eyes are deeply set, for the Yellow Emperor’s time reached them. There is **Zhong Mountain**, where a woman clad in blue-green robes is named **Xian**, the maiden of the **Red Waters**—a divine woman.

In the midst of the Great Wilderness stands a mountain called **Rongfu Mountain**, where the waters flow in order. There is a people named the **Dog Rong**. The Yellow Emperor begot **Miaolong**, Miaolong begot **Rongwu**, Rongwu begot **Bianming**, Bianming begot the **White Dog**, and the White Dog had two dogs, male and female, meaning they paired among themselves. Thus they became the Dog Rong, who harm and devour. There is a red beast there, formless and headless, named **Rongxuanwang’s Corpse**—the spirit name of the Dog Rong.

There is a mountain called **Qizhou Mountain**, also known as **Zeng Mountain**—*zeng* is pronounced *qian*—**Xianye Mountain**, and **Yu Mountain**. There is a people with a single eye, growing in the center of their face. Another account says they belong to the **Wei** clan, descendants of **Shaohao**, and eat millet. There are the **Jiwujiwu** people of the **Ren** clan—*ji* is also written as *qi*, meaning the navel—who have no bones and eat qi and fish. It speaks of the existence of boneless people. The *Master Shi* says: *“King Xu of Xu had sinews but no bones.”*

East of the **Flowing Sands** beyond the northwest sea lies a land called **Zhonglun**, descendants of **Zhuanxu**, who eat millet. There is a land named **Laiqiu**. There is the **Land of the Dog Rong**. There is a spirit with a human face and beast’s body, also named the **Dog Rong**.

North of the **Black Waters** beyond the northwest sea dwell winged people, named the **Miao People**—the people of the **Three Miao**. Zhuanxu begot **Huantou**, Huantou begot the Miao People, who belong to the **Li** clan. There is a mountain named **Zhang Mountain**. In the Great Wilderness stand **Heng Stone Mountain**, **Nine Yin Mountain**, and **Huiye Mountain**, upon which grows a red tree with green leaves, named **Bitter Artistry**. It stands at the western extremity of Kunlun, its leaves glowing red and illuminating the earth below.

There is the **Land of the Niu Li**. There are people without bones, the **Dang’erzi**—the Dang’er people give birth to boneless children. North of the **Red Waters** beyond the northwest sea stands **Zhangwei Mountain**. There is a spirit with a human face and a red serpent’s body, a thousand *li* in length. His eyes are straight and aligned, though the meaning of *zheng cheng* is unclear. When he closes his eyes, it is dark; when he opens them, it is bright—speaking of how his sight brings day and his closure brings night. He does not eat, does not sleep, does not rest, and summons wind and rain. It speaks of his power to invoke wind and rain. He illuminates the **Nine Yin**, shining upon the hidden depths of the Nine Yin. Thus he is called **Zhulong**. The *Li Sao* says: *“Where the sun does not reach, how does Zhulong shine?”* The *Han of the Divine* says: *“The northwest of the world is incomplete, lacking the ebb and flow of yin and yang, so there is a dragon holding the essence of fire to illuminate the midst of the Heavenly Gate.”*

**Completion of the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*, Scroll Seventeen**

*Collation Notes:*
1. *Liaoshou*: Tan’s collation reads *Shushí* (millet-eating).
2. *The one they wish to pursue is commanded to go north to the Field Ancestor*: Tan’s collation reads *The one they wish to pursue is commanded: “Let the spirit go north.”*
3. *There are heirs, no heirs, no people □ Ren clan*: Tan’s collation reads *There are heirs, no people; the heirless have no people of the Ren clan.*
4. *Observing the Miao people*: Tan’s collation reads *Meat-eating.*
5. *Qingniu* (Green Ox): Tan’s collation emends to *Men qingye* (Gate of Green Leaves).

---

**Guo Pu’s Commentary on the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*
*Scroll Eighteen: The *Classic of the Regions Within the Seas***

To the east of the Eastern Sea and the corner of the Northern Sea lies a state called **Chosŏn** and **Tiandu**. Its people dwell in water. Chosŏn is today’s Lelang Commandery.

The kingdom of **Tianzhu** (India) values the **Dao** and **virtue**; it possesses written texts, gold, silver, coinage, and *stūpas* that emerge from this land. In the fourth year of the *Taixing* era of the Jin, the king of Tianzhu came to pay tribute. The **Wei people** love others. *Wei* also means "love," pronounced *wēi*.

Within the Western Sea and amidst the Flowing Sands lies a state called **□shi**, pronounced *chì*. West of the Western Sea and the Flowing Sands is a state called **Fan Ye**, meaning "overflowing."

West of the Flowing Sands stands **Bird Mountain**, from which three rivers flow. The three rivers issue from a single mountain. There is **yellow gold**, **xuan gui** (a precious stone), **cinnabar goods**, and **silver**, all emerging from these waters—speaking of the treasures within these streams. There is also **Huai Mountain**, from which **Good Water** flows.

East of the Flowing Sands and west of the Black Water lie the states of **Zhaoyun** and **Sizhi**.

The Yellow Emperor’s wife, **Leizu**, bore **Changyi**. The *Genealogies* states: “The Yellow Emperor took a daughter of the Xiling clan as wife, calling her Leizu, who bore **Qingyang** and **Changyi**.” Changyi descended to dwell by the **Ruoshui River** and begot **Hanliu**. The *Bamboo Annals* says: “Changyi descended to dwell by the Ruoshui; the *Qiandi* (Heavenly Emperor) was **Dihuang**, and **Huang** was **Hanliu**. Hanliu was **Emperor Zhuanxu**.” **Han** had a protruding head and cautious ears—*zhuóshǒu* (protruding head) and *jǐněr* (cautious ears) are unclear. His face was that of a man, his snout that of a boar, his body that of a *lin* (a mythical beast), and his hooves those of a *qutun* (a large pig). *Zhǐ* means "feet"; *qú* means "cart-net," speaking of the tracks of his feet. The *Great Commentary* says: “As large as a *chequ* (a giant clam).” He took **Naozi**, called the **River Woman**, and begot **Zhuan**. The *Genealogies* says: “Zhuanxu’s mother was a daughter of the Zhuoshan clan, named **Changtiao**.”

Between the Flowing Sands and the Black Water stands a mountain called **Mountain of No Death**, which is **Fuqiu**. East of **Huashan** and the **Qingshui River** is a mountain called **Zhaoshan**. There is a man there, **Bogao**. *Bogao* is an immortal. Bogao ascends and descends here, reaching the heavens—speaking of his soaring through the clouds to this mountain.

In the southwest sea, between the Black Water, there is a mountain called **Mountain of No Death**, which is also **Fuwu**. East of **Huashan** and the **Qingshui River** is a mountain called **Zhaoshan**. There is a man there, **Bogao**. *Again mentioning Bogao.* Bogao ascends and descends here, reaching the heavens. *The preceding forty characters appear to be repeated.*

Between the southwest sea and the Black Water lies the **Duguang Wilds**, where **Houji** is buried. Its city is three hundred *li* square, said to be the center of heaven and earth, where the **Plain Girl** emerges. The *Li Sao* says: *“Crossing the Duguang Wilds, he cried out to the straight cypress.”* There are **gaoshu** (fat beans), **gaodao** (fat rice), **gaoshu** (fat millet), and **gaoji** (fat sorghum)—speaking of fine grains smooth as fat. The *Outer Commentary* says: “The son of **Gaoliang** is **beans**.” The hundred grains grow of themselves; in winter, they are sown and played like a *qin*. *Bo* means "to sow" or "to plant," a local expression. The **luan birds** sing of themselves, the **phoenixes** dance of themselves. The **ling shou** bears fruit—*ling shou* is a tree resembling bamboo with branches and nodes. The grasses and trees gather here, assembling in this place. Here dwell the hundred beasts, gathering in herds. *Yuan* means "here," where they herd together. This grass neither dies in winter nor summer.

Between the Black Water and the Qingshui River within the Southern Sea stands a tree called the **Ruomu**, with red bark and green leaves. *Ruoshui River.* There is the state of **Yuzhong** and the state of **Liexiang**.

On **Ling Mountain**, there is a red serpent dwelling upon its trees, called the *ruan* serpent, which feeds on wood. It does not consume birds or beasts—its name sounds like "soft and weak." There is the state of **Jianchang**. There are people there with bird heads, called the **Bird Clan**. In present-day texts on immortals, they are known as the **Crow Barbarians**.

There are nine mounds encircled by water, named: **Taotang’s Mound**—*Taotang* being Yao’s title; **Shude’s Mound**; **Mengying’s Mound**; **Kunwu’s Mound**—this mound abounds in metal, as the *Master Yin* says: "the metal of Kunwu"; **Black-and-White Mound**; **Redview Mound**; **Cansui’s Mound**; **Warrior’s Mound**—these are names of fine mountains; and **Divine Ascent Mound**, said to be where divine beings dwell.

There is a tree with green leaves, a purple stem, dark flowers, and yellow fruit, called the **Jian Tree**. It stands a hundred *ren* tall without branches, its crown bearing nine crooked boughs—*zhu* means "bent," sounding like *jin*. Below, its roots twist into nine knots—*ju* means "tangled roots," as the *Huainanzi* says: "When a tree is large, its roots coil." Its fruit resembles hempseed, and its leaves are like those of the *mang* tree—*mang* wood resembles the wild pear. The Great Radiance once passed by here, meaning that Fuxi traversed beneath it. It was the work of the Yellow Emperor, who established governance through yielding.

There is the **Yayu**, a creature with a dragon’s head that devours human heads. It dwells in the midst of the **Weak Waters**.

There is a green beast with a human face, called the **Xingxing**. It can speak.

To the southwest lies the state of **Ba**. The present Three Ba regions are its remnants. **Taihao** begot **Xian**, Xian begot **Chengli**, Chengli begot **Houzhao**, and Houzhao was the first of the Ba people, their primal ancestor.

There is a state named **Liuhuang Xin-shi**, also known as **Feng-shi**. Its walled city spans three hundred *li* square, and dust rises from it—an image of its flourishing might.

There is **Basui Mountain**, from which the **Mian River** flows.

There is also the state of **Zhujuan**. It has black snakes with green heads that consume elephants—these are the **Ba Snakes**.

In the south dwell the **Gan Giants**, also called the **Xiaoyang** (pronounced *gan*). They have human faces, long arms, black hairy bodies, and feet that reach their heels. When they see a person, they laugh, and their lips cover their faces, allowing them to escape.

There are also black-skinned people with tiger bodies, bird feet, and two hands that grasp snakes, devouring them.

There is the **Ying** (pronounced *ying*), a creature with bird feet.

There is the **Fengshi**, a giant boar. **Yi** shot and killed it.

There is a people called the **Miao**. They are the descendants of the **Three Miao**.

There is a divine being with a human head and a serpent’s body, resembling an axle or the hub of a great chariot—a marsh spirit. It has heads on both sides, branching into two. Clad in purple robes and a bannered crown, it is called **Yanwei** or **Weiyi**. When a ruler encounters and offers it sacrifice, he will dominate the world. **Duke Huan of Qi** once came upon it while hunting in the marshes and thereafter became hegemon over the feudal lords. It was also seen by **Zhuang Zhou**, who fashioned a vermilion crown.

There is the **Luan**, which sings and dances of its own accord.

There is the **Feng Bird**, whose head bears the pattern "Obedience," whose breast bears "Benevolence," and whose back bears "Righteousness"—its appearance heralds peace under heaven.

There is a green creature like a hare, called the **Junzhuo**.

There is the **Cui Bird**.

There is the **Peacock**.

Within the **Southern Sea** stands **Mount Heng**, the Southern Marchmount. It bears **fungus cinnamon**, or some say **Mount Heng** has fungus cinnamon resembling bamboo, as recorded in the *Bencao*.

There is a mountain called **Three Son-of-Heaven’s Barrier**.

In the southern abyss of **Cangwu**, within it lies **Mount Jiuyi**, where **Shun** was buried, on the border of **Changsha** and **Lingling**. Today, it stands south of **Yingdao County** in **Lingling**. The mountain has nine streams, all resembling the original **Nine Doubts**. In ancient times, the entire region was collectively called **Cang**.

Within the **Northern Sea**, there is **Snake Mountain**, from which the **Snake River** flows, emptying eastward into the sea.

There is a five-colored bird whose flight darkens an entire village. In the seventh year of **Emperor Xuan of Han’s** reign (73 BCE), tens of thousands of five-colored birds flew over the subordinate counties—these were the same birds, called **Yi Birds**, a kind of phoenix. The *Li Sao* says: "I harness jade serpents and ride the Yi."

There is **Buju Mountain**, where **Chui the Artisan** is buried to the west. Chui was the master craftsman of **Yao** (pronounced *chui*).

Within the Northern Sea, there are prisoners bound in reverse with shackles, bearing constant halberds and doubled axes—these are the assistants called **Xianggu’s Corpse**, akin to **Erfu** and **Minister Wei**.

**Boyifu** begot **Xixianlong**, and Xixianlong was the first to beget the **Hu Qiang**. The Hu Qiang bear the surname **Qi**. Boyi was the teacher of **Zhuanxu**, and the present Hu Qiang are their descendants.

Within the Northern Sea stands **Mount Youdu**, from which the **Black River** flows. Upon it dwell dark birds, dark serpents, dark leopards, and dark tigers—black tigers are called **Tiao**. There are also dark foxes with bushy tails. *Peng* means "thicket." The *Shuo Yuan* says: "The thicket fox and the patterned leopard bear patterned pelts."

There is **Great Mystery Mountain**.

There are the **People of the Dark Mounds**, for all things on these mounds are black.

There is the **State of Great Darkness**, also called the **Dark People**, who dwell in caves and wear no clothes.

There are the **Red-Shank People**, whose legs below the knees are crimson.

There is the **Dingling State**, whose people have hair from the knees downward, horse hooves, and excel at running. The *Shi Han Shen Wu* says: "The horse-hoofed whip their own feet and travel three hundred *li* in a day."

**Yanling**, grandson of the **Flame Emperor**, had relations with **A Nü Yuanfu**, wife of **Wu Quan**—*tong* means "to unite," implying they came together. Wu Quan was the name of a man. Yuanfu played the **zhong**, as the *Shi Ben* says: "Wu Gou made the *qing*, and Chui made the *zhong*." He created the **Le Feng**, composing the music’s melodies and establishing the mountain’s form.

**The Yellow Emperor** begot **Luo Ming**, Luo Ming begot **White Horse**, and White Horse was **Gun**, father of **Yu**. The *Shi Ben* says: "The Yellow Emperor begot Changyi, Changyi begot Zhuanxu, and Zhuanxu begot Gun."

**Emperor Jun** begot **Yu Hao**, Yu Hao begot **Yin Liang**, Yin Liang begot **Fan Yu**, who was the inventor of boats. The *Shi Ben* says: "Gong Gu and Huo Xia made boats." Fan Yu begot **Xi Zhong**, Xi Zhong begot **Ji Guang**, and Ji Guang was the first to make wooden carts. The *Shi Ben* says: "Xi Zhong made carts." This text says Ji Guang, showing that father and son jointly created the design, hence their interchangeable mention.

**Shaohao** begot **Ban** (pronounced *ban*), who was the first to make bows and arrows. The *Shi Ben* says: "Mou Yi made arrows, and Ze made bows." Since arrows are one thing, and yet both are credited with their creation, there is doubt in the account. This text says Ban made them.

**Emperor Jun** bestowed upon **Yi** a vermilion bow and white-feathered arrows—a vermilion bow and stone-tipped arrows fletched with white feathers. The *Wai Zhuan* says: "The white-feathered arrows, when seen from afar, resemble a field of *tu* (pronounced *tu*)." He did so to aid the states below, meaning Yi used archery to eliminate calamities and assist the subordinate states. Yi was the first to relieve the myriad hardships of the lower lands, such as slaying the **Chisel-Teeth** and the **Fengshi**.

**Yi of Youqiong** usurped and shot, hence his name. **Emperor Jun** begot **Yanlong**, and Yanlong was the first to make the **qin** and **se**. The *Shi Ben* says: "Fuxi made the qin, and Shennong made the se."

Emperor Jun had eight sons, who were the first to create song and dance.

Emperor Jun begot **Shen**, Shen begot **Yijun**, and Yijun was the first to become **Chui the Artisan**, thus beginning the myriad crafts of the common people.

Hou Ji was the first to sow the hundred grains. Hou Ji’s grandson was named Shu Jun, who first instituted ox-plowing.

Great was this **Red Yin**—or, as some texts write, the sound *yin*. He was the first to establish a state, receiving enfeoffment as its lord.

Yu and Gun were the first to spread earth, leveling and stabilizing the Nine Provinces. *Spread* here means *to distribute*. The *Book* says: "Yu distributed earth and stabilized the lofty mountains and great rivers."

The wife of the Flame Emperor, a daughter of the Red Waters named **Tingxian**, gave birth to **Yanju**. Yanju begot **Jiebing**, Jiebing begot **Xiqu**, and Xiqu begot **Zhurong**. Zhurong was the Fire Minister of the Gaoxin clan.

Zhurong descended to dwell by the Jiang River and begot **Gonggong**. Gonggong begot **Shuqi**, whose head was square—his cranium and nape were flat—and who restored the soil to its place by the Jiang River, reclaiming the land where Zhurong had once dwelled.

Gonggong begot **Houtu**, and Houtu begot **Ye** (pronounced *yan*). Ye’s son was **Ming**, and Ming begot the Twelve Years, twelve children named after the years.

When the floodwaters surged to the heavens, Gun stole the **Breathing Earth** from the Supreme Emperor to dam the deluge. *Breathing Earth* refers to soil that grows without limit, thus capable of stemming the flood. As the *Divination of Qi* states: "The surging floodwaters knew no bounds." Gun then used the Breathing Earth to fill and block the flood.

During the reign of Emperor Yuan of Han, the earth in Linhuai and Xu County heaved, rising six *li* in length and one *zhang* in height—an instance of such Breathing Earth. Gun acted without the Emperor’s command, so the Emperor ordered Zhurong to execute him at **Yu Jiao**.

At **Yu Jiao**, the outskirts of Yu Jiao Mountain. Gun later begot Yu. As the *Divination of Qi* states: "Gun lay uncorrupted for three years after death; when split open with a Wu blade, he transformed into a yellow dragon." The Emperor then commanded Yu to complete the work of **earth-spreading**, thereby stabilizing the Nine Provinces. Gun’s achievements remained unfinished, so the Emperor again tasked Yu to fulfill his work.

*Mountains and Seas Classic*, Scroll Eighteen ends here.
